“THE BULLETIN” BOOK EXCHANGE.
On receipt at The Bulletin Office, Sydney, of Postal Order, Postal Notes, or Stamps (of any province) equal to the price stated
(which is published price with postage added), books in the subjoined list will be forwarded to any Australasian address.
A BELATED BOOK.
A memory of hastily-scanned chapters in Har-
per’s fathered the suggestion in this column, a
week or two ago, that “ Trilby ” might be “dull.”
That is the last thing which should be said of it.
Author and reader enjoy a camaraderie which
puts tedium out of the question. Certainly the
fascination fades in the last half, where the
machine-gods perspire at their labor, and pathos
and humor grow middle-aged along with the char-
acters ; but the book can be read to the end with-
out much trouble —and the first half is delicious.
English has no other picture of Paris Bohemian-
ism to equal this, though Thackeray has caught
the atmosphere here and there. It is not a pic-
ture to hang on the line—not at all a formal story
—just a vivid, natural sketch which glows and
breathes and charms you. So far as there is a
plot, the personages make it as they go along, and
the interest clusters always around them, not
round their deeds. The air of the Latin Quarter
may have changed in the gone half-century ; but
it does not matter. Du Maurier’s tints are real;
if the so-called reality cannot match those brilliant
reds and blues, so much the worse for the grey and
drab reality.
Whether “Trilby” deserves all her vogue is
another matter; probably she doesn’t; certainly
her vogue will pass, if it has not passed already.
Her poetic feet cannot make her a classic, even a
pseudo-classic. Her voice is better —the descrip-
tion of her wonderful powers fills some of the most
effective pages in the book; but “Ouida” is at
least as good when lavishing similes on the voice
of Oorreze. It is the studio scenes and chat which
one is thankful for—with Trilby only a figure, not
the central figure, and the priggish Little Billee
surrounded by so many foils that he has not time
to be heavy and objectionable.
Du Maurier’s knowledge is well seconded by his
styles—it might be truer to reverse the statement.
His should be the triolet’s motto—“sans peser, sans
rester ” —with such an air of incorrigible butterfly
does he flutter from paragraph to paragraph ! He
is spirited, and unaffected, and breezy, and easy,
and handles his pen as freely as his pencil—more
freely, perhaps.
A word to readers. “ Trilby ” isn’t good, solid,
fictional roast beef; it isn’t exciting; it wants a just-
a-little-cultured palate to appreciate it fully. If
you can’t make a meal off cheese and salad
and a bottle of light wine—the salad oiled
with a liberal sprinkling of French phrases—
better stick to people with reliable plots. Du
Maurier does, indeed, kill off four or his chief
figures at the close; but the killing is rather
clumsy, and the blood doesn’t make an attractive
mess on the carpet.
Australians get “Trilby” in a very good illus-
trated edition —paper, 35.; cloth, 4s.
NOTES.
The Bulletin Magazine is now well under
weigh, and the first number should make the port
of publication in a few weeks. It will contain
about 48 pages of prose, verse, and illustrations,
chosen from the best in The Bulletin repertory,
with possibly a new feature or two. The size will
be somewhat reduced from that of The Bulletin.
“ The Banjo’s ” book of verse will probably see
light about the end of this month; and Henry
Lawson’s verse should follow it before the end of
next. His prose tales and sketches will come in
another volume a little later. Daley’s book is
still in the clouds.
Ethel Turner’s new book will reach Australia in
another month or so. * * Seven Little Australians ”
has sold well in this country ; but, so far, hardly as
well as was anticipated in England or America.
However, there is plenty of time; for juvenile
books are almost independent of “seasons.” It
is interesting to note that there is nearly as much
difference of critical opinion regarding the death
of Judy as touching that of “ little Nell.” Coul-
son Kemahan, Ward, Lock and Bowden’s
“ reader,” whose “ Dead Man’s Diary ” and other
works are sufficient proof of his literary capacity,
is affected almost to tears when the big tree
falls. Others say they feel the jar of false senti-
ment and bad art. When critics disagree, it is
lucky that the author has decided beforehand.
Wilkie Collins’ “ Moonstone,” which rivals
“ The Woman in White ” in power and interest,
will also be obtainable shortly in a cheap edition.
Olive Schreiner (Mrs. Cronwrlght) recently gave
birth to a girl, which only lived a few hours. How
jioor woman pays for genius !
All the wholesale booksellers declare that
nothing goes better in Australia than Mrs. Henry
Wood’s novels—especially “ East Lynne,” which
.-ells every day. This is the more surprising, as
these books are only issued at 6s. —a price gener-
al'y too high for good Australian trade. But,
when you go into middle-class homes, you will
almost'certainly find “East Lynne,” or “The
Channings,” or “Mrs. Haliburton’s Troubles,”
among the half-dozen volumes which the mistress
of the house specialises as her own. Mrs. Wood,
in fact, is the favorite woman’s-novelist. She is
moral, yet not too moral, but just moral enough ;
and she spices her sanctity with a substantial thrill,
hither authors, like Miss Braddon, can give the
dear ladies the sex-stimulant which they crave ;
others, like Edna Lyall, can give them the con-
.scieuce-sedative which they need ; but none has
ever so well as Mrs. Wood combined stimulant
and sedative, naughty “nip” and hypocritical
peppermint, in a single delicious draught.
Grant Allan has dramatised “The Woman Who
Did.”
Mrs. Humphry Ward’s new tale, “The Story of
Bessie Costrell,” will be to hand this month.
The original of Kipling’s “Mulvaney” is said
to be a certain William' M‘Manus, now of Ban
Francisco, ami formerly in the British Army.
M ' Manus remembers K ipling well, and describee
him as “a plucky, inquisitive little fellow with a
short, stubby black moustache and specs., who
used to pass the bottle round freely among the
m-ivates. and pump out of .us all the yarns of the
Larrack-room. ’
Miss Dunne, “George Egerton,” of “Keynotes”
tune, is now alleged to have been born in Mel-
bourne. With two editions of 50b0 each, her first
and best book *an be counted as successful com-
mercially as it is in a literary sense.
Weyman’s new story, “Memoirsof a Minister,’
v il ] Km iooii<v) 4r» RiPiifcfltinWvT. ”
PUBLICATIONS.
“ The Australian Slang Dictionary ” (paper,
Is. 3d.), by Cornelius Crowe, is an amazingly
ignorant production. The author has mixed an
olla-podrida of linguistic scraps picked up every-
where—most of which are either not Australian or
not slang—and enriched it with little bits of his
own. The spelling is atrocious, and the defini-
tions are worse— e.g., “Pyjands, a kind of loose
drawers;” “Axe to grind, disseminating (!) for
personal ends ; ” and so on. The collection, how-
ever, is not without interest —one likes to know
that “ japanned by the Salvos ” means “ converted
by the Salvation Army ; ” it has a good deal of
unconscious humor —■“ bubble company ”is defined
as “ land and mining syndicates; ” and it will
doubtless find the readers it deserves.
Three new books in the handsome “ Keynotes ”
series (cloth, 4s. 6d. each), are “ Women’s
Tragedies,” by H. D. Lowry; “Monochromes,”
by Ella D’Arcy; and “Grey Roses,” by Henry
Harland. All three are worth buying, though
possession of literary tastes improves their flavor:
the two latter are largely compiled from “The
Yellow Book.” Mr. Lowry has, perhaps, the
greatest gift of dramatic presentment; though
Ella D’Arcy runs him close. This new writer
does characteristic woman’s work, exhibiting a
fine emotional cleverness, but her limitations are
obvious and never overpassed. Henry Harland’s
characteristics are an easy, graceful style, cosmo-
politan fluency, amid a knack of interesting us in
sentimental pictures of modern life seen from a
thoroughly modern point of view.
Morier’s “Adventures of Hajji Baba” is re-
issued by Macmillan in a very nice cloth edition,
well illustrated (45.). This hook, though thrown
into narrative form, is really one of the classics of
travel, and gives a quite unique picture of Persian
life and character. Another good old book in the
same seines is “Tom Cringle’s Log” (45.), a
splendid sea romance whose tang and gusto Time
has vainly tried to stale.
One of the best and most readable books about
China is Chester Holcombe’s recently-published
“ The Real Chinaman ’’(cloth, Bs.), adorned by many
good photo-illustrations. The author, an Ameri-
can, was for many years secretary to the U.S.
Legation at Peking, and reads the riddle of the
Mongolian sphinx by the light of a knowledge
which' few other Westerners have attained to.
The book is unusually well worth its price.
Vols. 3 and 4 of the re-issue of the “Author’s
Favorite ” edition of the Waverley novels—“ Guy
Mannering ” —are now to hand (cloth, 2s. 6d.).
The handy size and neat typography of this edition
(which contains all the original engravings) have
already been commended.
“ Studies of Men ” (cloth, 10s. 6d.), by G. W.
Smalley, is almost an ideal illustration of gossipy,
personal journalism, and most pleasant reading.
The subjects are many and various.
Anthony Hope’s new novel, “A Change of
Air” (cloth, Gs. 6d.), reaches his latter level, and
is readable enough.
George Moore’s “Celibates” (cloth, 6s. 6d.), is a
series of character studies in which the author’s
toucli, always a little heavy, crushes out most of
the interest of his subjects in the effort to make
them true.
“The Ameer Abdur Rahman” (cloth, 45.),
by S. E. Wheeler, can be cordially recommended
as an up-to-date and graphic sketch of Afghan
men and affairs.
In his monograph on “Louis XIV.” (cloth, ss.
6d.) Arthur Hassall gives a very good and readable
account of the French monarchy at the zenith of
its power.
“The Ruined Cities of Mashonaland ” (cloth,
45.), by J. T. Bent, is a new and interesting book
of African travel.
Webster’s “ Book of Beekeeping ” (Is. 3d.) can
be recommended to amateurs and beginners.
Baddeley’s “Lawn Tennis” (Is. 3d.) is a good
little manual, with records and wrinkles up to ’94.
83T NOTE.— As most of the books men-
tioned are in strong demand, some may
be temporarily out of stock. In such
case, orders will be filed, and the books
forwarded immediately on arrival of next
shipment.
Any book not in this list will be for-
warded on receipt of price, particulars
of which will he given to enquirers.

QURSE L V E SI
We wish it to be understood that the character of the
business we do, and the absolutely inclusive variety of the
goods we sell, entitle us to be called
UNIVERSAL PROVIDERS,
We are also not far out, considering the low prices we
ask for everything we sell, and the consequent blessings
we are continually showering upon the people, when we
assert, not in timorous tones, but with the trumpet voice
of confidence in the truth of what we say, when we dub
ourselves
PUBLIC BENEFACTORS.
There are good men in every sphere of life —honest
citizens in every community, whose deeds stamp them
for the approval of their fellow-men, and single them out
from the common herd. Thus we find among the crowd
who aspire to serve their country on the benches of the
Legislative Assembly a patriot here and there who stands
out in bold relief against the majority, whose sole aim
is place and pay. And in commercial life the same thing
occurs, and the same strong contrast between the few
disinterested friends of the people, and the many selfish
lookers after the big profits they would like to make, is
plainly apparent. To the former class, to the little band
of merchants who study their customers’ interests, and
who, in self-abnegation and contentedness, are willing to
accept small profits in reward for their services, belong
ANTHONY HORDERN’S.
They say that a good action is its own reward, but if the
good action entail hard work, much tribulation, and con-
tinual strife, then surely the laborer is worthy of his hire,
and the doer of good actions entitled to some recompense
at the hands of those who profit by what he does.
ANTHONY HORDERN’S
never cease from their toil; in the burden and heat of the
noonday sun they work, oppressed by the cruel frost of
somewhile baffled efforts and frustrated negotiations, they
continue, knowing always that the approval of millions of
souls is theirs. For three-quarters of a century it has
been so, and for a longer time than that it will remain as
it has been. Generations passed away have bought from
the great house, and generations yet to come will do the
same. Like the practice of honesty or truth, there is a
policy in it which brings to its adherents
PROFIT AND SATISFACTION.
ANTHONY HORDERN & SONS,
UNIVERSAL PItOVIOEUS.

A GOLDEN SHANTY,
ELLEAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Your face is with me yet—
I loved you so—
Sweet, tender, sad regret
Of long ago !
Time cannot dim your eyes.
The old-time grace—
Each look in loving-wise—
I still can trace ;
Still see the sweet, faint flush
That once I saw.
When Love first made you blush
In days of yore;
When ’neath the scented vine
You stole unseen.
And kissed me, and were mine.
My lost Ellean!
There, murmuring each to each,
’Neath listening boughs,
In passion-shaken speech
We made our vows;
And though the years’ grey ghosts
Throng thick between,
They cannot steal from me
That night, Ellean 1
I wonder if the breeze
That kissed your hair.
Beneath the arbor trees,
Still seeks you there ;
Seeks through the roses round
To find a red
To match the lips caressed
In nights long dead.
It never shall be found
And ne’er has been
Since the last night of all—
Of all—Ellean !
• • • • •
Ah ! I would fain forget.
If that might be,
The sorrow and regret
In thoughts of thee ;
For when the lips of one
Who loves me now
Press as I felt yours press
So long ago,
I see two great sad eyes
Rise up between,
And know I loved but once—
Loved you, Ellean !
And when at night I lie
Awake—alone—
I hear the tremulous sigh,
The tender tone,
Whose music sways my soul
As some strong wind
Sways whispering leaves ; but when
They bring to mind
“The saddest thought of all,
What might have been,”
I wish that 1 were dead
Like you, Ellean !
Sydney.
BURON GRAHAM.
“I lately saw a queer prospecting party
start from a Westraliau town (writes a Bulletin
correspondent). There were four—father and son
(Dutchmen), a mouthy English youngster about
19, and a sailor who was to be a sort of man
Friday. None of them had ever been in the bush
before, so they equipped themselves for all
emergencies. They have 10 eases of tinned soup,
fish, meat, and vegetables ; 10 guns and rifles, 10
revolvers, C saddles, 3 cane couches, 3 stone water-
bottles, and various items too numerous to men-
tion, including 7 or 8 bowie-knives. The swag
weighs about two tons, and there are three pack-
horses to carry it; three elephants couldn’t get it
all aboard, much less hump it. They are all
dressed just as they left ’Ome; even the sailor
wore a blue pilot jacket, wide trousers, and
oheesecutter cap ; each one had a revolver strapped
to his waist day and night in the township, and
they keep a sharp look out over the sandhills for
hostile blacks. It is said they had several severe
chills when they first came, caused by the sudden
appearance of the Japs, who run the laundry
here, hanging out clothes. However, confidence
was restored when one Jap. shook out what they
took to be a flag of truce. It was only one of the
local dude’s white shirts at the wash, but they
didn’t know any better. The amusing part of
the matter is, they are going out to Cossack to
select a reef, then to London to float it into
a co. in three months from date. They have not
the slightest doubt about their ability to do it, at
least the Dutch professor—who is the leader—has
not. They don’t want to kill any more blacks
than they can help, so Herr Professor has pro-
vided a large quantity of detonators which will
explode on striking the ground. These they will
throw at the blacks as they approach— to frighten
them. Should the black breddern disregard the
warning, then their blood will be on their own
heads— i.e., if the prospectors can find their guns
in time and shoot straight. The serene asinine
oocksureness of the party, even to the finding of
the reef, and the price they will ask, is simply
marvellous.”

SATURDAY, AUGUST 17, 1895.
The Eu-Chen Massacre.
The periodical tale of missionary massacres
arrives from China. In that country the
missionary-slaughter season comes much as
the drought year does in Australia ; it has
no fixed date ; there may be a long or short
interval between the times of its recurrence,
and its severity and duration and the area
affected are all uncertain—but sooner or later
its coming may be relied upon. Just now
China is more disorganised than usual, and
a faction known as the “ Vegetarians” has
been raging more bitterly than it generally
does, and the latest missionary slaughter—
so far as the daily-paper accounts are to be
lelied upon —has been even more gruesome
than it is at ordinary times. Many of the
victims are women —some of them Australian
women, for the fanatics of this country have
lately been seized with a craze for the con-
version of the Chinaman ; and, therefore, the
tale of outrage and horror is of much local
interest. So far, it is only the missionary
side of the story that has been heard, and
probably things will remain that way ; the
Chinese story, if there As such a thing, is
never listened to. A discordant howl for
vengeance has gone up in all -directions,
and in reply to the demands made for
blood and retaliation the Mongolian autho-
rities have promised the fullest satisfac-
tion. Satisfaction, in such cases, mostly
consists in the arrest of a number of
the poorest and least influential in-
habitants, whq are tortured, probably along
with their wives and children, until some
acknowledgment of their guilt .'fs'obtained,
and then executed by various shuddery pro-
cesses. The real criminals, as a rule, repre-
sent secret societies which the local man-
darins dare not provoke ; so when the repre-
sentatives of European alleged Christianity
demand justice—well-knowing what manner
of justice is to be expected—and leave the
details of it to the Chinese official intellect,
there is very little to choose between them
and the “Vegetarian” mob. The pious
element of this country is hereby invited to
seriously consider this aspect of the case.
* * *
The periodical missionary slaughter is a
subject which is generally regarded from one
side only. Yet it is a question which has
many sides and many aspects. The mis-
sionary is forced upon the Chinaman by
brute force and insolent aggression. Be-
cause the Mongol is much more ignorant
than the Western, therefore his religion is
much more real —to himself ; education
breeds religious indifference, whereas ignor-
ance and pious fervor go together. To him
therecomes an aggressive, black-coated person
to tell him that he is a fool and a mere heathen
—that his religion is blank superstition —that
his ancestors, whom he regards with a rever-
ence wholly incomprehensible to the Euro-
pean, are probably in a new and awful variety
of hell, and many other things. The .mis-
sionary, because he is backed up by foreign
ambassadors and foreign ironclads and
foreign bayonets, is a symbol of conquest.
If he is insulted or injured his country
demands “ satisfaction,” which means .
butchery and torture, practically on his own
unsupported version of the case ; therefore,
he is not only an insult to the Chinaman's
faith but an insult to his sense of justice and
his national feeling—if he has such things.
The Englishman, or Scotsman, or Irishman,
in the days when he believed sufficiently in
his own creed to take any serious interest in
it, would have probably been almost as mur-
derous, if somewhat less brutal, under simi-
lar provocation. The history of the British
isles is dotted over with stories of religious,
wars and disturbances and persecutions and
tortures, some of which compare not at all
unfavorably with the recent doings in China.
And it is a dead certainty that the English-
man, or Scotsman, or Irishman of to-day
would rise in revolt against foreign mission-
aries who were forced upon him in the same
fashion that he forces himself upon the
Mongolian. This is not intended to excuse
the Chinaman, for that unspeakable heathen,
in some of his aspects, is past all excuse ; it
is only meant to show that there is more
than one side to this case, as there is to
every other.
«
Australia, of recent years, has taken the
Chinese missionary craze badly. Many
enthusiastic young people, mostly female,
have gone to almost certain death in some
shape or other—probably a death of violence,
torture and outrage—in the interior of that
almost unknown land, and have been
whooped on in their fatuous career by stout
and comfortable pastors who have them-
selves carefully remained at home. The
Bulletin's opinion of these pastors would
be best expressed in tar and feathers ; the
conduct of the men who will cheerfully shed
the blood of some foolish and misguided girl
in a cause for which they will not adventure
their own, can be better described in that
way than in any other. And the cause itself
is so pitiably, miserably, unspeakably hope-
less. Europe and America and Australia
are about as little Christian as Asia. The
Gospel has been preached to the white races
for nineteen centuries, and almost the only
result is a contemptuous indifference. There
was some semblance of real belief in the
ignorant Middle Ages, and the Church was
then a great and unsavory reality ; now it is
little more than a shadow. If the white
races to-day believed in their own creed the
whole fabric of their civilisation would drop
to pieces, for it is all built on greed and
commercialism and strife and injustice and
miscellaneous iniquity. And since the Church,
after many centuries of opportunity, has
failed to convert a respectable fraction of its
own professed believers—since it has even
failed to convert itself—it has exceedingly
little hope of making an impression on the
stupendous dead weight of Asiatic heathen-
dom. It knows this fact well—it cannot but
know it—and therefore its action in sending
out helpless girls to be outraged and
slaughtered by the unspeakable leper to no
possible purpose, save that they may serve
as a gaudy advertisement for its own inter-
ests, is all the more reprehensible. The
man whose sister or daughter insists upon
going upon such an errand, if no possible
form of dissuasion will prevail, is morally
justified in shooting her to save her from the
far worse fate which, sooner or later, will
almost certainly befall her.
» * *
The whole scheme of missionary enter-
prise is based on that curious kink in human
nature which makes the most distant field
look the greenest, and the Chinese apostle,
and the Paraguayan emigrant, and the
Soudan Contingent, and the digger who
wanders to drought-stricken Coolgardie, are
all cranks of the same order. Asia and Africa
are dotted oyer with mission stations,
and thousands of pounds are spent annu-
ally in offering to the Moslem and the
Buddhist and the. Brahmin and the Con-
fucian those dogmas in which their own
professors are steadily losing faith—and the
really authentic converts could probably be
countedonthe fingers of one hand. The whole
propaganda is a dismal failure. And mean-
while the ugly, prosaic, dingy, unromantic
heathenism of Little Bourke-street and the
Sydney “ Rocks,”* and countless similar
places, not to speak of the equally hopeless
heathenism of Toorak and Darling Point,
passes unnoticed. In the desire to convert the
man afar off who has a religion already--and
perhaps almost as efficient n one as that
which is offered in its stead—very little
account is taken of the person near at hand
who has no real religion at all. The sensa-
tional adult fanatic who desires to go to a
strange, weird land of images and old tradi-
tions, and preach to a picturesque face with
pig tails, is, after all, only an etherealised
variety of the sensational small boy who
reads penny dreadfuls and wants to go
wildly forth and scalp the alleged Indian in
Arizona. The people who subscribe vast
sums for the conversion of the picturesque
person with the pig tail, and yet leave the
hungry, sordid, dingy ruffianism of their own
cities practically unnoticed, represent the
same order of mind at second-hand. The
English-speaking races send out more mis-
sionaries than all the rest of the world put
together, for the same reason that they
explore more strange countries, and kill
more Indians, and climb more tall moun-
tains, and shoot more bears, and go to more
prize-fights, and are more anxious to find
the North Pole than most other people. In
other words, they do it because they are a
roving, restless, unsatisfied race with the
love of adventure and loot in their souls, and
some of them take the Scripture along with
them by accident.
• *
This same spirit of. missionary enterprise
has been a curse to many lands, but to few,
of late years, more than to China. The
teacher who goes to the back blocks of that
feebly-governed land takes her life —for they
seem to be mostly females nowadays in her
hand, and frequently loses it. Then the
unspeakable mandarin, being ordered to do
justice to the murderers, with whom he is
generally in sympathy, indulges in wholesale
butchery among the more friendless of the
inhabitants, or among his personal enemies,
with all manner of miscellaneous horrors
thrown in. The converts are very scarce,
and the butchery is generally very plentiful.
Therefore, every Australian girl-fanatic who
wants to go on .the Chinese mission, would
do well to remember that she not only risks
her own life but .may i>o indirectly the cause
of torture and’ death .and unimaginable
horrors to dozens of innocent inhabitants
who will have cause to curse her memory,
and that the missionary in China is re-
membered chiefly by the blood of the wrong
person that is shed to avenge her death.
Presumably every individual lias a right to
suicide as he or she may think proper, but
the right to buy a gaudy martyrdom at the
expense of others, even indirectly, is more
than questionable.
# * *
The recent massacre will probably assist
rather than discourage the Chinese mis-
sionary movement. There is an element of
pathetic romance in the story which appeals
strongly to a certain class of mind. Pew
people could read without genuine sympathy
the tale told in the Australian daily press of
the two sisters from Melbourne who were
killed in that upheaval, and of their mother
who looked with tearless eyes through her
spectacles and refused to grieve over their
“glorious death” as martyrs —adding that
if she had more daughters she would gladly
give them to the Cause. And, meanwhile,
as likely as not, some yellow mother on the
river Min looks out as steadily through her
horn spectacles and refuses to weep for her
sous who were executed .for their share, or
alleged share, in the massacre, because they
also died in the good cause of religion. The
good cause of religion covers a multitude of
sins ; and the relatives of those who died in
trying to spread one set of incomprehensible
dogmas, and the relatives of those who were
executed for defending, in a crude and
primitive fashion, another set equally in-
comprehensible, probably feel the same up-
lifting of soul. Also, each crowd regards
the other as benighted heathens with the
same pitying self-complacency, and the
same stolid confidence, born of their
own ignorance, in the ignorance of the
other lot. All religions rest, more or
less, upon ignorance ; by the time one
gets to know anything worth speaking
of he has begun to realise how little he
really does know, and then he hasn’t
sufficient faith left in his own dogmas
to obtrude them upon anybody. There-
fore, there is about as much chance of the
Confucian converting the Christian in the
end as of being converted himself ; the
former, being the more ignorant of the two,
is at least a great deal more certain of his
Confucianism than the latter is of his Chris-
tianity. And there is very little chance of
any nation being converted from one creed
to another by modern methods ; most, if not
all, of the nations which have been con-
verted owe their conversion either partially
or entirely to brute force, and if there is
even one reliable instance to the con-
trary The Bulletin fails at the present
moment to recollect it. Therefore, the
Chinese side of the case —for even so bad
a case as this has a Chinese side which
deserves a fair hearing —may be summed up
in three sentences : (1) The sending of mis-
sionaries to the “ heathen ” is a supercilious
insult, inasmuch as no one really knows for
certain which creed is more heathen than
the other ; (2) the sending of missionaries
to teach a creed which its own professors
obviously don’t believe is an anomaly ; and
(3) the Chinaman is more or less human,
like the balance of humanity, and when a
superior foreign person, who tells him that
his dogmas are mere folly and superstition, is
thrust on him by military force he resents it
‘ —just as the Englishman would under the
same conditions. But, being a barbarous,
medi.eval sort of person, he resents it more
brutally than the Englishman would do.

So that the purely Liberal minority in the

The Parnellite section has won ground ; the

Conservative party is far the strongest in the

other parties combined. \ Just before the

leaders hoped for was 65 —some put it as low
as 31 ; the electors gave them 154.

Mathematicians claim that, given a complete
knowledge of the state of a particle ol matter-
at any instant of lime, you would know its
history from and to all eternity. Doubtless it
is the same in politics : a complete knowledge
of conditions would mean a complete know-
ledge of causes and effects, and make prophecy
inevitable and superfluous. Though the Liberals
may not be able to understand the necessity of
their defeat, it seems natural enough to both
sides. Three months ago “one of the ablest
Radicals (Laboucheke) acknowledged that
the Conservatives were likely to be in power
for fifteen years out of the next twenty. The
Unionists, he said, will probably be in power
from 1896 to 1902. And even if a Separatist
party is then returned, it will scarcely be able
to keep office more than two or three years
against the Opposition in the Commons and in
the Upper House ” (Saturday Review, May 4).
# # •
The fact is that when Gladstone espoused
Irish Home Rule lie laid his axe to the root of
Liberal-party prosperity. Not through the
rupture alone, though that was sufficiently
serious ; but because he saddled his followers
with what is, for the present, essentially a
losing cause. Nearly every impartial observer
admits the justice of Home Rule for Ireland ;
but a great many impartial observers doubt its
expediency for Britain —if conceded on anything
like the lines which the Irish desire. A
majority of English electors certainly share this
doubt. In spite of the advances of Liberalism
in the towns, the bulk of English sentiment
remains persistently Tory. To the instinc-
tive dislike of change is added a racial antipathy
to the Irish. Seventy-five years ago Sydney
Smith published a cultivated Englishman’s
opinion of the Irish. He said :
The Irishman has many good qualities : he is brave,
vsltty, generous, eloquent, hospitable, and open-hearted ;
but he is vain, ostentatious, extravagant, and fond of dis-
play-light in council—deficient in perseverance—without
skill in public or private economy —an enjoyer, not an
acquirer—one who despises the slow and patient virtues
who wants the superstructure without the foundation—the
result without the operation—the oak without the acorn
and the three hundred years of economy.
The lapse of time would not prevent many
cultured Englishmen of to-day from cordially-
assenting to this judgment; and the uncul-
tured distrust the Irishman for his bad
qualities, distrust him almost as much for the
good ones which they are characteristically in-
capable of comprehending.
» # •
Every year since ’B6 has made it clearer
that Gladstone shouted for Home Rule before
English electors were ready—and English
electors send to the House of Commons more
than two-thirds of its members. At the outset
it was confidently prophesied that the Unionist
Liberals must soon return to the Grand Old
Shepherd’s fold ; that their coalition with the
Tories could not last; that Home Rule was a
certainty in a year, in three years, in five.
Yet even while Gladstone’s influence remained,
it was clear that he had either very much
over-rated that influence, or very much under-
rated the obstacles opposed to it. Now that
Gladstone is gone—with all his faults, the
Achilles of the Liberal camp—the achieve-
ment of Home Rule seems indefinitely postponed.
The mere passage of a Home Rule Bill through
the Commons by a small majority, as in ’93,
will never be accepted by the Lords and the
Crown as sufficient reason for a constitutional
change so momentous. And the Crown and the
Lords must be reckoned/ with ; they are part of
the system of “things as they are;” and
though reason and enthusiasm may destroy
them every day in speech and argument,
they still exist. There is little effective
republican sentiment in England ; the cru-
sade against the Lords has fallen miser-
ably flat. Rosebery may not have been
diplomatic, but he was certainly correct
when he endorsed Salisbury’s statement that
“ before Irish Home Rule is conceded
by the Imperial Parliament, England, as the
predominant member of the partnership of the
three kingdoms, will have to be convinced of
its justice.” And the conversion of England
proceeds more slowly than in the days of
Colitmba. Possibly the issue may be what
seems to many Australians the most sensible
course : the concession of Home Rule to Eng-
land, Scotland, Ireland and Wales alike, and
the release of the “ weary Titan ” among Par-
liaments from the burden of all but matters of
Imperial concern.
*•■ ; *
The position of the Unionist Liberals in the
new House is interesting. The English genius
still runs to Government by Contradiction—as
is partly shown by the snuffing out of the
independent Labor-party at last election, and
the failure of the highly-moral “New Party”
to make any impression on the serried Liberal
and Tory ranks. Hence.the Gladstonian con-
clusion that it was only a question of time till
the dissentients should find a third-party career
impossible. But it was forgotten that there
was a choice. Instead of advancing with
Gladstone, the Unionist Liberals have re-
treated with Salisbury ; or, rather, they have
marked time until Salisbury came up. The
drift to the Tories has ended in fusion, and
quite probably the Balfour - Chamberlain
alliance will result in the permanent loss of the


Liberal- Unionists to the Liberal camp, and
their permanent gain by the Tories. After all,
new Liberal is but old Conservative writ broad ;
the Tories to-day (thanks partly to Randolph
Churchill) are further advanced on many
points than the Liberals of half-a-century ago;
and the Chamberlain strain will still further
improve the blood. So that the new coalition
Ministry is likely to be quite as democratic as
British electors wish.
# # *
The Parnellite gain of three seats, as con-
trasted with the Nationalist loss of two, has
significance, and it is a gain which may be ex-
pected to increase. The Irish are characteristic
extremists, and the results of the M'Cauthy
policy arrive too slowly to satisfy them long.
Ireland has not yet recovered from the loss of
Parnell. Apart from the weakness of the
“split,” there is the weakness from the trans-
ferred leadership. Justin M'Cakthy, with all
his capacity, is too much the amiable old gentle-
man to stand at the Irish party’s helm in stormy
political seas. Undoubtedly his policy of
moderation is the best policy possible. The
antics of a dynamite faction stir, not fear, but
resentment in English minds. On the other
hand, English minds are sluggish, and apt to
forget Irish wrongs altogether unless persis-
tently pricked to notice them. Perhaps
M‘Carthy has been too lax : already there are
symptoms of revolt from his mild rule. And if
Irish Home Rule is to be indefinitely deferred,
it is unlikely that the Irish calm will continue.
Genius and Gingerbread.
“ Letters, ” wrote Hobbes, “are the Message
-of the Ages, and I hold him no true bearer
thereof who knoweth not that in such sort
there can come to him no honor from the hand
of lesser man—-even though that man weareth
crown and calleth himself king!” “Litera-
ture,” says Hume, “ is the loftiest of all pur-
suits, and confers, in itself, a real rank upon its
followers which cannot be other than soiled and
diminished by the futile distinctions of an
adventitious one.” The straight, strong voices,
these, of men, who honored and were honored
by that Ars Maxima so pitifully bedraggled
nowadays by such small, apologetic snivellings
as marked the recent congratulatory Besant
function. The “Sir” conferred upon that
assiduous book-builder was intended, it was
alleged, to proclaim to a respectful universe
that “a man of literary genius might properly
be a man of rank.” “ Rank, sir ?” said Vol-
taire to Congreve, when that superlative
person affected to take the great Frenchman’s
visit as paid, not to the author, but to the fine
gentleman. “Have you tlje temerity to suppose
that, but for the man of letters, Voltaire
would have wasted a thought upon the mere
man of rank ? ”
* 1 # #
That was the case in a nutshell, and no self-
respecting scribbler, with this trumpet-note in
his ears, can fail to blush for the broken-backed
amenities of the Besant, banquet. Whether
Hall Caine meant that the title could now
“properly ” come down to the man, or the man
to the title, is neither clear nor of much conse-
quence, for, in the opinion of everybody who
doesn’t crawl upon literary all-fours, he is
wrong both ways. The title cannot condescend
to the man, any more than the man should
condescend to the title. And such a title !
The literary taker of a tin-pot “ knighthood ”
has not even the miserable satisfaction of find-
ing himself upon the social level of the multi-
tude of men whose genealogy has been for-
gotten in their genius. Smollett, Fielding,
ByroN and Shelley claimed aristocratic lineage,
yet recognised, nevertheless, their truest distinc-
tion in a pursuit the dignity of which is now
grotesquely supposed to be enhanceable by the
same titular label that is bestowed, as a
matter of form, upon the successful huckster,
who chances to be Mayor of Lickspittleton
when Victoria by the Grace happens along that
way.
* * «
It was a remark of Addison’s that things are
often considered inherently ridiculous merely
because they have been cleverly laughed at;
which is not more true than that, conversely,
the most intrinsically pitiful absurdity may be
gravely accepted because it has not hitherto
been laughed at at all. And, so hard is it to
make the middle-class Briton conceive of any
sort of title, in any sort of circumstances, as
other than a lovely and delightful thing, that—
even when, with Besant and Caine, and a host
of others, he takes successfully to literature—
he never realises that, as a partaker with the
Immortals in the dignity of letters, he cannot
hut be humiliated with a title aristocratically
endurable only when it is proudly borne aloft'
by a member of the profession which gallantly
cuts throats for glory—and wages !
", v # # *
Right away at the tail-end of that melancholy
catalogue, the Order of Precedence, “ literary
persons and others ” are faintly discernible-
lumped thus ignominiously together as being
quite too absurdly remote from the haughty
cognisance of the Boss-Jeames to be worth any
further discrimination. But the “ literary
person,” when he isn’t also a self-seeking snob,
will be the last to quarrel with the inference
that in this gilded register of flunkeydom he
has properly no place at all, and that—were
the procession, with a right-about-face, to
march from the throne, as Men, instead of
towards it, as lackeys—the Last would be First
and the First Last !
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Judge Windeyer's “Hard Luck.”
■'N'.S.W. Judge Windkyjsk lias “bard luck.”
On top of a judicial record stained with such
an outrage on justice as the Mount Rennie case,
with such an averted outrage on justice as the
Dean case, are now piled his Honor’s misfor-
tunes in connection with the Butler divorce-
case and the Sanomann perjury case. The
first lias to do with Thomas Butler, a Sydney
chemist .who wanted to get rid of his w.fe. He
hired Charles Davis, a private detective, and
Susannah Peake, a concocter of pills, to fur-
nish evidence to incriminate her. Failing to
get it by fair means, it was obtained by foul.
Davis got admittance to Butler’s house while
Mrs. Butler was alone there ; Butler burst
in, according to a pre arranged plot, and found
Davis under the table. Judge Windeyer heard
the case; summed up against Mrs. Butler;
she was found guilty of adultery ; and the
divorce was granted. In giving his decree,
the Judge said tiie adultery was clearly proved ;
Mrs. Butler’s story (that Davis and her hus-
band were in collusion) was full of discrepancies
and he entirely disbelieved it ; while he be-
lieved the petitioner, dismissed a counter-
charge of adultery against him, and.gave him the
custody of his children. Further, one Johnson,
Mrs. Butler’s brother, had given evidence
tending to exonerate her. Judge Windeyer
said he believed Johnson wasa perjurer; gaveas
partly justifying the belief the grotesque reason
that Johnson shuffled his feet in the witness-
box ; and committed him for trial accordingly.
In due course Johnson, who shuffled his feet,
was hauled up before another Judge, and got
three years’hard labor for perjury. There, so
far as Judge Windeyer was concerned, the
matter rested. Luckily, Mrs. Butler didn’t
rest. Broken in health, mourning for her dead
father, craving for her children, without money,
almost friendless, she struggled on until she
accumulated evidence on which Butler, Davis,
and Peake Were last week brought to trial for
conspiracy, found guilty on clear evidence, and
sentenced, Davis and Butler to seven years’,
and Peake to four years’ penal servitude;
while the jury also found that Mrs. Butler
was not' guilty of adultery. In fairness to
Judge Windeyer, it should be borne in mind
that lie had before him only part of the evidence
adduced at the last trial, and did not know
the vital fact that Davis, the detective,
and Williams, the missing co-respondent, were
the same person. It was just his “ hard luck ! ”
In the second case, an obscure person named
August >Sandmann, on June 11, swore before
Judge Windeyer that his landlord smashed
open his door with a hammer and chisel. Mrs.
Sandmann corroborated. Judge Windeyer
didn’t believe them; and they were both thrown
into gaol on a charge of perjury. Sandmann
was brought out for trial on July 29, and a jury
acquitted him at once—just like Judge Win-
de yen’s “hard luck!” Mrs. Sandmann
remains in gaol with an eight-months-old baby ;
her husband is too poor to bail her out ; and he
says he can’t ascertain when her trial is to take
place. Johnson, who shuffled his feet, is also
in gaol at. time of writing. Possibly he and
Mrs. Sandmann will, in the.course of the year,
be. let out to rejoin Sandmann and Mrs. Butler.
Then they may gather up the tangled skeins of
their .lives; and thank Fate that at least theya
have not, like other victims, been crushed out
of existence altogether ; and bemoan Judge
Windeyer’s “hard luck.”
Tote Charity.
Editor Bulletin,— Bluff parson Edgar (Melb.)
wanted to know, the other day, why all the
tote profits are not to be given to charities.
The question probes a fundamental principle.
Edgar’s 17 stone of admirable misdirected
enthusiasm is aghast at the penury of dole and
donation societies. This poor-sinner protests
against the whole mob that is palliating and
soothing the sense of wrong in suffering man.
The charity clause in the tote proposal is catch-
penny at the best—in its essence it is one with
all the schemes that keep breath in the bodies of
laborersuntilFat Man wants to hire their brawn.
Charity is rose-water, and we want disinfectant.
The rule of the old French courts was perfume
and no bath—civet disguised the dirty-human
exhalation, and the timps were satisfied. In
our times charity hushes up a multitude of
sins. Charity says with Compromise, in the
words of a Bulletin bard :—•
The limpid clarity of truth
I phosphoresce with lies.
Charity is among the many vicious propensities
that make Fat Men dangerous. Some brewer
who has committed adulteration all his life
gives .£lOOO to the benevolent-.asylum—what
does it do but spin out the miserable existence
left to the survivors of his adulterant practices ?
In the case of tote-charity—what wilt the pro-
ceeds do, as far as they go, but help to stave
off the day of reckoning with the laud-mono-
polist, the machinery-monopolist, and all the
hell-brood ? Be just, and you will not need to
be generous. In my opinion, gambling is a
luxury as indispensable to the present genera-
tion of men as linen collars. The man who
goes to excess in linen collars is an evil, but no
one proposes to put a tax of per cent, on
linen collars to keep down dudes. Let the
• tote be fought for on its merits—don’t let- it
rank with the wicked dramateur performances
that defame art in the name of charity. Men
ask for justice, and they get—charity.
The tired world sits hungry and cold
’Neath Charity's cuis ,
A thief that lias stolen ns gold
Returning the purse.
The other Bulletin bard who said this prac-
tically wrote finis to the discussion. So 1
answer the excellent Edgar by saying that
tote-charity is a mistake. Gambling is an
element of man’s enjoyment, and he should get
it unadulterated. I l ’.
xhe Bogan Bridge at Monkey— again.
A large number of remarkable items appear
among theN.S.W. Government’s loan expendi-
ture for the first half of 1895. Some of the
most striking are £74 for cranes, moorings, etc.,
at Byron Bay; £Bl9 for additions to Newington
/Asylum ; £3718 for alterations to Circular
Quay, Sydney ; £972 for wood-paving on certain
city streets; £(i lbs. lid. for removing an
obstruction in Clarence River ; buying sites for
Land Board offices, £770 ; lighthouse, £157 ;
alterations lo sundry gaols, £6019; bridge at
Bullock Island, £1075 ; dredging Richmond
River, £15,931; ditto Clarence River, £10,636 ;
ditto Cook’s River, £437 ; and draining Rook-
wood cemetery, £3182. Then come—surveying
and taking soundings of sundry rivers, £6212 ;
more alterations .at Circular Quay, £1513;
taking snags out of creeks tunning into Rich-
mond River, £562 ; removing obstructions from
Clarence River, £lO9l ; ditto Tweed River,
£241 ; more alterations to Circular Quay,
£l4ll ; more dredging of Tweed River,
£8574; altering and patching old dredge,
£1591; and thinning-out forests, £13,488. The
total expenditure of borrowed money for
the six months, apart from mere book
entries re the conversion of old loans, is
£1,024,000. And of this amount about
£OOO,OOO is for works which produce no reve-
nue, and which there is no possible excuse
for charging to posterity, seeing that the nation
will need to keep on building new bridges, or
re-building old ones, and dredging rivers, and
repairing old dredges, and thinning out State
forests, and making drains, and repairing or
altering, or rebuilding public offices as long as
the present variety of civilisation lasts. As
posterity will have to pay its own annual bill
for these works for ever and ever, it is the
meanest kind of dishonesty to charge it with
the bill of the present generation also. Previous
New South Wales Treasurers, who were mostly
a politically dishonest and unscrupulous crowd,
spent, on an average, £400,000 a year of loan
money on works which yielded no revenue
and which should have been charged to current
revenue, but G. H. Reid, the most utterly
unscrupulous of the crowd, “ blews ” the
borrowed funds for revenue purposes at
the rate of fully a million per annum,
and may fairly be described as times
worse than his predecessors. The country, as
a rule, only demands that the Treasurer should
have an alleged surplus at the end of the year ;
if one Treasurer meets the public liabilities
honestly and comes out with £300,000 shortage
his reputation promptly drops to zero, while
another, who pays £1,000,000 of his ordinary
ex.’s out of loans and then has £lOO,OOO of
bogus surplus, can live for years afterwards on
the financial reputation thus acquired. Premier
Reid, however, charges over £1,000,000 a year
of his current expenses to posterity, and has a
huge deficiency besides, and the fact that he
has contrived to make some kind of a financial
reputation out of these two circumstances,
either one of which would have wrecked any
other man, is a marvellous proof of the power
of bunkum and moral grease.
A Fire-Insurance Item.
For 34 years past there has been in force in
Queensland a wise law whereby no bequest
to any church is valid unless the will by
which it is effected is made in the presence
of three witnesses, and is executed and regis-
tered at least a month before testator’s decease.
The reason of this is that almost every man is
more or less weak-minded in his last moments,
and when a ghostly comforter with an itching
palm whispers horrible things about hell in his
ear he is sometimes willing to disinherit all his
relatives and make over his property in the
most reckless fashion by way of effecting a fire
insurance on his soul. The said law is not an
absoluteguaranteeagainst undue influence on the
part of the ghostly comforter, but it does a good
deal in that direction. Now, however, Attor-
ney-General Byrnes has a bill before Queens-
land Assembly for the repeal of this wise
enactment, so that the ghostly comforter may
have better opportunities than hitherto to grab
the inheritance of the deceased as he drifts into
the shuddery hereafter. Up to date, though
several Labor and Opposition members warmly
opposed the bill, and thereby made Attorney-
General Byrnes very angry, the public has not
manifested any serious interest in it; even the
local Worker passes it by with the casual
remark that it contains nothing of public in-
terest. The Bulletin has grave doubts whether
any bequest to any church should be valid, even
if it were made 40 years before the death of the
testator and signed in the presence of 3000
witnesses, let alone three. Death-bed libe-
rality is not a thing to be encouraged. If a
man doesn’t care sufficiently for the Church to
give his money to it while he is alive and well,
and thereby deny himself for its benefit, there
seems no sufficient reason why he should be
allowed to deny his |g,mily—-supposing he has
one—-for its benefit when he can’t hang on to
the money himself any longer. This paper has
a profound respect for the person who sacri-
fices his own comfort for a good cause, or even
a bad one which he believes to be good ; but the
person who enjoys his wealth to the last day of
his life, and then tries to be a public benefactor
at the expense of his family only, succeeds in
being a first-class hog.
Spasmodic Charity.
Several Victorian bodies have been moved by
the Eldorado disaster to suggest the establish-
ment of a permanent fund for the relief of
sufferers by fatal mine accidents. Something
of this kind is badly needed, and not in Victoria
alone. New South Wales is even worse off;
for there is some sort of fund in Victoria, and
there is none at all in the older province. At
present the widow and family of the miner who
is killed by himself in a commonplace way
stand very little chance of getting a sixpence
from mineowners or public. He may leave eight
or nine children in peculiarly bad circumstances;
but the public knows nothing of that —it
reads only the two-line notice that “John
Smith was yesterday killed by a fall of stone
in the Big Blow mine,” and thinks no more of
the insignificant item. But when half-a-dozen
men are killed in a sensational manner, the
public does not stop to inquire into the circum-
stances of the families, but hysterically rushes
off either to get itself on the committee or to
plank down its money. Nine men were killed
at Broken Hill the other day. They were
nearly all young men, several of them un-
married, and they left “only 25 children among
them ” ; and several of these were, compara-
tively speaking, well provided for. In a
few days, however, £l5OO had been col-
lected the mine-directors paid £5OO, the
Melbourne Stock Exchange, £lOO. At the
same time a woman with seven or eight young
children was living in destitution, her husband
having just before been killed at another of the
mines and only with the greatest difficulty
could a few pounds be scraped together Locally
for her and her children’s relief. During last
year nine.men were killed in. the Broken Hill
mines; but, except in one case, they had the
misfortune to be killed “by ones.” What became
of their families nobody seems to know, and
nobody seems to care. It is not complained
that the relatives of the sixes and the nines
who are killed get too much ; but it is com-
plained that the relatives of the ones, who
suffer just as keenly, should not get anything.
And it seems that nothing short of a permanent
fund, administered by reason and not controlled
by hysterics, will remove the cause of the com-
plain t.
Another Pipe Contract.
Premier Reid, the head and boss of the
N.S.W. Freetraders, has fallen away into Pro-
tection. Every Freetrader wants Protection
for his own industry, if he has one, and every
Freetrader has to go back, from utter shame,
on his own soulless, bowelless faith sooner or
later. A large number of the pipe-moulders of
Sydney are out of work just now, and, to
relieve them, Premier Reid has given a large
contract for pipes to two local firms. This is
certainly not consonant with any Freetrade
doctrine which he has ever preached. When
the deputation of workless pipe-moulders waited
upon him he should simply have said something
like this : “My friends, I am sorry for you,
but the doctrine of Freetrade says that we
must buy in the cheapest market, regardless of
the personal sufferings of individuals. Free-
trade is absolutely correct at all times,
and, even if you, and your wives, and your
infants, starve under its operations, yet it
remains correct all the same.” Then he should
have called for tenders, and if an English
or Belgian firm offered to do the work at a
fraction under the local price, lie should have
given them the business and let the local
workers and their families starve if need be.
Instead of which he did not even call for
tenders locally, but gave the order privately in
order to supply employment, regardless of pos-
sible cost, on the theory that it is better to
find work for the people of the country than to
get things a shade cheaper from a foreign
market. This is the doctrine of Protection
exactly. But why should the city workers,
who mostly voted for Freetrade, not get it,
while the country ones, who mostly voted
against it, are to have it in all its beauty ?
Premier Reid’s ten appointments to the N.S.
Wales Legislative Council are another proof
that the good politician and the bad politician
are very much alike when they get into office.
When he was an irresponsible fat man in Oppo-
sition, G. H. Reid complained bitterly, from a
lofty moral standpoint, of the iniquity of shov-
ing rejected and cast-off politicians into .the
Council, and thereby making a life-long legis-
lator out of a person whom the country didn’t
regard as. good enough to be trusted with the
same office for three years. Yet, of his own
nominees, Daniel O’Connor has been rejected
at three successive elections ; S. E. Lees has
just been been badly beaten at the Nepean
by a constituency which had tried him and
found him a woeful failure; Pulsford is a
defeated candidate who never even got into
the Assembly ; and Blanksby, the Newcastle
ex-preacher and building society manager, has
been rejected at two successive elections. In
all, seven out of the ten are politicians whom the
people have decisively voted down at the polls.
All ten are solid Foreign-traders, despite the fact
that Premier Reid was wont to complain
bitterly of the policy of making appointments
on purely party lines; and only two are sus-
pected of any serious democratic leanings.
Altogether, the only serious objection to G. H.
Reid’s nominees is that they are almost every-
thing which G. H. Reid has proved that they
should not be.
The Rev. Edgar, Wesleyan pulpiteer of Mel-
bourne, predicts the demoralisation of owners
if the “ tote” is permitted. It seems, accord-
ing to the parson’s view, that the good, kind
men who own racers will strive to deceive
people as to the condition of their nags in order
to work the machine for all it is worth. Then,
to show that he knows what he is talking about,
the reverend wedges slabs like this into his
sermon. Verbatim quote
Repents will be circulated that “ Daimio did a three-
mile ao the other morning, and, after clearing six fences
‘ like a bird,’ without putting a toe on one, skipped over
the last six furlongs in 1.4, and pulled up without turning
a hair.” Or that— “My horse, poor devil, I don’t know
what’s up with him. He couldn’t raise a gallop fit to
keep himself warm. I gave him a ‘go,’ and, after baulk-
ing once, he got over three fences, clouting every one of
them, barking his front legs, and, I think, hurt his stifle.
I am just starting him on the off chance.”
We are always wishing parsons would be men
of the world, and yet when they try to be they
invariably make pathetic exhibitions of them-
selves. The spectacle of a seven-foot adult like
Edgar anathematising the machine as calculated
to introduce an element of fiction into the tips
and racing rumors, as if they weren’t already, as
full of it as they can be, is calculated to give
the man in the street cramp in the stomach.
The bi-metallic problem is not growing -any
more lucid as time goes on. The perpetual
depreciation of the silver coinage of India,
China, Japan and other countries is admittedly
producing strange convulsions in the commercial
world. The Indian rupee, for example, con-
tains practically the same amount of silver as
the British and Australian florin, yet while it is
worth almost as much as it ever was to buy goods
in India it is worth only a little over Is. for ex-
changing into gold. Therefore, the Hindoo who
sends wheat to England can exchange every
sovereign he receives in payment into 20 florins
while the Australian exporter can only get ten
florins for his sovereign. Consequently, the
Hindoo gets twice as much for his produce as
he used to do. And, on the other hand, he has
to pay nearly 20 rupees instead of 10 for ill
worth of English goods, therefore ho-is protected
to the extent of 100 per cent, all round, conse-
quently in India and Japan manufactures are in-
creasing much faster than in England—in some
lines 10 or 12 times faster, according to
latest advices. This looks like a solid argu-
ment in favor of bi-metallism, only several
other considerations block the w;ay tl 'lndia
and Japan are doing well at present in
certain, directions because they have a depre-
ciated silver currency. Argentina is also
booming in certain directions because. jtd'MA
depreciated paper currepey, w hich produces

fore, under certain circumstances which are not
very well defined, a depreciated silver or paper
currency may be a blessing to the community,
for a time, at all events. The latter has the
advantage of being much easier to obtain ; all

Also, if it shows any signs of leaving off being

again by a new issue. Bi-metallism consists
practically in enacting that so much silver is

Walter Besant, the English novelist, says
in one of his books that England will one
day slick up a huge placard on her side of the
Straits of Dover, with the inscription for all the
world to read, “Suicided for the sake of Free-
trade.” Joseph Hatton, another English nove-
list and journalist, made a few remarks recently
on the same subject
It was so cold on that mountain that you couldn’t
sp ak the truth. Mark Twain had been there, and he
knew. It is tile same with the Fieetrade mountain. No-
body is ever certain of himself there. He certainly never
speaks the truth about it. Political controversy is, how-
ever, proteeted by a generally acknowledged licence to
exaggerate, if not to lie outright. The late John Bright
couldn't lie any more than Washington, but, like bis
great prototype, lie could strong language. If you
talked Protection or even reciprocity to him he called yon
a fool, an idiot, a pudding-head, and metaphorically landed
you one in the eye. Cobden'S breach of Freel rade principles
in his negotiation of the French treaty did not influence
him in the least. Bright squared his broad shoulders as
before, and swore—in his heart, if not in words—at every-
body who did not bow down to the fetish which he and
Cobdkx had set us. It often happens that men who can
manage everybody else s affairs cannot manage their
own. Coburn couldn’t manage his. Admirers had to
come to his rescue with subscriptions. Bright knew
better. His Freelrade was not all absorbing. It didn’t
allow of any interference with freedom of contract
between him and his work-people. He would have no
Government controlling their hours or wages. The wicked
Tories, however, like the Dock in Dover’s play, “had
sumthin’ to say to that,” and the English laborer got a taste
of freedom at Rochdale as well as llre foreigner. Nobody
asks for Protection to-day. A good many, however, sigh
for a little reciprocity. Freetraders no longer call these
people fools, but they tell them flnnncial romances. Even
Mr. Brvce, M.P., among his West Aberdeen constituents,
talked as if “ other count ties ” had gone hack to Protec-
tion. Few of his hearers probably knew any better.
“The countries which are now under Protection are suf.
fering as much as us,” he said. What other countries?
No other nation has ever adopted Freetrade or dreamt of
it. Every foreign nation since the days of Cobdf.x lias
increased its tariffs, to thedeslrn -lion cf more than one
ort wo great Engli-h industries. Then,of course, Mr. IIRYCK
talked of what we had done with the aid of Freelrade.
He might have sp'ken of what we had done in spite of
it. With a moderate import duly ou foreign goods,
not a protective duty, a duty for revenue, our agriculture
would not have been a ruined industry, our workhouses
would not be full, our poverty a blot upon civilisation.
The arrogance of Freetraders is colossal ; they claim
everything, allow nothing for natural progress,’for the
discovery of gold, for invention, for the world’s advance
in any way except such as they trace to Freelrade. One
would think trade and commerce had stood sli I in the
firotectire nations, which, on lh" contrary, have advanced
■I an equal ratio to that of England, and, in the case of
America, in a far greater ratio. I am not advancing
Protection, but toleration of opinion, honesty in con-
troversy, and at least a show of respect for men who
don’t choose to be ruled by a shibboleth or dragooned
by a party.
Peter Anderson and Co.
A SKETCH OF CITY LIFE.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
He had “ offices ” in Sydney, not so many years
ago,
And his shingle bore tho legend, “ Peter Ander-
son and Co. ;
But his real name was Careless, as the fellows
understood—
And his relatives considered that he wasn’t any
good.
Their gentle tongues had damned whatever “ char-
acter ” he had—
He was fond of beer and leisure—and tho “Co.”
was just as bad.
It was limited, in number, to a unit, was the Co.,
’Twas a bosom chum of Peter’s, and his Christian
name was Joe.
Tis a class of men belonging to these soul-for-
saken years:
Third-rate canvassers, collectors, journalists—and
auctioneers.
They are never very shabby, they are never very
spruce—
Goin" cheerfully and carelessly and smoothly to
the deuce.
Some are wanderers by profession, “ turning up ”
and gone as soon,
Trav’ling second-class, or steerage (when it’s cheap
they go saloon);
Free from “ists” and “ isms,” troubled little by
belief or doubt—
Lazy, purposeless, and useless—knocking round
and hanging out,
They take what they can get, and they give what
they can give,
God alone knows how they manage —God alone
knows how they live !
They are nearly always hard-up, but are cheerful
all the while—
Men whose energy and trousers wear out sooner
than their smile !
They, no doubt, like us, are haunted by the bore-
some “ if ” or “might,”
But their ghosts are ghosts of daylight—they are
men who live at night!
Peter met you with the comic smile of one who
knows you well,
And is mighty glad to see you and has got a joke
to tell.
He could laugh when all was black, and he could
grin when all was blue,
Sing a comic song and act it, and appreciate it,
too.
Seldom serious—doing business just as ’twere a
friendly game
Of billiards —nothing graver. And the Co. was
much the same.
They tried everything and nothing ’twixt the
shovel and the press,
And were more or less successful in their ventures
—mostly less.
(Once they ran a country paper while the editor
was ill,
And the local sinners chuckle over dingy copies
still—
Which are not upon the file—“ standing ” cause of
pain’d regret
To the editor. He hasn’t done apologising yet.
Yet, the editor would often, with an odd, pathetic
smile,
Bead a copy of the issue which is not upon the file,
And he’d think of his chum Peter, and perhaps of
his chum Joe,
And, maybe, he’d write a letter, care of Anderson
and Co.)
They'd been through it all and knew it in the land
of Bills and Jims—
Using Peter’s own expression, they had been in
“ various swims.”
Now and then they’d take an office, as they called
it —make a dash
Into business life as “agents”—something not
requiring cash.
(You can always furnish cheaply, when your cash
or credit fails,
With a packing-case, a hammer, and a pound of
two-inch nails—
And, maybe, a drop of varnish and sienna, too,
for tints,
And a scrap or two of oilcloth, and a yard or two
of chintz.)
They would “pull themselves together,” pay a
week’s rent in advance,
But it never lasted longer than a month by any
chance.
The “ office ” was their haven, for they lived there
when hard-up—
A “ daily” for <* table-cloth—a jam-tin for a cup;
And if the landlord’s bailiff happened round in
times like these
And seized the office-fittings—well, there wasn’t
much to seize,
They would leave him in possession. But, at last,
it came to pass
That a landlord seized the shingle, and he sold it
for old brass !
And it troubled Peter greatly—’twas a name, you
understand,
That he couldn’t purchase cheaply on a brass-plate
second-hand.
“ Might ’a’ left our name,” said Peter, with a
doleful smile at Joe.
“ Dash it all! I wish to gracious that our name
was ‘Smith V Co.’
What did Shakespeare say about it when they
treated him the same ?
‘ He that stole his purse was better than the skunk
that stole his name.’”
But a happy notion struck him and he got a plate
to match—
Sort of movable arrangement that was fasten’d
with a catch,
And when bad-luck brought the bailiff there’d be
nothing to lie seen
Save a piece of bevelled cedar where tho tenant’s
plate had been,
There would be no sign of Peter—there would be
no sipi of Joe
Till another portal boasted “ Peter Anderson and
Co.”
They hung out as long as possible, and after that
they shot
The moon, and took an office where the landlords
knew them not.
And when times were locomotive ! billiard-rooms
and private bars —
Spicy parties at the cafe—long cab-drives beneath
the stars;
Private picnics down the Harbor—shady camp-
ings-out, you know —
No one would have dreamed ’twas Peter—no one
would have thought ’twas Joe !
Free-and-easies in their “diggings,” when the
funds began to fail,
Bosom churns, cigars, tobacco, and a case of
English ale—
Gloriously drunk and happy, till they heard the
roosters crow—
And the landlady and neighbours made com-
plaints about the co. ;
But that life, it might be liken’d to a reckless
drinking-song,
For it can’t go on for ever, and it never lasted
long.
Debt-collecting ruined Peter—people talked him
round too oft,
For his heart was soft as butter (and the Co.’s was
just as soft).
He would cheer the haggard “missus,”and he’d
tell her not to fret,
And he’d ask the worried debtor round with him
to have a wet;
He would ask him round the corner, and it seemed
to him and her.
After each of Peter’s visits, things were brighter
than they were.
But, of course, it wasn’t business—only Peter’s
careless way;
And perhaps it pays in heaven, but on earth it
doesn’t pay.
They got harder up than ever, and, to make it
worse, the Co.
Went more often round the corner than was good
for him to go.
It seems the girl that he loved with the one pure
love of life,
Who, perhaps, loved him—and pitied—had more
sense than be the wife
Of a vagabond and drunkard —well, the world is
free to think
That his., death was caused, or hastened, by
debauchery and drink.
“ I might live,” he said to Peter, “but I haven’t
got the nerve—
“l am going, Peter, going—going, going (no
reserve).
“‘Eat and drink and love’ they tell us, ‘for to-
morrow we may die,’
‘ Buy experience ’ —and we bought it—we’re ex-
perienced, you and I.”
Then, with a weary movement of his hand across
his brow:
“The death of such philosophy’s the death I’m
dying now.
“Cricket, football, gambling, racing, larks and
syrens, roaring sprees—
Pull yourself together, Peter, there are better
things than these !
When you feel your life is sinking in a dull and
useless course,
And begin to find in drinking keener pleasure—
and remorse—
When you feel the love of leisure on your careless
heart take holt,
Break away from friends and pleasure, tho’ it give
your heart a jolt.
Shun the poison breath of cities—billiard-rooms
and private bars,
Go where yon can breathe God’s air and see the
grandeur of the stars !
‘ Find again and follow up the old ambitions that
you had ”
[See if you can raise a drink, old man, I’m feelin’
mighty bad.]
“ Pull yourself together, Peter, ’tis the dyin’ wish
of Joe,
* That the business world shall honor Peter Ander-
son and Co.
Bid ’em bear me to the forest when I’ve smoked
to kingdom come—
Let me slumber ’neath the daisies—for my last
request is—rum.
Hot and sweeten’d, nip o’ butter—squeeze o’
lemon, Pete,” he sigh’d.
And, when Peter went to fetch it, Joseph went
to sleep—and died
With a smile —anticipation, maybe, of the peace to
come,
Or a joke to try on Peter—or, perhaps, it was the
rum.
Peter staggered, gripped the table, swerved as some
old drunkard swerves,
At a gulp he drank the toddy, just to brace his
shattered nerves.
It was awful, if you like. But then he hadn’t time
to think —
All is nothing! Nothing matters! Fill your
glasses—Dead man’s drink.
Yet, to show his heart was not of human decency
bereft,
Peter paid the undertaker. He got dnink on what
was left;
Then he shed some tears, half-maudlin, on the
grave where lay the Co.,
And he drifted to a township where the city
failures go.
Where, though haunted by the man he was, the
wreck he yet might be,
Or the man he might have been, or by each spectre
of the three,
And the dying words of Joseph, ringing through
his own despair,
Peter “ pulled himself together and he started busi-
ness ” there.
But his life was very lonely, and his heart was
very sad,
And no help to reformation was the company he
had—
Men who might have been, who had been, but who
weren’t in the swim—
’Twas a town of wrecks and failures—they appre-
ciated him.
They would ask him who the Co. was—that queer
company he kept—
And he'd always answer vaguely—he would say
his partner slept;
That he had a “sleeping partner”—jesting while
his spirit broke—
And they grinned above their glasses, for they
took it as a joke.
He would shout while he had money, he would
joke while he had breath—
No one seemed to care or notice how he drank him-
self to death.
Till at last there came a morning when his grin
was seen no more—
He was gone from out the office, and his shingle
from the door.
And a bound’ry-rider joggingout across the noigh-
b’ring run
Was attracted by a something that was blazing in
the sun ;
And he found that it was Peter, lying peacefully
at rest.
With a bottle close beside him and his shingle on
his breast.
Well, they analysed the bottle, and it would ap-
jiear that he
Qualified the drink with something good for
setting spirits free.
Though ’twas plain ’twas self-destruction—“’twas
his own affair,” they said.
And the jury viewed him sadly, and they found —
that he was dead.
And they “laid him ’neath a gum-tree”. Drink-
ing mates—inspired by rum—
Took the nlate in maudlin sorrow and they nailed
it to the gum—
(And the friendly hands were shaky as evinced by
many a dent) —
Nailed with nails of diff’rent breeds and sizes (most
of them were bent),
And for months the plate was polished 1 Wo are
tempted here to state
That the angels down from heaven came and
polished Peter’s plate—
Or the spirit of his partner. But ’twas rumored,
anyhow,
That 'twas polished every evening by a noighb’ring
cocky’s cow.
Twas a sheltered spot in winter when the flats
were bleak and dam]:) —
Cosy nook among the ranges where the cattle used
to camp
And perhaps some mangy horses. Let the gentle
reader choose
Twixt the angels and the cattle of the local cock-
atoos.
And the cattle went on camping till there wasn’t
any mound —
There was nothing save a dusty little hollow in
the ground.
There’s a chance the plate corroded and it fell off
bit by bit,
And was buried in the grasses, or the bark grew
over it—
Anyway, it disappeared. We have reason though
to think
That a wand’ring swaggy stole it and exchanged
it for a drink.
’Mongst the happy careless angels, loafing round in
promised land,
Some there will be who have “been there,” who
will smile and understand
When they see a spectre drifting (rather vaguely)
_up the aisle
With a brass-plate in his hand, and on his face a
lonesome smile —
They will know that it is Peter —having heard of
him no doubt—
And perhaps he’ll get a corner there to hang his
shingle out.
Where he’ll start again in business, and in part-
nership with Joe :
“Harp and crown and music-agents—Peter
Anderson and Co.”
HENRY LAWSON.
The Wild-Cat Column .
The annual report of the Maoriland Government
Insurance Department is another solid evidence that
Stale Socialism, in this line of business, is a vast success.
For Ihe year, the department is-md 3301 policies com-
pared with 3204 last year and 2615 in 1892. The total
premiums for the year were £242,424, and the total in-
come, including interest on the invested funds, £369 478,
or mo f e than in 1893. Interest alone yielded
£115,270 —while the total claims by death and maturity
of endowments were barely £114,000. Over £135,000
was added to the funds during the year, making them,
apart from a large and solid reserve, £2,264 239. The
expenses, despite the fact that the Land and Income-tax
bear far more heavily on this institution than on any
other of its kind in Australasia, were only 15 per cent, of
the income—the smalltst rate in Australasia, ihat of the
A.M.P, Society alone excepted. Considering that the
JI.L. Government department charges the lowest pieminm
rates, one society alone excepted, in Australasia; that it
is the only concern of its kind where every policy is
guaranteed by the State ; and that it is economically
managed, and further supported by the second largest
insurance fund in these provinces, it is the ideal insurance
office of the world. Also, it is a solid evidence that State
management can be both cheaper and better than the
private article.
