The British depositors having proved utterly
unmanageable, the City of Melbourne Bank closed its
doors last week. There is talk of a possible re-opening,
but the chances are 100 to 1 against it. Looked at from a
purely business standpoint, the institution is much belter
dead. It bad hardly em ugh business latterly to pay
expenses, yet its existence helped to intensify the com
petition. Its deposits were almost wholly English, so
most of its interest, payments went out of the country,
and its closure slops an outflow of £120,000 per annum.
If the British depositors had accepted the offers made to
them, it is just possible that they would have got the
whole of their £2,750,000 in the end, whereas by liquida-
ting they will probably get about half of it, so that one
item of Australia’s foreign indebtedness will he wiped off
at a cheap rate. The shareholders won't lose much in
calls, for past experience has shown that they
are a very unpromising lot to extract calls
from, and, meanwhile, the attempt to collect them
may afford profitable employment to somebody. They
won’t lose much in the way of dividends either,
for there was no visible prospect cf any divs. for
many years to come, and their capital—which wasn’t
much—was practically gone already. Also, there is just
a chance that the fate of the City of Melb. Bank, which
burst through its British deposits, may help to prevent
other banks rushing, in the same insensate fashion, after
British capital, and that the less of the British dep sitors
may scare some of them off this country for the future.
Apart from the melancholy fact that about 70 people are
thrown out of employment, the result is a thing to be
thankful for in every respect.
Already it looks as if there was going to be a
desperate struggle to smother possible “ revelations ” in
re the City of Melbourne Bank. It was known about 9.45
on Wednesday morning that the British creditors were
going to demand a liquidation order, hut instead of
waiting for the inevitable the board look such exceed-
ingly active steps that by 10.30 the Attornev-General
hurst into Judge Hood’s court and obtained an order
on tile hoard’s own petition. He also turned up
befor- the C.J. with a second petition by a private share-
holder, and shortly afterwards he arrived again b< fore
Judge Hood and secured the appointment of the board’s
nominee as liquidator. A few hours afterwards lie turned
up again as the representative of the British ci editors and
wailed to the eff* ot tbat. it was an absurdity to appoint the
directors’ nominee to inquire into the directors’ alleged
mismanagement, and demanded the appointment of
Dtn-ker as liquidator, hut lire Court declim d to alter the
arrangement already made. Evidently tire slow-going
British cnditor dorsn’t understand yet the velocity of
which a Melbourne director is capable at a crisis, or he
would have climbed over tire hoard’s dead body to get
his petition in first.
Fivo minutes after the City of Melb. Bank
closed its doors, a youthful lover of tire naked truth bad
chalkeel on its walls near the door lire legend “ BUNG I "
The Colonial Bank would almost certainly have
met with a similar fate, only that it wisely abjured
British dipositors altogether. And now, if the board
has any sense of gratitude, it will hold a special service of
thanksgiving for its escape, and set apart a day for a
great orgy among the staff, and Swilliarn Claike. if a
really grateful man, will let off rockets, and set a barrel
of tar on lire, and bold a notable dog-flght on the lawn in
front of his big mansion.
Over .£300,000 of the money which the Melb.
Metropolitan Board of Works borrowed at au exorbitant
rate in England is in the City of Melbourne Bank. If
that Institution pans out for 10s. in the £ the Board's
financing will cost the people of Melbourne £150,000 in
immediate cash and £6700 a year interest thereon for the
next half-century or so.

A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION. ,
It has long been known to scientists that for
improving the appearance and beautifying th<
face oatmeal is far and away superior to all other
foods, but it had one drawback—the tendency to
heat the blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists has been devoted to
the removal of this imperfection; and now, after
long years of careful and diligent research, art has
tiimnphed, and Uncle Toby’s Rolled O its is the
result, for, by skilful manipulation in the manu-
facture, all the heating properties have been ex-
tracted, so that now a beautiful complexion of
peach and ivory mav fie had by anyone who makes
a continuous use of Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats.
Maoriland Govt, reckons on a surplus next year
of just. £9357. The estimated revenue is 165,000 less
than last year, and the estimated expenditure 165,0 0
more. If these figures prove correct the beautiful
Treasurer Ward is getting dangerously near to having a
deficit instead of a surplus. In Maoriland the surplus is
the only support of t lie Public Works Fund; if it slops
the fund necessarily breaks down, and then the borrowing
disease will probably start afresh.

« * • •
The Royal Bank of Queensland still lives up to
Its reputation as a sound and well-managed concern. Its
profits for the half-year are £5946 against 16476 last half,
and £6360 the one before. It, pays its usual dividend at
the rate of 2t) per cent, per annum, adds £l5OO to reserve,
making it £28,500; and has a balance of £7051 still to
credit of profit and loss. The coin, bullion, Govt, securi-
ties, and Treasury notes ( 1245,444) equal 31 per cent, of
the entire liabilities to the public. Theßo.vnl, by I lie
terms of its reconstruction scheme, lias to carry to reserve
regularly cue-fourth of its profits, and it is a great pity
that the same wise provision does not apply to all other
banks. Ttie balance-sheet lumps together, after the
usual heterogeneous fashion, current accounts, recon-
structed deposits, and new fixed deposits, and in other
respects it gives about the same amount of information
as usual. So far ns the information goes, however, it
looks remarkably well.

At the Commercial Bank of Australia’s meeting
a bird of ill omen named Gillolt got, up to remark that
*'further reconstruction must he carried out hecaus it
was impossible for the hank to pay ail its debts within the
five years prescribed by the reconstruction scheme;” If
the idea is that every depositor will take his money when
it comes due and carry it away in a hag, it is quite
probable that neither the Commercial nor a good many
other hanks could stand the shock. Hut the chances are
that, so long as Gyles Turner’s institution seems able to
pay the 4) per cent, regularly, it will have to make its
depositors remove their money by brute force. The
depositor only wants his money when he thinks he can’t
get it; if he can get it, then lie would rattier not have it,
and wants the interest instead. When it looked as if tlie
City of Melbourne couldn’t pay, the depositors wanted
their cash in less than half an instant; hut when the Vic-
toria recently released an instalment, it was almost ail
re-deposited at a lower rate of interest. Hanking is a
curious science, which depends mainly on the fact that
mvi always wants immediately the thing lie can’t get,
and doesn't care a straw for the tiling he can have at any
time.

The New Zealand! Accident. Insurance Co. pre-
sent a gorgeous balance-sheet. On a paid-up capital of
£5900 it lias made a net profit for the year of £3900. It
pays a dividend of 124 per cent., adds £lOOO to reserve-
fund (making it £6000), and £2OOO to contingent liabilities
fund to provide for any sudden disaster of great magni-
tude (making it £3000). It has also a re-insnrance fund
of £5OOO and £1994 to credit of profit and loss; and its
balance-sheet, which is a model of perspicacity, sets
forth in detail how all these funds are invested, so that
they look a lot more real than the average reserve.

• • • •
Maoriland proposes to subsidise a direct steam
sen ice with Canada to the extent of £20,000 per annum,
tlie bulk of the utu to he raised by a re-adjustment of
the death-duties. Maoriland is Hie only one of
the Australian where property devolving upon a
widow or widower is free from succession - duty.
All Much property is exempt, and where either widow
or widower takes a life interest in property yielding
a yearly income of less than £5OO, collection of the duty
is postponed until the death of the life tenant. So the
Treasurer proposes to abolish all exemptions on all pro-
perty devolving upon a wife or husband, barring estates
under £3OO, which «ill go free. Also, the rate of duty
to he collected on all properly disposed of by will, settle-
ment, gift, or family arrangement is to be determined
as in England.

Melb. Jews say that a spunky pawnbroker
holding out for 12 months is sure of a fortune, Vic,
pawnbrokers’ recognised 80% interest-ride is calculated
on half-crown loans. A man gelling 2s. 6d. on a pledge
pays 2d. a month interest; anything over 2s. 6d. and
under ss. pays 4d. In other words, the charge is 2d. per
month on every half-crown or part of half-crown. Small
advances, therefore, pay the broker best—as the workman
scon finds out. An unfortunate mechanic forced to pawn
his tools one by one receives, p rhaps, on a chi-el 3d. ; on
a plane, 4d ; on a saw, 61. U •is charged in e ich case (he
full interest on 2s. 6 1., and by the time his whole kit is
“up t.ie spout,” though he may not have had half-a-
crown da the lot, lie will have to pay a 500 per cent,
redemption penalty. In England 25% is charged on the
same basis, the average for European countries being
about 121%. After the State Hank, the Slate Trustee,
and the State Grog-shop, must come the Slate Pawn-shop.

When Mr. Patton introduced the system of
squ ire set timbering with Oregon into the Broken
Hill mine, he said he adopted this method because
filling in w ith surface mullock (as cosily a work,
lie aided, as the breaking of the ore) would he
avoided. Very soon, however, it was found that filling-in
was still necessary. Mr. Zeb. Lane was the first to pro
minently insist upon the absolute necessity of it; audio
that policy, pursued under his ninnagem nt and ever
since, is to he ascribed the fact that Block 14 lias siood fast
while serious movements were occurring on both sides.
The collapse of the British, costing, according to some
estimates, £BO,OOO, proved once for nil that, no matter
how much timber might he put in, a Broken Hill-mine
would cave in if the workings were not “mullocked up.”
The new si stem of limbering was, however, pretty to look
at and the nice sawn limb r was easy lo handle ; so the
system has not been seriously challenged since, notwith-
standing that it failed so utterly to do the work which
Mr. Patton expected from it.
One proposal made some years ago, and now
likely to he revived, is to substitute concrete pillars for
timber in the B.H. mines, slag being the base of the con-
crete. Some Adelaide people (they afterwards established
the cement works at Marino) first put this proposal for-
ward. Their claim, they say, was jumped by a Sydney
syndicate, which did nothing hut spend ah its capital.
The Proprietary Co. alone has been paying over £100,090
a year for timh r, got in shipments, through a contractor,
from Puget Sound, America. Eight years ago Mr. J. L.
Ueid, afterwards a director of the co., made inquiries
regarding possible Tasmanian supplies of hardwood;
but. shortly afterwards Mr. Patton came along lo knock
that idea on the head.

The Broken Hill fire has been a boon to the
local Water Supply Co. During July the consumption of
water from the co.’s mains was 16,500,000 gals., of which
it is estimated that the Proprietary mine took 13,000,000
gals. And the fire only commenced on the evening of
July 21.

George Brown, a Yandoit (near Castlemaine)
miner found a 2160 z. nugget a few days ago. For the
previous four months work lie bail earned half a dwt. per
month, and he bad just made up bis mind that the shaft
he was in wis a failure when ids pick rang strangely. It
was tlie old commonplace story of the conventional
miner. Brown had hit a nugget worth £855. lie took it
straight to the Mint, and the papers w rote pars about
him. The nugget was very ugly—a mere coarse clot of
gold with no more beauty than a bullock’s liver. As far
as the present writer knows, Westtalia has produced no
nugget bigger than 950 z. The Yandoit nugget, had it
been found in Westralia, might, have led on a rush of 5000
men. But Yandoit look it very calmly. Brown was
asked if he were going to take a holiday on the strength
of Ins And. ‘* What nonsense,” he said, “I shall work as
hard ns ever I did ; 1 have worked hard all my life, and
I’ll die that way.”

The Outtrim Coal Mining Co.’s main seam
(4ft. 6in.) at ouinbuinm (Vie.) was discovered by a young
man named Johnson in Aug., '92. Johnson—though his
time was valuable—spent several weeks in sinking a pit,
diverting the course of a stream, and cutting a saddle-
track from the road to the find. The speculators turned
out in great force to admire the newly-fmn d seam. They
formed a co. ; christened the mine ; paid Johnson a few
Shillings for cutting the track, and rewarded him with—a
glass of champagne.

A mining boom is on in Auckland province
(M.L.) owing, principally, to the attention of British
capitalists and spectators having been attracted
by the rich yields from the Hauraki Co.’s mine at
Coromandel, and the Wailii Co.’s mine. Upper Thames.
Since Ist O t. last £65,000 bullion has been won from the
former mine. The Wailii has yielded during the present
year £60,742, and last year £83,023.
A number ot Coromandel properties have been
floated in London on the strength of the Hauraki output.
Some have good prospects of success, hut the field is noto-
riously patchy, and there are likely to lie many failutes.
Fifteen claims have also been floated in Auckland - iii
some instances with the idea that British co.’s will even-
tually buy.
Messrs. Byles (a mining expert), Roth well
(panner in a London firm of stock-brokers), and Well-
stead (an English capitalist) lately inspected several Upper
Thames properties which are being placed in London on
terms which should prove very satisfactory to the present
shareholders. These mines have all been considerably
d velnped, are yielding bullion, and will at hast, give the
shareholders a for run. The Held is extensive, hut, the
slom> is not rich. The bullion v aries greatly in quality,
Woodstock being worth about 95.; Wailekann,2ls.; Wailiq
275.; Cronri. 355. Though most of whatever profits may
he mad 1 will go into Brilisli pockets, the winning of the
bullion, especially in the low-grade Upper Thames district,
w ill give much employment. Twice in the past the failing
fortunes of ttie Auckland province have been retrieved
by a mining r rival. And th-re is every indication that
the i xperience will again be repeated.

don at 15s. A small parcel changed hat <ls in Auckland
at 20s. The co. is a most entirely British, and, until a
f> tv months ago, there was only one shareholder in the
pioxince, hut some thousands of shares have since been
bought by Aucklanders. .None of the local men sue-
Deeded in getting in at much under 10s. There art
320,000 sliares in the co.

A lesson for English co.'s mining in Australia.
Tile South German mine, Vic., crushed, during the last
six months, 6361 tons for 57610 z. Declared in diva.
£12.600, and the office expenses and directors' fees were
£149 16s. 2d.

Victoria last year employed 27,889 gold, 748
coal, and 67 other miners. 673,6800 z. gold (419,372 quarts,
254,308 alluvial) were raised, and 175,447 tons of coal,
worth £97,000.
The largest Vic. quartz and alluvial mine vields
last year were: Long Tunnel, 20, C0W. (quartz), and
Madame Berry, 18,7350 z. (alluvial). £459,721 was paid in
mine dividends ; Madame Berry heading the list with
£38,250.

In these chiys of rabbit-factories, bunny’s
unpopularity is a thins of the past. At Camper-
down (Vic.) cannery, the preservers start work at
4 a.nil on carcases prepared over-night at the rate
of 1500 pair per man.
Says a lawyer ; “ All attempts at cheapen-
ing law have only lessened the lawyer’s labor, not
his costs. Take the simplification of wills :it
only resulted in more people making their own ;
and you know the last toast at law-students’
dinners—‘ Here’s to the man that makes his own
will.’ ”
After all, Connaught cannot accept the
British Army chieftaincy. This is a severe blow
to Queen Victoria, and it must be assumed that
Salisbury’s advice was against the step. In fact,
he has only made the offer for form’s sake, after
obtaining an assurance it would be declined.
William V\ ilson, the last of the Queensland
buccaneers, who abducted a half-caste girl and two
male natives in a stolen lugger from Thursday
Island, has been heard from. He got us far as
Hutch New Guinea, and his impressed crew got
overboard and swam a-bore, Then, seeing another
lugger—a harmless craft in the pearl-shell trade-
bearing down on him, lie poisoned himself to
avoid capture. The buccaneer doesn’t get much
of a run for his money nowadays.
A Glebe (Sydney) man, about three weeks
ago, was thought by bis wife to be dead ; his jaws
were tied up in regulation fashion, a shilling was
put on each eyelid, and he was “laid out.” Some
two hours later, his vv'fe, from another room,
heard a call— 41 Florrie ! ” Entering the corpse
chamber, she found the dead man sitting up ; and
he spake—‘*l've been such a long way, and I’ve
only come back to tell yon that I’m going to
Heaven. Give me a drink !” Then he drank a
glass of ale (I) and one of milk ; and the next time
that he “ went off” lie stayed off.
The ghastly ignorance which directs
“ tariff reform ” in the Vic. Assembly came out
strong in a discussion re friction-grease. There
was some apologetic idea of reducing the duty on
friction-grease, until somebody arose and stated
that it was made from tallow, a native product
deserving all possible encouragement. Then one
speaker after another expressed an earnest desire
for the advancement of the tallow-trade, and the
item of friction-grease was cautiously treated,
with that noble end in view. Probably there is
not one man in the Assembly who knows that
friction-grease contains no tallow whatever.

WOLFE’S
SCHNAPPS.
Society
Out. in one of the south-western parts of
N.S. Wales there is an immense plain, and in over
100 square miles of dead-level surface one tree
forms the only break in the monotony. Apart
from it there is hardly even a shrub or a twig in a
long day’s ride. A few years ago an old resident
of the locality was driving across this howling
waste -in open- daylight at midsummer, and by
some misadventure, which he never has been able
to explain, he wrecked his buggy against that
tree, maimed his horse, broke his harness in
several places, and dislocated himself considerably.
All the Marine Board inquiry in the world would
never have revealed how he did it. This is
probably the way the Catterthun got on to the
Seal Rocks, only as her misadventure took place
at night instead of in broad daylight, it is all
the less unaccountable. The Board will doubtless
find that she arrived at her lamentable end through
the course being altered prematurely, or not altered
in time, so that she was going in the wrong direc-
tion—as no vessel ever arrives at the wrong place
except by going in the wrong direction, this is a
thoroughly gafe assumption. The settler who, in
his sober senses and in broad daylight, drove into
the only tree within 100 square miles, got there
from the same cause. But this explanation doesn’t
explain how either of them happened to go in
the wrong direction.
The cussedness of objects inanimate probably
has a good deal to do with such mishaps, and bad
luck has a good deal more. The writer once came
across a beautiful illustration of the latter cause.
He was troubled with nocturnal blindness, and
for some weeks he used to get home late at night
along a very quiet road on the outskirts of a very
quiet town, principally by groping his way
along the fences. One night he put a hand
out suddenly to feel for the fence and struck a
Photo. by Talma.
Miss OLLTFFE, as Paula Tanqueray.
fndy a tremendous whack on the mouth —the only
1 idy probably who had come along that way at
that hour any time in six months or more. Ten
minutes of copious explanation in the middle of
the road left her still unconvinced, and when at
last she went on her hysterical way to tell the
story to her male relations her assailant had lost
hi a, sense of direction utterly, and had to make a
fresh start at random. If he had been the Catter-
thun and she had been the one "small rock in a six
months’ voyage, there would have been a high-
class wreck that night.
On the evening of the wreck some of the people
on board the Catterthffn got talking of the
Wairarapa disaster, and the chief officer remarked :
“I have quite made up my mind that whatever
death I die I will not be drowned.” Next morning
he was among the missing, but he may yet turn
up floating on a hen-coop somewhere ; the opti-
mist seldom gets drowned. Captain Fawkes, the
pilot, who hung on to a board in a cold, stormy
sea, and cheerfully told the man on the other end
of the plank to “ keep his pecker up,” is a case in
point.
Already about 10 people have been heard of who
just missed sailing by the Catterthun through a
variety of remarkable causes. About this number
of people always just miss sailing in every vessel
which is lost, so presumably an eqdal number miss
the one which gets safely to its destination. From
these data The Bulletin roughly estimates that
every year over 100,000 Australians miss their
passages and are accidentally left behind. Evi-
dently we are a beastly unpunctual race.
# * *
Most of the lady passengers by the Catter-
thun seem to have been too terrified to leave the
saloon, and they drowned there. So far as the
evidence goes the Chinese and other Asiatics who
formed the crew behaved remarkably well ; but
there were only a few minutes in which to do any-
thing, the night was dark and stormy, all the
boats save one were smashed by the heavy seas,
and in every possible respect luck was against the
unfortunate ship. Also, though every possible
survivor seems to have been persistently inter-
viewed no one seems able to explain how the
Catterthun contrived to get so far out of her course.
The gale, at all events, cannot be held responsible,
for it would tend to drive her further out to sea
rather than to force her in-shore. Moreover, there
are two lighthouses in the immediate vicinity of
the disaster. So far there are not even the usual
number of picturesque incidents to relate—pro-
bably because no one had time to be picturesque.
Probably the best one up to date is that told by
Capt. Fawkes, the Torres Straits pilot, who bor-
rowed the captain’s knife to cut a table adrift.
He went overboard with it in his hand, and as he
sank it occurred to him that he would like to
preserve the knife as a memento, so he put it in
his pocket. AVhich is strongly suggestive of Mr.
Wemmick, who always held on to Portable Pro-
perty, even under adverse circumstances,
* * *
The lenities of the drowning. Catter-
thun passenger T. C. Crane has fluked on to a
floating meat-safe, and is presently joined by a
heathen Chinee. Chow (persuasively): “More
better you go catchee ’nother piecee box. ” Crane:
“More better you.”

The message on the billy is a familiar
pl an with entombed miners. The writer was
trucking in a Vic. mine, about 13 veara ago, when
r i r ... . ... ’i ■ m i ■
a Cousin .lack faceman was trapped m a i- drive.
They were 50 hours getting at him, and when the
victim was dragged out he looked as if he had
been in a month. “How long have I been in
here, men?” he asked. He was told. “Thee|s
lyin’ to me,” he said ; “aw counted 10 days.” His
billy was brought on top after he was taken home,
and the boss of the shift and the engine-driver
spent an hour trying to decipher the message
scratched upon it. At last it panned out :
“Praetor, I did steal they ducks, and may the
Lord have mercy on me. Amen ! ”
THE TROUBLES OF AN UP-COUNTRY BANKER.
(Scene — Manager's office.)
CUSTOMER: “Say, guv'nor, will ye obleege by puttin' this 'ere gold-watch
away in yer iron box fer safety? I be afeard some thievin'
blaggard may come along and may be take the works out on her,
and lave on’y the case on me! And I wud 'ave ye w'ar her from
time to time to kape the rust and flies off her, and be most sartin
to wind her reg’lar ev'ry day. Gan ye spare time to come out
and 'ave a nip?”
The men entombed in the M‘Evoy mine
at Eldorado, Vic., burnt all their caudles. They
evidently recognised from the first the impossi-
bility of a rescue, otherwise they would have
extinguished their caudles. Every miner knows
that it is wise to put out his light when trapped,
and reserve all the air for his own consumption—
or, if there is air enough, to save the candle for a
possible meal. The M’Evoy miners seem to
have looked the situation in the face, and resolved
to have it over as quickly as possible.
# * *
It looks as though Acting-Gov. Madden
may need to be placed under proper control before
bis hour of greatness has expired. His sense, of
the eternal fitness of things as regards vice-regal
performances seems to begin and end with a serio-
comic “make-up.” In a burst of enthusiasm
Excellency Madden stuck his signature at the
head of a requisition to the O’Hara, desiring that
athletic citizen to stand for the position of indoor
surgeon at Melb. Hospital. Such displays of
partisanship—for the Hospital election is gener-
ally a fierce struggle between rival practitioners—
ill become a deputy-royal personage, and ought to
be promptly suppressed.
• * *
Mrs. Governor Madden’s hysterical glori-
fication of debutantes at Government House has
added a new horror to Melb. life. Local papers
publish portraits of these young women. Every
fond mocher in the district is laying plans to get
her budding daughter “presented ” as soon as
possible, because she’s certain that the dear child
can’t suffer by comparison with several of the
visions that have already got into print. The
average picture of a debutante seems calculated to
console any aged buck for the loss of his dancing
days.
# • #
In their Guvmentouse dances the Maddens
have been trying to improve on private secretary
Wallington’s methods. The result has been
disaster. Instead of inviting all from the same
district at a time, they proceeded alphabetically,
thus upsetting all the neighborly cliques of the
Hopetoun rigime. And the dais exhibition of
debutantes was unpopular, too; but their worst
blunder was the supper, which even the Argus
distinctly reflected on. Sassiety won’t wag its
tail without cakes, and so for the second dance
there were 400 refusals.
* * *
'Victoria's Acting-Governor is making the
best of his time in office. He goes everywhere,
and never refuses to open anything from a dog-
show to a sewing-meeting. Rumor has it that
he is booked to open the first dozen oysters in a new
saloon.
* # •
In Morrison’s “ Tales of Mean Streets ” is
a story of two women, mother and daughter,
named Perkins. From being in a position of
comfort their little possessions gradually go from
them, the piano first, until in the end, having col-
lapsed through utter destitution, they are found
by their neighbors after being dead some days.
All of which, without exception—name, circum-
stances and relationship—fit the deaths of Mrs.
Perkins and her daughter at South Melb. about
the beginning of the year. Unconscious cerebra-
tion or coincidence —which ?
* * *
Minnie Dean, the Maoriland baby-farmer,
according to a recent Dunedin paper, is getting a
new and very becoming dress to be hanged in.
She eats well, sleeps well, and talks pleasantly to
her visitors about all manner of subjects, including
her trial and execution. “ Hanging has no terrors
for her,” says the local scribe, “and she is not at
times averse to a mild joke on the subject.” No
doubt, to that rare woman who has a genuine
sense of humor, there is something wildly ridi-
culous about an execution, even her own execu-
tion. Just here the question arises; What does
the alleged deterrent effect of capital punishment
amount to in the case of a woman who gets a new
and very becoming dress to bo hanged in ?

One of the Miss Saunders, killed by
Chinese fanatics, in a letter to a Melb. girl friend,
describes the undreamt-of temptations which assail
a lady missionary entering on work in China—
the proposals made them by wealthy English and
Americans.

Maoriland hospital nurses are coining on.
Their demand that all head-nurses lie called
“ Sister ’’was first granted. Asked whether t.iey
would prefer to append their Christian or sur-
names to this handle, they answered Christ ian.”
.So when some rude kid remarks, “ Sister Mary
walks like that,” the nurse will understand that
the fault is her own. One boorish trustee then
proposed that male attendants should he called
“Brother,” and the matron “Mother,” and the
doctor “Father John,” and then the trustees
might drop in as “Grandpa,” with the secretary
as “Uncle.” The “Sisters” said he was a very
nasty man. But, really, is not the whole system
of uniforms and “Sisters” and things nothing
more than a sickly imitation of the nunnery, or
else a copy of that silly custom that men have of
sticking on blue aprons and tin regalia, and then
calling erfch other Lord High Knights and Right
Worshipful Nincompoops, and things like that?

The Melb. divorce-case, wherein ib was
alleged that the woman had misbehaved herself
with the co-respondent, a butcher, in the refriger-
ating chamber attached to the shop, somewhat
recalls the case of a certain Australian hospital
nurse who, having an extensive amatory corres-
pondence, was in the habit of repairing to the
hospital dead-house to read her letters. Passion
in a freezing chamber! Australia is a great
country.
The tremendous in the Butler con-
spiracy case, at Sydney, was in no small degree,
it would appear, brought about by a vendetta
between two divorce-detectives.
# « •
Mrs. Butler’s brother, now under sentence
for perjury, is the unfortunate whom Mr. Justice
Windeyer knew to be guilty of perjury “ because
he shuffled his feet while in the witness-box.”
The learned Judge cited excellent authorities in
proof of this theory that shuffling the feet is an
indubitable proof of lying, and then committed
Butler for perjury. And yet the foot-test, despite
its great antiquity, has shown itself as unreliable
as Dr. M'Oarthy’s more modern door-test. So
long as arbitrary Judges suffer themselves to be
in any way influenced by such fads s trial by jury,
faulty though it is, must remain immeasurably pre-
ferable to trial by Judge.

The Sunday average attendance for July
at Sydney National Gallery was 2309; the week-
day average, 518. Or nearly as 9to 2. Which
should furnish an argument to liberal, educative
and unSabbatarian legislators to move for the
opening also of public libraries and reading-rooms
on Sundays. As an afterthought, will some
expert give the Sufiday average for attendances
at the pubs. ?

The Vic. authorities have of late been
doing their best to form a Hebrew colony in
Pcntiidge prison. But it is safe to predict that
when the Chosen again breathe the air of freedom
their very first move will be to apply for the
sequestration of the estates of the gaol officials,
who by then are bound to be heavily in their debt.
That they will still contrive to trade and take
down the Gentile admits of no manner of doubt.

More than one very big Australian estate
has, of late years, been effectually burst up
through the daughters of the house being left,
under their father’s will, large marriage-portions,
which the sons have to provide (by mortgage on
the estate) upon their sisters’ wedding-days.

A Melb. “ hard-up,” who got a job on a
Yic. dairy-farm, gives his experience to The Bul-
letin;:— .
This farmer owns close upon 2000 acres of land, and
gets £2l per week for his milk. The men’s hut is a
wretched hovel, so draughty that we could hardly keep a
candle alight; the beds, broken-down stretchers ;no con-
venience of .any description, not even a tin-disb to wash
in. I and three other men had to rise at 4.30 a.m., milk
.26 cow’s" %’ch,' then wash up cans and buckets. After this,
breakfast (sait meat, dry bread, tea). Sinking post holes
in stony ground till dinner-time (for dinner, salt mutton,
potatoes, rice, water). More post-holes till milking (4.30).
At 7.30 supper (corned “junk,”, bread, treacle, tea).
Then mix up cows’feed for nex( morning. Finished the
day’s work at B.3o—about 98 hours a week for 10s.
Atyfithis is no exception ; men are treated so on
scores of other Vic. farms. -<-

Broken Hill miners observe that, while
Acting-Gov. Madden and Mines-Minister Foster
hastened to show their practical sympathy with
the victims of the Eldorado accident, not a word
was heard from either Lieut.-Gov. Darley or
Mines-Minister Smith, of N.S.W., either by the
relatives of the men killed in the bigger accident
at the B.H. South, or by the committee which has
the relief fund in hand.

Mr. Justice Simpson deprecates, in the
interests of a “ most repectable body of men,” the
application of the term “ detect! ve ” to persons,
some of whom, lie has just discovered with amaze-
ment, “ would apparently stop at nothing !” But
isn’t this lamb-like innocence of the Bench a little
unconvincing—considering that the unscrupulous
scoundrelisrn of the divorce-evidence-jackal is as
commonly notorious as the fact that a good many
of Judge Simpson’s “respectable men” (from
whose ranks the private detective very often
comes) also will “stop at nothing” to secure a
conviction. Still, as the official “D” is under
responsibilities entirely lacking in the case of the
private inquiry man, why not place the latter
under license, promptly forfeitable for malpractice
by himself or his creatures?

August, ’9O; disgorge and pay costs! Second
do., August, ’92 ; shell out again ! Third do.,
August, ’95; actually “ struck-off ” at last!
Which things suggest a conundrum : Why
wouldn’t the unprotected shark have committed
i lie second and third of these “indiscretions?”
Because he might possibly still be in gaol or
“struck off,” for the first.

The Yic. railway department has again
raided the habitues who go first-class with second-
class tickets. The smart man, and, above all, the
smart woman, on such occasions, never produces
the ticket, but pleads having got in hurriedly
without any.

Melbourne Mayor Snowden’s one fierce
anxiety at present is to raise a memorial to the late
Photo. by Mendelssohn.
The slaughtered Lady Missionaries. The Misses SAUNDERS (late of Melbourne)
and their mother.
Salmon Wilson. What he wants to commemorate
in the late knight of Eroildoune nobody really
knows. All Wilson did was to fail in acclima-
tising Angora goats, ostriches and salmon, and to
succeed with axis deer. He also accumulated
much money from Australian sources, and care-
fully spent most of it at the other side of the
world. Being but a little pot of sounding brass,
he wanted a long handle, and got it by paying
some £30,000 to Melb. University to build a big
and useless hall. Also, bis money got several of
bis family married into swell but impecunious
families. One prominent lady, bothered by
Snowden at a Govt. House function about this
memorial project, gave him a prompt and decisive
retort courteous : “ If Sir Samuel Wilson’s family
arid friends think a memorial to him desirable,
then they are quite well enough off to pay for it
themselves without bothering the public!” Said
a distinguished member of Melb. University :
“ Snowden getting up a memorial to Wilson ?
Humph ! One nobody commemorating another
nobody—and Snowden is such a nobody.” And
the congregation softly sighed “ Amen ! ”

According to Dr. W. L. Mullen, of Melb.,
nine or ten years ago it was the regular thing for
a new arrival, or a fresh local M. 8., to take in his
first two years fees amounting in the gross to
,£3OOO. One medical man he knows started in a
country town, and in five years had netted
<£16,000. Nowadays, the man who can make
,£5OO a year in Melbourne has good cause to crow.
The passengers on an Adelaide to Mel-
bourne train had a breezy adventure, the other
dav. The driver, who was peacefully drunk
during the earlier part of the journey, suddenly
developed a fine case of the horrors after leaving
Nhill, and started trying to outrun a hairy green
devil with a blue tail who was pursuing him along
the rails. Then for about eight minutes the train
bumped along at a tremendous pace, and pas-
sengers were thrown on the carriage floors and
against the roofs, and everybody had his hair full
of travelling-bags and hat-boxes and umbrellas
which flew off the racks and moved around in all
directions. Meanwhile the fireman and driver
were having a joyous free fight for the possession
of the engine, and the latter being finally laid
out with a crowbar the train was pulled up and
the circus terminated. Some appropriate re-
marks about the accursed drink are held over for
want of space.

A Melbourne advt. :
YOUNG Lady, tired of the ballroom and tennis
young man, wishes to meet middle-aged gent.
Address, &c.
She will doubtless meet him —he is so plentiful
that it would be difficult to avoid him—and as
likely as not she will be sorry for it afterwards.
Meeting middle-aged gent, has been a turning-
point in the lives of more young ladies than any-
one can remember.

Countess Wachtmeister is being daily
interviewed by the faithful at the rooms of Syd-
ney Theosophical Society. To all questions con-
cerning the Judge exposure she maintains a
discreet silence ; and, if pressed, avers talking of it
will do no good, as Judge has himself settled
the matter by leaving the society and forming one
of his own. Two clergymen? who honored the
Countess with an interview, were much impressed
by her powers of doctrinal exposition.

LOOKING BACKWARDS.
Upon the glimmering seascape low
I see her face divine,
And in the rippling wavelets slow
Her azure glances shine.
Behind the purple dusk of even
Her paler form appears ;
Her whisper hushes in the heaven
The music of the spheres.
’Tis all a shadow that I see,
A form unreal and vain ;
For long ago she passed from me
And left me to my pain.
Too pure, too radiant white she gleamed
For love’s tumultuous glow ;
So tender and so frail, it seemed
She only came to go.
Her lily’s life was but begun
When God, who loved her best,
Took her, a maid unwoo’d, unwon,
To His scarce brighter breast.
Vic.
WYVIS.
AT LAST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
O'er wind-piled weeds I feel her breath
As we tread thro’ a shingled strand.
How well I can banish the fear of death,
Just by holding her dear white hand !
There’s a drug for the pain of life
And a chance in eternity ;
So I catch at frail Lilian, my wife,
And hurry along to the sea.
One long kiss in the velvet dark,
While the life’s on your trembling lip :
Together we’ll lie in the depths that mark
The lashing white wake of a ship.
ALRA DORION.

August 13,1895.
My Dear Moorabinda—
The Rev. Chad band’s moan of horror over
the birth-markon the “Shop Girl ” has been sud-
denly squelched by the missionary massacres in
China which have filled the papers for the last
few days, and set the “religious” world sending
black-edged cards to the various sections of itself,
and buying its cabbagee from Caucasians until the
thrill wears off. It is certainly dreadful reading,
conveying to the frivolous worldling the idea that
the Deity is not in touch with the Zenana mission
and the Church Society. Yet these institutions
will not take what looks to an outsider like the
very broadest hint from head-quarters, but will
continue sending young men and women to use-
lessly preach a strange doctrine, at great expense,
to a large, bland, apathetic yellow nation which
wouldn’t buck up spiritually even if the Pope him-
self went itinerating. Heaven is not a British
institution, and English-speaking people are not
responsible for the state of soul of a dun-colored
heathen planting cabbagee on the borders of
Russia; but still they will go out and try a fall, as
it were, with Confucius, and they never seem to
think when they get knocked out that, after all, the
money mayn’t be all behind them, as the sporting
men say. But these little suggestions that they
aren’t Wanted in that corner of the vineyard work
just the opposite way with the missionary party.
The certain result of this holocaust will be hordes
of hysterical pietists breaking their necks to win
bold advt. and martyrs’ haloes abroad, while their
own back-alley probably spells God with the
smallest of g’s.
Mark Twain, and his wife and daughters, are
now eii route from Vancouver to Sydney, where
M. T. starts his Australian lecture-tour, under the
aegis of the Most Travelled—at least, they were to
come by the VVarrimoo. I am prepared to find
Mark a disappointment—he cannot be so enter-
taining as his books. A book has the same
advantage over the writer as hidden music has
over a fat, oily tenor, with short arms, howling in
the open—the musio may be the same, but the
entourage destroys the bouquet and distracts your
attention. You cannot picture an ideal Mark
Twain—your fancy patches up a weird impos-
sibility with a poet’s hair, a cynic’s eye, an artist’s
hands, a wit’s profile, and a low-comedian’s feet —
and the reality will perhaps be n-iyiniscent of
Pastor Haweis. There is only one thirty I will
not forgive Mark Twain. 1 will not forgive jiim
if he only suggests “ Mr. Clemens.”
The Wibbigilla (or, words to that effect) dance,
got up by ’Varsity students at Need’s rooms
last Thursday, was in every way successful
—partly, I think, because it is so new, in
this day of charity festivals, to go and
dance purely for your own amusement, and not
to provide toothpicks for converted Chinese or
something of the sort. The place was stiff with
nice frocks, and if the prettiest turn-out was a
pale-blue satin and chiffon gown, with little blue
shoes and cheeky forget-me-nots in her hair, still,
a pale-yellow silk with snowdrops on top, worn by a
fine-looking Melbourne girl, made a close second.
Indeed, the field finished in a heap, as you may
say, for a dark-eyed double of Miss Inez Bensusan
in pale-blue silk with lace and blush ruses ran up
well; and the only really unpleasing turn-out was
that of a damsel in a very decollete take-poison-
in-the-limelight frock of white satin, green velvet,
and tea-colored lace. It was an ideal gown in
which to play the first act of a “Woman with a
Past ’’—the sort of garment which looks lively
with a cigarette, and completely out of place
on a well-meaning citizen at a students’ dance.
Miss “ Seven Little Australians ” Turner (who is
engaged to Mr. Curlewis, of the Sydney legal
brigade) wore a simply-girlish frock of white
cr£pon with scarlet poppies sealing her corsage, as
it were. Besides being so prettily dressed, the
wibbly-wobbly dance introduced the fashion of
having dance-programme and admission-ticket
on the same card—an economy that secretaries of
charity dances would do well to follow, if it
would work on a large scale.
Sydney Suburban Bicycle Club and the Dar
linghurst Harriers announce their intention of
holding a ball at West’s rooms on 22nd inst. Mr.
W. Kerr and Mr. Brierley are secretaries to what
promises to be a very smart little hop. And,
talking of dancing, reminds me that I have re-
ceived a waltz from W. H. Paling and Co. en-
titled “ The Venus,” by Madame Terlier. It is a
very chirrupy and melodious waltz with a certain
—shall I say—fretfulness about it that is piquant.
I like the “Venus;” it is so Frenchy, for one
thing. From Melbourne Miss Louisa Clarke
sends “ La Vision ” skirt-dance, dedicated to one
of the noble army of Melbourne amateur high-
kickers. I don’t know how it would go with the
skirt, but going alone the trifling music does not
leave a deep impression.
I hear that “ Lady ” was taken in hand,
some little time ago (at the instigation of a
Governor’s wife, I believe), by the Sydney Church
Home people, who thought to make a big ad. out
of Australia’s most notorious female character by
leading her gently back into the paths of repent-
ance. Their ill-success was the more disappoint-
ing because, while in the Home, Lady ’s
natural refinement came out on top and her
behaviour the whole time was absolutely perfect—
it was not possible to find fault with her. How-
ever, the Church Home should have had more
experience of human nature than to be buoyed up
by that. If a real work of reformation had begun
Lady would have been a little restive —to
say the least of it—now and then. The woman
who behaves perfectly while her whole life is
being stood on its head only means to endure it so
long as it suits her.
It strikes me that “Ladies ” would be
painfully frequent in the streets of the Australian
capitals if patient male relatives didn’t turn their
homes into inebriate-asylums to shelter them. It
happened recently, at a certain fashionable
Southern store, that a society lady rolled up in
her carriage and dived into the refreshment-room,
where she sat and imbibed a couple of bottles of
wine. When she rose she began to stagger and
daw the air for support in so alarming a manner
that an attendant (not altogether unused to such
crises) escorted her, with difficulty, to the lift, and
thence to her carriage, in which she bowled home
with her bonnet sideways and herfeeton the front
seat—a solemn warning to her tippling friends.
Sydney, also, is not without its share of red-faced
women who smell of corks. ■
There is a huge station-property in the droughty
Australian West where they do things in heavy
style—reminiscent of Lonnen’s “We dine at
sa-a-ven; whether there’s any dinner or not.”
Evening-dress is an of course, and. when the
rouseabout, disguised as a butler, announces dinner
served, the animals go in two by two d la Putts
Point. There everything is the usual silver-
smith’s show—and all is lovely but the bill of
fare. Salt horse, boiled potatoes, and pumpkin
cannot very well be disguised in even menu-
French,
Apropos of style in the back-blocks, a Bourke
correspondent tells me that black dress ties have
just reached Bourke, and asks me why men have
taken to them, repudiating the old white choker?
It is so long ago and men’s fashions are generally
so unreasonable (if anybody objects to this I only
point silently at sheep-hurdle collars which saw
their ear-lobes, and silk hats in summer) that I
scarcely remember; but I believe the first idea
was to distinguish the guest from the waiter.
Then, in Sydney, people pretended they wore
black haberdashery as mourning for the late
Governor. The only people who wear them in
town now are people who make a point of wearing
their clothes right out.
Mr. Malone, the popular Gee-Gee manager, has
had a heavenly time (from a strictly feminine
pbint of view) since the score of “ Gentleman
Joe ” came to hand. He has had all the costumes
to fix up for the new production (which arrived
with no instructions as to business or dressing, by
the way). People who pump-handled Mr. Malone
last week were left with little bits of frivolous
feminine materials in their grasp, and odds and
ends of pink silk and other dress patterns might
have been observed bulging out of his pockets.
The “Gentleman Joe” dresses are going to be
gorgeous, so they say, and so they ought to be if
a hungry craving for patterns on the managerial
part is any criterion.
This rush of new gowns is a nuisance to the
Gee-Gees, who pass hours at the frock-builder’s,
which might be more amusingly (I won’t say
profitably) passed elsewhere. Pretty Grace
Palotta found the rag-rush too much for her last
Thursday, and fainted at David Jones’, to the
consternation of everybody in the vicinity. I hear
that the rush for sal volatile and other (stimulants
was nothing short of a stampede—the men flying
for restoratives, while the women soaked her face
with water, hoping to see some of her fresh com-
plexion slipping, I suppose—but it didn’t. If you
really want to prove that your color is your very
own, there’s nothing like fainting when there are
women about. Though I don’t suppose, for in-
stance, that cold water would fetch off much color
from the face of a certain stalwart actress whom
we all know. It looks as if only sand-paper would
be equal to that.
“ The Shop Girl ” prospers at Sydney Lyceum,
despite its reputation for a certain malodorous-
ness of text here and there—though in one in-
stance the present play scores one for gracefully
waiving a rare chance to be suggestive. I mean
where Sir George Appleby (Cecil Hope, who
means to try his luck at later on) re-
marks : “Good Heavens ! is this my sou ? ” No-
body even winks or coughs here. The stage feel-
ing following this leading remark is absolutely
correct, and, I think, atones in part for the much-
discussed strawberry-mark dialogue, or Blanche
Massey’s reply to the question : “Does he take
your temperature?” “He takes everything."
The new polyglot (Hobson and “'Dolly ”) in
“ The Shop Girl,” distinguished throughout
by the perfect English accent of the per-
formers, no matter what tongue they are
warbling in, is a poor substitute for Maud
H. alone in “The Foundling” song; but
wherever Mr. Kooley uprears that strange ginger
poll a deep sadness falls on the show, and you lose
sight of the “ pond re de riz and little touch of
rouge” aspectof the “Shop Girl,’’and fall to think-
ing drearily that the Legislature really ouyhl to
make a rule to let the poor things sit down in work
hours. Apropos of the gigantic Mr. Monkhouse—
his taste for glittering jewels in private life is
quite Masonic and reminisceucy of the lady with
rings on her fingers and bells on her toes. One of
his chief ornaments is a diamond watch-chain,
which suggests (in the evening) that the elephan-
tine funny man has an electric railroad across the
frontiers of his person. Apropos of the Gee Gees,
I hear, on the very best authority—the manager’s
—that the co. is only to appear in Melbourne
and Sydney, and anxious enquirers from the other
Australasian centres are hereby informed that it’s
no use saving up their pocket-money for a show
that will never come. After Melbourne and
Sydney are squeezed dry the triumphant co.
either goes to Japan or straight back to England.
Sydney Criterion is the prettiest theatre, and
the best adapted for comedy, in Sydney—though
the performers do seem to rather loom over you
after the longer perspective of the larger play-
houses. There was only one fault in front on
Saturday night, and that was that the electric
points were kept full-cock when the curtain was
up. Although this sort of thing is very gratifying
to the woman with diamonds on, it detracts from
the brilliancy of the stage-picture. Quite the
feature of the Elton revival of “The Guv’nor”
was the orchestral music, under David Cope, who
received an encore for the march-past before the
last act. Mr. Cope’s interpolation in the overture
of what used to be “ Elton’s music ” in the “ Old
Guard,” in the days when Mr. E. was funny-man-
in-chief to the Firm, was a master-stroke, and
quite warmed the house for the star.
“ The Guv’nor” was so picturesquely put on
that it didn’t matter a row of pins how the ladies
dressed—the scenery always went one better.
Harrie Ireland, of the Bernhardt locks, suggested
sackcloth and ashes in the first act; but her other
gown of black silk with a Queen Anne horse—no,
1 mean a collar—of black satin fastened with a
huge paste buckle was exceedingly emotional—
only rather more suggestive of Mary Queen of
Scots in a tumbril than the frisky young spouse of
an elderly comedy-husband. Pretty Madge Cor-
coran wore a cheerful pink-and-white peppermint-
lolly sort of frock in the first act and a maize
gown in the last, while Blanche Carlyle’s cham-
pion effort was a well-fitting, drab-colored turn-
out in which nobody could help missing her in a
dust-storm. A stage debutante, so far as I am
concerned, was Miss Josie Kelso, who played the
chambermaid in natty cap and apron. Miss
Kelso is a very plump mummeress and good-look-
ing to boot, which is probably the reason why she
never-turns her back on the audience.
Apropos of the Governor—l mean the rea*
Governor Hampden—the happiest augury up to
date is that the Tocumwal (N.S.W.) Jockey Club
has already named a racehorse after him. Thus
his approaching Ex. is metaphorically taken to the
bosom of Tocumwal, which may be the key to the
vice-regal position, for all you know. I haven’t
heard of any iS.A. racehorse being christened
“ Buxton,” yet—though the name seems peculiarly
appropriate for some wild-eyed, rat-tailed neddy
with a hump in its back, don’t you think ? Gov.
Buxton has been farewelled in London, I read,
and it has been ponderously remarked that Sir
Fowell’s administrative capacity, and his loyalty,
“ would tend to solidify the connection of the
colonies with the mother country.” Tough Fowl
evidently intends to govern everybody’s head off.
“Solidify the connection,” too, is depressing
language. It sounds as if a state plumber were
being exported from ’Ome.
But this is the sort of neat-and-tasty thing
which really does more towards another strand in
those silken bonds, and encourages the deeper dye
in the crimson thread of kinship, than all the
“administrative” Governors in a bunch. It
comes from Maori land, and is to the brief effect
that the Princess of Wales has ordered two dress-
lengths of Kaiapoi tweed —Kaiapoi being the
place where they make the loveliest and cheapest
all-wool rugs I have ever seen. That, I repeat, is
the sort of invidious overt act which makes every
tweed-faker a most loyal person. When the Royal
Family import all their cabbages from Victoria—
their oranges from N.S.W.-, their bananas from
Bananaland, and their chaplains from Adelaide—
the Democratic orator will find his occupation
gone.
Apropos of locally-manufactured dress-stuffs, a
very interesting article on “Dangerous Dress
Materials ” appeared in a Sydney daily, the other
day, setting forth the peril that lies hidden in the
folds of the flannelette wrapper, “ this style only
four-and-elevenpence-three-farthings,” which so
many women wear o’ mornings when they’re coax-
ing the fire to light with kerosene. “Few people
have any conception of the readiness with which
this substance will ignite,” says the article referred
to, owing to the “fluffy or feathery nap of its sur-
face.” This, it appears, blazes like dry chips at
the least encouragement, and “ is so combustible
as to be almost explosive.” Quite a nice Xmas
present of drapery for your mother-in-law, evi-
dently. The more cottony the fabric the more
danger of its igniting. Altogether, anybody who
in the land of wool makes flannelette after this
notice ought to be found guilty of arson in the
first degree.

My Melbourne correspondent: Between us, the
conversazione given by the W. J. Clarkes at Clive-
den was not a success. Both the members of the
Salon and the members of VAlliance Francaise
were invited ; and, though the Salcnites lay claim
on all the local “culchaw,” few (if any)
of them are regarded as “society” folk.
On the other hand, the Alliance people
may not be intellectual, or know anything
whatever of parley-voos, but they are smart.
Consequently, the two “ pushes ” don’t blend, so
all the evening the Salonites wished they were as
“smart ” as the Alliance and the Alliance wished
that it knew as much French as the Salon, and
there was no health in the affair. Besides, what
■on earth is the good of spending much money on
a new gown and tricking oneself out in gauds and
■otherwise undergoing torture when there is not a
single man to behold one ? The few men present
were not single; they were mostly greybeards,
and weren’t permitted to behold anything but
their own large wives, who clung to them fiercely
all the evening.
The musical programme was excellent, nearly
all the singers being professionals. Miss Maggie
Stirling, who might pass for a handsome copy of
the Countess of Hopetoun, is about the best true
mezzo-soprano Melb. possesses; Miss Regina
Nagel, Mine. Steinhauer’s latest discovery, has a
most promising voice, with about twice the power
of Ada Crossley’s, and a timbre unlike anyone
else’s; Miss Laura Fisher, a tall and stately
young Maori lander, another fine cdtatralto, sang
charmingly. The supper was as good as the
music,’ and on a still more lavish scale.
Mr. Purvea’ lecture to the law-students, legal
fraternity, and friends, received with uproarious
approval at the time, has since encountered much
hostile criticism, on the ground of its alleged
“coarseness.” Knowing ones say that the audi-
ence went home in so high a state of chuckle that
their womankind never rested till they had
wormed out all the Q.C.’s spiciest stories, only to
ruffle their feathers as soon as their curiosity was
satisfied, and set up a shrill, infectious cackle of
disgust. Certainly, the lecturer’s reminiscences
of Bench and Bar were gay enough—so much so,
indeed, that even the diluted, second-hand version
pune as a shock to the mammas of law-students.
These good ladies, ’tis said, have been doleful
ever since, lamenting the “awful temptations,”
unsuspected till now, awaiting their “poor, dear
boys.”
An Australienne in England : “I spent a day
with Mrs. Haweis at her home, Queen’s House,
Chelsea,- where Dante Gabriel Rossetti used to
live. You may imagine what a joyful thing the
drawing-room is—panelled with unpolished oak,
the celling of gold with a dull cinnamon band all
round the frieze. On an exquisite stained-glass
window languishes one of Rossetti’s damosels.
The room is full of books, pictures and statues,
&c. No trivialities. They are quite ‘ out ’in
smart drawing-rooms, so please burn bulrushes,
fans, photos., Jap. figures and all ‘sic havers ’ (as
the Scotch call them).
“ I met Mrs. Mannington Caffyn, whom success
has in no way spoilt. She was moaning about the
expensiyeness of living in London. Australia is
much cheaper, she assures me, now that house--
rent is down.
“There is an affair on at the Col. Institute to
which I am bidden, but I won’t go. Too slow—
everything given in that pile is deadly save for
the orchestra. There is always splendid music
there. I heard Dan Godfrey’s band play ‘ Marche
Lorraine,’ at the Institute. If you should by
chance hear of its being played within 100 miles,
go to hear it or you will be sorry. It has in it
‘ Just look at my Sabots’ (evidently an old French
air), wi(;h the acoent different from the song.”

girl who started costume-building a year or two
back is doing such a roaring business that her
head woman is getting .£3OO a year, and she keeps
about 40 girls employed all through the season.
Yours affectionately, Sappho Smith.

Extract from a letter by one of the
massacred missionaries: “The head catechist of
the district, Lung Sing Mi, is such a devoted,
simple-hearted Christian of many years standing.
I would ask you to remember him in your prayers.
The Lord has seen well to deal with him in what
one might think a strange way, in taking from
hjm one by one all his children except one little
girl.” Perhaps the Lord forgot to remind Lung
Sing Mi that his drainage was unsatisfactory.
The action against Huddart, Parker and
Co. by Mrs. Kilpatrick, widow of an engineer
who went down with the Alert, promises to be an
awful satire on trial by jury, Mrs. Kilpatrick in
the first instance got £6OO damages from a Melb.
jury. This verdict was set aside on appeal to the
Full Court. The widow is now struggling to raise
means to start afresh, and she applied last week
for a postponement of the new trial until next
sessions. Her application was granted, although
Huddart, Parkers counsel made out that any
postponement would be unnecessary cruelty, inas-
much as Mrs. Kilpatrick can’t possibly succeed,
after the Full Court finding, and ought to be
wiped out without more ado. The case is well
worth watching. If Mrs. Kilpatrick couldn’t get
damages from the court in which she won her
case, what was the use of trying it, anyway? And
if she can't win another case, after the Full Court’s
decision, why is the unfortunate woman permitted
to wear out her body and soul in further legal
proceedings? The particular Judge who con-
sidered the matter last week thought it was pos-
sible that further evidence might be obtained. If
that learned man can suggest any kind of evidence
capable of getting over the law laid down by the
Full Court (and which the same tribunal would
laydown again if a second jury awarded damages
to the plaintiff) The Bulletin will buy him a
new wig.
F. : Yic. general municipal-elections take
place on 22nd Aug. next, when the ratepayers will
have a chance of saying a lot of things to candi-
dates. The sleek and oily person who downs the
minimum wage and anti-subletting principles
every year will come forward with an oleaginous
smile to indicate that he has done faithfully and
well for the workers of his constituency in the
past, and will do so some more if returned. Then
a seedy Socialist at the back of the hall will ask,
in a loud voice, if the candidate has not ground
the faces of the poor, and sweated the helpless.
Two large policemen will thereupon run in the
seedy Socialist on a charge of blasphemy, and a
motion of confidence in the candidate will be
carried with acclamation.
Thanks chiefly to the exertions of mem-
ber Pratt, Yic. Legislative Council rejected the
flogging clause proposed by Cooke, M.L.C.,
as an amendment to the Gaols Act. From
a return it appeared that prison-visiting jus-
tices already had power fo award terms of im-
prisonment extending sometimes to 18 months,
with plentiful doses of solitary confinement. It
was generally agreed that this power is excessive,
it being, as regards “solitary,” beyond that ever
exercised by Supreme Court Judges—not one of
whom, said Solicitor-General Cuthbert, had ever
been known to give more than a fortnight’s soli-
tary, while the prison justices frequently awarded
a month.

round the waist —an increase of 4in. since becoming
Premier.

be 44, and Purves, Q.C. (V.), will be 51, on

Premier John Forrest (W.A.) will be 48
on Aug. 22. His Ministry is the oldest on the
Continent.

A Vic. millionaire, perhaps the biggest
ready-money man of the province, is suffering
from the delusion that he is a pauper.
Viscount Suirdale, the 20-year-old heir of
the Earl of Donoughmore (who married a Tas-
manian beauty, Miss Stephens), is a passenger by
the Orient.
David Syme exports several hundred cases
of choice apples to Scotland every year. He has
an apple-orchard, covering close upon 20 acres, at
Worri-Yallock (Vic.).

Alice Pascoe, a Mac Donnell Bay (S.A.)
widow, was a successful plaintiff at Cornwall
County Assizes recently in an action which
practically establishes her claim to an estate in
Chancery worth .£2,500,000. She goes to Eng-
land immediately.
William Lyon, the sturdy old Chartist,
who, in a tit of homicidal mania, killed his wife at
Wellington (S.A.) on May 25, died in Parkside
lunatic-asylum Aug. 1. One of his last messages
was “love to his wife,” of whose death he
seemed completely ignorant.

The latest photo, of the new S. A. Governor
Buxton accentuates previous impressions of that
fuzzy-haired brewer. His face is a cross between
Dr. Jefferis and Charles Bradlaugh, and one
instinctively glances round for a collection-plate
and a table—and there the Bible is on the table,
with the subject’s hand reverently grasping it.
History repeats itself. An old colonist
states that during the excitement attendant on
the first election contest under constitutional
govt, in S.A., George Kingston, father of the
present Premier, thrashed Sparks, senior, an
aggressive Yankee, for dragging the Union Jack
in a Hindley-street gutter. That was early in
1857.

Melb. University has re-appointed Prof.
“ Martial ” Hall holder of the chair of music
for an indefinite period “on good behavior.” The
addendum, it is said, has been inserted at the
instigation of local musical critics. The Professor
is expected to conduct himself as well as his
orchestra at a reduced screw—.£Boo per an. instead
of £lOOO.
Dr. J. E. Neild and Dr. James Jamieson
own the most interesting private libraries in Mel-
bourne. Neild’s special strength lies in early
Vic, newspapers and in Shakespeares, while
Jamieson has a magnificent lot of “first editions.”
Both have excellent collections of Australian first
editions, as also has Dr. “Pat” Maloney, of
Coll ins-street.
James Blanksby, of Newcastle, one of the
Reid batch of Ms.L.C., once ran a little journal
in Sydney, and had hopes of moving a large part
of the world with his lever. He had at that time
enthusiasm enough for several, and energy that
made its own work. He had also faith. “This
spread,” lie said to the writer one night, waving a
hand towards “groaning” tables for the frail out-
casts of Sydney, “will stand me in quite .£l5,
and I haven’t a penny. But it’ll come.” And,
before the writer left, a note came to hand from a
rich man (Kbenezer Vickery), stating, “ What-
ever the expense of this night’s entertainment is,
charge it to me,”

after he was made Acting-Governor, he entered
the Governor’s pew at Toorak Presbyterian
Church. The old verger at once pounced on him :
“Coomoobof thot, mon, that’s for the Gov’nor
ainly.” And H.E. had to give a satisfactory ex-
planation forthwith.

tracted beleaguerment. A few years back the

without renovation for Heaven knows how long.

Lecturing recently at Adelaide Democratic
Club, Premier Kingston got stuck through not
being able to read his memoranda, which were in
his own writing. After the meeting, the Register
reporter asked him for his notes, and the Premier,
who has been quarrelling with that paper, had
delicious revenge. The only readable epistle
Kingston ever wrote was, it is averred, the lette*
he sent to Chaffey Baker containing the famous
challenge to fight a duel.
Jack Want, predominant partner in the
Reid Ministry, belongs to a school almost extinot
—the old Sydney “ cabbage tree mob.” They
were men of large stature, large ideas, large pas-
sions and large appetites ; strong, hungry, lusty,*
brainy men, who spoke, and still speak, the argot
of old Sydney. Of this type, Dibbs and Want
remain to astonish the country with a bygone
slang interspersed through debating speeches.
The son of a well-known
Maoriland politician became bank*
rupt some years ago, mainly
through backing a bill for his
father. It was not, as he after-
wards explained, his father’s failure
to pay which hurt his feelings,
but the soulless levity of the old
man. The bill was for a larg*
amount, and had been signed undei
extreme pressure. When it was
nearly due the young man began to
walk about at nights thinking
over it, and at length he called on
his progenitor and laid the matter
before him. “ Look here, father, 6 ’
he said, “the long and the short
of it is, if you don’t pay that bill
I’ll have to go insolvent. Now,
can you pay it?” “My son,”
said the patriarch, placidly;
“You’ll just have to go insol-
vent. I hope this will be a warn-
ing to you never to back bills in
the future, I thought, with all my
careful training, you would hav«
known better, but it seems that
you can only learn by painful ex-
perience. Good morning.” And
the son found himself gently
shown out, while the good old man
wend back to his books. That’*
why theold man and his son don’t
speak now.
Mr. Alex. Kennedy Beve-
ridge, P.M. at Bega, N.S. W., who
died the other day- after a long
lilness, the result of a lung being
rendered useless by a broken rib,
belonged to a well-known Scottish
family and was originally a British
cavalryman. After acting as a
Japanese army-instructor, became
to Australia and took a police
captaincy, whence lie drifted into the magistracy.
Mr. Beveridge was an ornament to the Bench,
and in all relations of life an ideal gentleman.
“In the case of two of the Melb. girls'
murdered in China, the whole cause of then
going,” writes a well-informed Bulletin cor-
respondent, “ was that they were affected almost
to hysteria by a ranting evangelist down in Mel-
bourne, The excitement was kept up by another
devil-dodger of the same type, with the result as
per cable the other day.”
Mrs. Campbell-Praed, in her novel, “Mis.
Tregaskis,’’ now meandering in the Age, speaks
of the unionist shearers “fighting for freedom of
contract.” She also describes the carriers as cart-
ing their families about with them, the wife and
children living in a tent pitched on top of the load
of wool. Residence abroad always improves one’s
insight into Australian morals and manners.
George L. Lukin (brother of Gresley
Lukin), for years police-magistrate at Gympie
and Rockhampton, and latterly at Maryborough,
purposes retiring on a pension. Friends credit
him with an intention to enter Q. Parliament.
From G.L.’s previous tussles with authority, it is
expected that in the House —if he gets there—he
will be generally “ agin ” the Government.
Mrs. Mood, who died at Parramatta last
week, had for 70 years resided there, having
arrived, when only 22 years of age, as matron of
the female convicts’ “factory.” She claimed close
relationship with Geo. IV. It would have been
to the pecuniary advantage of a Parramatta busi-
ness man had the old lady died years ago, as he
purchased her property with an annuity, and lost
about 150 per cent, (not including interest calcula-
tions) on the transaction.
Vogel as a speaker revelled in finance.
He never missed an opportunity of talking mil-
lions, and when he got into seven figures he wa*
unction and impressiveness itself. In his last
M.L. budget speech he fairly waded in millions,
and the manner in which he wound up with—
“ And the entire wealth of the colony is one-
hundred—and—twelve mill-ions,” lingering lov-
ingly over each syllable—was up to date the most
impressive sample of oratory ever heard in the
Land of the Moa.
In a recent S. A. Assembly debate, Opposi-
tion member Howe accused Kingston of dragging
Parliament down to the lowest depths of degri*-
dation, and prophesied all sorts of evil if the
House did not rise and fling off the incubus.
Kingston (loq.): “The hon. member for Glad-
stone predicts that the Ministry will sink, sink,
sink like a Japanese diver. Well, like all other
bad eggs, the hon. member will float, float, float,
and similarly age will only increase his buoyancy
and malodorousuess.”
A Melb. land magnate, who died some
five years back, when he became a “ gentleman
commissioned a popular artist to paint his por-
trait. After several sittings a flattering (profile)
likeness of Michael, minus ten years, duly arrived
at his stucco mansion. Mickey, however, refused
to pay, and was haled before the county court,
where he pleaded that the artist had painted him
with “ wan oye,” and he wasn’t going to throw
away his hard-earned cash on half a likeness.’
The court decided for plaintiff, and Mickey com-
pletely “ swore off ” artists.

At the Man and the Camera blended !
The Bull with the focussing cloth got wild ;
And the Artist’s career was ended.
The moral’s as plain as the moon at its full—
Don’t put up your Sticks till you’re sure there’s no Bull.

POLITICAL POINTS
The Boodle-Rath Queensland Legisla-
tive Assembly.
Mr. Blatiksby, M.L.C. (N.S.W.) But
why not Mr. Sneezby, M.L.C.?
Federal Enabling Bills have been intro-
duced into the 8. A. and Tas. Parliaments.
Premier Reid is merely ex-Premier Parkes
—Bowdlerised and with his hair cut; emasculated
and bald ; chubby and glass-eyed. Parkes is Reid,
leonine and rampant.
The Sparks assault episode has made
Kingston’s seat for West Adelaide absolutely
safe; whereas had he been whaled, his election
path would have been stormy. This is a queer
world.
Over Premier Turner, now in the zenith
of his popularity, a dark cloud impends. The
Argus has taken to blessing him, and, to a public
man, the benison of the Argus is “ fatal as cat’s
breath to baby.”
Murray Smith, M.L.A, (Vic.) : “You can
no more make a country rich by Protection than
you can make water run uphill.” Rogers, M.L. A. :
“You can make water run uphill. I’ve seen a
Chinaman do it.”
The Bulcock circular issued by the Queens-
land National Ass. (Nelson’s pet organisation) was
entitled, “How to Purify the Rolls.” At the
bottom was inscribed the legend, “ To be detached
and destroyed.” Suggestive!
The Wise-Parkes letter, read at the Wise
banquet, Sydney, last week, was published in full
by the S.M. Herald, but wholly suppressed by
the Daily Telegraph. The letter revealed
Premier Reid as his closest political friends know
him.
Illiteracy and loyalty again. The most
unlettered man in a certain Australian Parliament
was the lately-deceased A . It was touching
to hear him talking about his respect for the
Governor and the Governess (i.e., the Governor’s
wife).
South Australia, which has just practically
abolished its militia in the interests of economy,
grows very tired of the annual payment of £lO,OOO
towards the expenses of the Australian squadron,
more especially as the ships are so seldom seen in
local waters.
A strange feature of
Vic. civil - service re-
trenchment is the hazi-
ness which many old
fogeys seem to have about
their own age. A man
on entering the service
always endeavors to
make himself as old as
possible in order to be
senior to bis mates. But
when the Act was passed
making retirement com-
pulsory at 60 years, a
good many officers found
that they had made a
mistake in stating their
ages, and instead of being
59 they were only 56.
One venerable gentle-
man, who looked at least
70, lately wanted to
amend his birthday and
make himself 43, or so,
but the tyrannical Board
wouldn’t dream of it.
“Beal prosperity,
such as will reduce the
workless thousands to
hundreds,” writes the
eternal William Mainer
(who is he?), in S.M.
Herald. The sort of
mind that can contem-
plate hundreds - of men
going about starving, in
the vain search for em-
ployment, and call it
“real prosperity,” ought
to be labelled and sent to
a psychological museum.
Of course, everyone
guesses what is meant.
Just a sufficient number
of unemployed to play
olf against labor, but
not enough to become a
source of anxiety to the
plutocracy. Enough to
jostle other working men
for bread, but not the big
number that would take
the cake.
“ Mr. Deakin is a
very young man. He
always will be.”— Melb.
paper.
Zeal, grand Panjandrum of Yic. Leg.
Council, opines that, “notwithstanding the de-
ficit, if our railways were let to commercial men,
I believe they would pay well.” (Applause.) No
doubt. Highway robbery is usually profitable
while it lasts.
The Vic. Executive will shortly consider
the advisableness of retaining in custody the con-
victed Abrahams brothers, who are allegedly very
ill. The spectacle of Premier Turner’s Govt,
sitting in judgment on perhaps the best lot of
paying clients its chief ever had will be a truly
edifying one.
A substantial testimonial is being raised
,for “Jim Morgan,” late M.P. for Dubbo, by his
local supporters. He got beaten at the election
mainly by Ins own poverty—and makes no secret
of the fact that he embarked in the fight with .£3
odd in his pocket.
Under the old Vic. tariff, the local
distiller paid 4s. per gallon less duty than the im-
porter, and flourished considerably. The new
tariff protects him to the extent of from Is. to 3s.
only, and'whether the distilling industry can
stand the shock is doubtful.
In 1888, Sam Griffith pointed out that the
then surplus of .£116,000 in Queensland was con-
tributed by the North, and calmly abstracted and
absorbed by the South. Things have mended
since then, say the Southerners. Here are the
“ mended ” statistics : Taxation per head—<£4 12s.
for the North, <£4 for the Centre, .£1 17s. for the
South.
“I thank God I am able to supply you
with work,” said Premier Reid to a deputation
of pipe-moulders. G. H. R. always closes with a
benediction nowadays, and The Bulletin hereby
requests him not to be an objectionable snuffler,
but to stick closely to business. It doesn’t look
well to see a fat gentleman thanking God through
an eyeglass.
Regulations to limit the proportion of im-
provers and boys to tradesmen are badly wanted
in Vic. Govt, contracts. The minimum-wage
provisions are evaded by the simple device of em-
ploying, as improvers or boys, workmen forced to
accept less than minimum wages. The latter (im-
provers or boys) are exempt from the operations
of the principle.
The Labor League at Junee (N.S. W.),
which gave its enthusiastic support to Arthur
Rae, the Freetrader, at the late election, has
passed a resolution demanding the retention of
the present duties on agricultural produce. Junee
is an agricultural district, and the local Free-
trader wants Protection for his own industry, as
usual. They are all built that way.
The Maoriland Referendum Bill passed its
second reading in the House of Representatives by
a two-to-one vote, despite the almost open hos-
tility of Premier Seddon. Seddon’s objection to
the Referendum is almost as violent as his objec-
tion to the idea of an elective Ministry. The
present system made him a Premier, and a system
that does that is quite good enough for the burly
ex-storekeeper from the West Coast.

facturing hair-restorers ” placed on the free-list,
Staughton asked the chairman whether he, as a
bald-headed man, could vote on the motion. The
House roared. Then the hairless Gurr opposed
the motion on the ground that baldness was a
desirable state and should be encouraged, and
the House shrieked. Finally Zox got off the

M‘Ellume’s electorate (Fitzroy, Sydney)
comprises Potts Point and a good slice of Wool-
loomooloo—wherein the principal public building
is the Fish Market. For Billingsgate, read
Woolloomooloo. Also, Potts Point is only Wool-
lonmooloo writ largo. In fact, Potts Point is
Woolloomooloo in the second generation, just as
Darling Point is Cockatoo in the third.
