A GREAT HERO .
(A fragment of a stump-speech on football.)
“ With harrarkers to de right ob ' im; barrackers to de left oh 'im;
followers to de front oh ’im; an’ sneaks to de rear oh ’im—
hollerin’ an’ bellerin. Theirs not to make reply; theirs
not to reason why; but hand up de ball at de whistle ob
de noble umpire.”
Parliament, it is alleged, holds the written resig-
nation of each one of its members in safe keeping
to be forwarded to the Speaker, without consulting
either the member or his constituency, if he
should offend the majority or break away from
the fold. Does Premier Reid intend to apply a
modification of the solidarity pledge to his new
Council appointments?
The ballot is nominally secret, but it’s not
very secret on those stations where there is a poll-
ing-booth presided over by the overseer with his
jackeroo as poll-clerk. “ All the men on the
station,” writes a Bulletin correspondent, “vote
as understood beforehand. They are watched
carefully if there is any doubt about them, and if
the count doesn’t come out according to calcula-
tions ‘ the sack’ is the prescribed remedy. As
there are only a few strange voters at these places
it is easy to tell how the station votes.”
A candidate and his trusted henchman
awoke one morning at the principal hotel of
Heifer Flat, N.S. VV., and called for whisky and
milk. They were told there was plenty of whisky
but no milk, as there were no cows on the Flat.
“No cows!” said the henchman, “then tell the
inhabitants that if Mr. X. gets in he will have a
sum of money at once placed on the estimates for
the purchase of a public cow for Heifer Flat.”
He got every vote there except one, that of a man
who wanted a sum also placed on the estimates
for public whisky.
Sydney D.T., which seems to have taken
a Boodlewraith retainer, upbraids Michael Davitb
for promising to enquire into the case for Q.
separation. It urges the refreshingly naive argu-
ment that, if there was any case, Q. Parliament
would have petitioned long ago. Bless the
D.T.’s innocent little Japanesy heart! It doesn’t
know that? the Southern members in Q. Parlia-
ment form about a two-thirds majority ; that the
revenue surpluses of the Centre and North are
annually milked to makegood the chronic deficits
of the South ; and that as long as separation
means a big cash loss and no-separation a direct
cash profit, it is as hopeless to expect the Southern
majority to pass a Separation Bill as it is to expect
a usurer to petition Parliament for a bill to reduce
the rate of interest.
Says Sydney Telegraph :
Taken in the larjfe, the new N.S.W. Legislative Coun-
cillors are a severely unobjectionable lot. From every
pore they exude right-thinkingness, and the ten white
flowers of their ten blameless lives Will, on the opening of
Parliament, appear in the Council as a bouquet of fresh-
pulled politieal lilies. Many of them have been raised
from the political grave, where they lay like warriors
taking their rest, with their martial cloaks around them,
or words to that effect. Others had wandered away and
died in the political hush without anybody having missed
them until Mr. Reid stumbled over th.ir political skele-
tons. For all these it is a glorious resurrection. Another
some have nervously shied at the ballot-box, and got a
quiet leg-up while the electors were not about.
To which it may be added that every one of the
ten wears a beiltopper. Hardly one of them is in
himself “ toney ” enough to be able to dispense
with His beiltopper. Which in one act ends their
reputation as democrats —of the sort that Reid
promised to appoint, or denounced his enemies for
not appointing.

The N.S.W. Ministry
may have reckoned without
its host in its Leg. Council
appointments. Dan. O’Con-
nor, for one, is known to be
not unfriendly to the Na-
tional Ass, and got a lot of

The champion humorist
of the N.S. W. elections was
a man who stood for Denili-
quin and district in the in-
terests of general reform.
He proposed to swoop down
upon the Legislature with
a Bill for the manufacture
of a 500-mile canal to con-
nect the Murray and the
Darling, from Moama to
Wilcannia. His idea was to
work the canal at low rates,
in order to do the railways
badly, and make a splendid
profit for the enterprise by
planting mahogany trees at
brief intervals on both
sides of the waterway. The
yield of costly timber would,
by the candidate’s calcula-
tion, pay the interest on
’Enery Parkes’s national
debt, and the foliage of the
trees (after they had been
cut down and converted into
furniture) would afford
delightful shade for mariners
cruising between rivers.
This genius polled eight
votes, including three from
grateful Hindoos. He inter-
prets the Hindoo tongue,
and to this devoted trio he
unfolded his policy in a
speech that nearly turned
’em pale.

MERIT,
HONESTY,
When any article is found in use in all parts of
the civilised world, it is proof positive that such
an article is necessary and does its work well and
effectively.
Especially is this true when the article is only
purchased because the buyer believes it is not wise
to longer do without it. Suoh an article is
WARNER’S SaFE CURB.
A remedy that can be found in the bazaars of
India, at the fairs of Russia, on the Equator at
Singapore, in the far North at Stockholm, under
the Southern Cross of Australia, and in every city
and hamlet in Europe, Canada and the Uuited
States, must possess remarkable power for the
healing of the nations.
WARNER’S SAFE CURE
is a medicine with history. It has revolutionised
the treatment of Bright’s disease, and to-day
stands without an equal for the cure of all kidney,
liver, urinary and female diseases. The inhabi-
tants of the civilised world say so.
This universal remedy is now controlled entirely
by H. H. Warner and Co. Ltd., of London,
England.
Offices and laboratories are established at Lou-
don, Eng. ; Rochester, N. V. ; Melbourne, Aus. ;
Toronto, Ont. ; Paris, France; Frankfort, Ger-
many ; Dunedin, N. Z"; Kreuslingen, Switzer-
land ; Bombay,’lndia.
You make no mistake in buying and using a
medicine which bears the stamp of the world’s
approval.
EPITAPH.
COn a certain eminent statesman lately deceasedJ.
He sought our land, a prince of roaring Rada.,
Possessed and puffed with fifty furious fads;
Deep down he dived, a Legislative whale,
And banged a myriad minnows with his tail.
In youth a Chartist bold—mature, a Tory;
In age a doggerel bard and Premier hoary ;
One t'me he waved aloft the Orange rag
And “Down with Popery ” flew upon his flag ;
One time “Freetrade” the subject of his lyre;
Anon he joined the Pure-Protection choir,
The while with juggling arts and jeering jaw
He smote a listening land with silent awe.
Avowing all he wished was Peace and Rest
He sought the battle with a furious zest;
Protesting ne’er for office did he lust.
For office fighting'fierce he bit the dust.
He fooled his simple country while he could—•
His country left him for his country’s good.
Q.

Sydney Criterion re-opened on Saturday
and looked the brightest, cleanest, healthiest and
least microbe-infested establishment in the city,
Lohr’s new company started well. “ The
Guv’nor ” is a breezy comedy with just enough of
a possible New Woman element in it to suit the
popular taste, and as the suspicion that pretty
Harne Ireland is a New Woman proves wholly
unfounded in the last act, the comedy is as respect-
able as anyone could desire. The plot is simple
enough. An elderly man has a young wife, a
next-door neighbor insists on putting his death’s-
head over the garden wall and telling him about
a strange man who is frequently seen in the
summer-house. Whereupon the husband collects
corroborative evidence from a deaf man who
thinks he is talking about something to drink,
and believes that he sees a chance of selling a
boat, and from a man with a dreadful stutter
whose statements are wholly incomprehensible;
and among them the story becomes about as
shapeless and horrifying as a dog that has been
under a tram-car. There is no moral intended,
unless it be the moral that the man next door is a
ghoul by nature, and a perjurer by habit and
education, and when his clammy skull appears
above the fence it should be hailed witli road-
metal. But Elton, as the deaf boat-builder, is a
flowing river of mirth, and his chuckle is an in-
spiration ; and his call hat, and his hopeless incon-
sequence, and the patient, doggish expression with
which he regards tlie enraged husband, and the
dreadful cordiidity with which he shakes hands
with the people who are cursing him in a loud
voice, and his untimely offers of rum hot, and his
negotiations with the man who the brick in his
speech, are alarmingly funn\.
When one comes to remember
that Hans the Boatman possibly
came to look and chuckle like
that in his old age, all the
poetry vanishes out, of Arnold’s
big-dog drama. But this is a
side issue.
• * *
Westmacott, as an athletic
young man of fortune with a
stutter, is more humorous than
he usually is. His stutter
doesn’t bear the slightest resem-
blance to the real aiticle, but it
is amusing, while the authentic
stutter is just as interesting as
a corn. Miss Maine Ireland is
a lovely and picturesque heroine,
and Frank Emery, as the wicked
old head appearing over the

Still another revival of
“The World Against Her”
eve ituated on Saturday at Her
Majesty’s, Sydney. The man gcinent accom-
panted it with an intimation to the general effect
that a wave of something or other was passing
nver the community, and it was considered advis-
able to endeavor to promote something or other
with a view to combating the affair before men-
tioned and encouraging various things which The
Bulletin can’t remember just now. But the
tendency of the advt. seemed to be that it was
necessary to give virtue a lift at this critical
moment! And with a view to doing this, Mr.
Rignold has deliberately staged a drama in which
a bad man tries to mash a beautiful and virtuous
woman, and her noble husband comes in and bangs
thingsaboutand refusestolisten toany explanation,
and gets a divorce, and throws his wife out, and
sends certain ugly-looking individuals after her,
and makes her life a misery generally. The
obvious moral, through three weepful acts, is that
only a cast-iron woman, who can stand living in a
damp cellar, and going barefoot through the snow,
can afford to live up to her principles, and anyone
whose constitution isn't copper-fastened had better
elope with the rich, bad man at once, provided be
will settlea water-tight house and aspring-mattress
upon her. Misit Maud Williamson is the heroine on
tlie present occasion, and looks very sad and pic-
turesque with her hair down ; and Mr. Scot Inglis
is the bad man who calls on her, and Mr. Plimmer
is the good man who throws her out, and Mr.
Sweehey is the kind, but coarse, man who takes
her in and gives her a baked_ tatur, and they all
help to point the moral that virtue doesn’t pay,
** * *
Further consideration of the “Shop Girl”
only confirms last Week’s impression that it is a
sketchy performance and as thin as the side view
of a New Woman. Moreover, the opinion is
gaining ground that Hooley’s desire to inspect the
ladies’ marks is not altogether respectable, and
the fact that Maud Hobson’s mark is located in
the middle of Iter back doesn’t seem so interest-
ing, after a week’s acquaintance, as it did at first.
The airy fashion in which Bradfield skims over
his part covers most of its deficiencies, but Monk-
house belongs to the hulking variety of humorist,
and when he plunges heavily through his charac-
ter, like a mail-clad knight going over a bog, the
fact that there is very little character becomes
apparent. The “ Shop Girl ” doesn’t seem likely
to develope any new humor as time goes on, and
the old humor is pretty thin, and mostly concerns
Miss Hobson’s back. The rest of it —that part
which doesn’t concern Miss Hobson’s back—is
concentrated in Fred Kaye’s legs, and when Fred
Kaye’s legs are not in evidence and Miss Hobson’s
back has dropped out of sight, there is very little
left. Monkhouse gets a “farewell matinee per-
formance ” next Wednesday.
** * *
Several more “ unrivalled artists ” have
lately joined the two Sydney variety houses.
Harry Shine lifts up a fine, well-trained voice at
theTiyoli; and Miss Ada Colley, who is vastly
superior to the ordinary variety person, lias re-
turned to the Empire, and helps to keep it out of
the desolate rut of the nigger and the Irish come-
dian. The Bulletin takes this opportunity to
remark that the nigger with his dreary yarn about
the stolen chicken, and tlie Irish comedian with
his wailful song about tlie Bridget he left behind
him, ought to be about extinct by this time.
** * *
A movement is on foot to give Mr. Rig-
nold a large and enthusiastic benefit before he
leaves for England. If only for the sake of the
scenery which he has placed before the public he
deserves the largest thing of the kind which can
be put together.
** * *
The juvenile variety troupes that ape their
elders around the Melb. suburbs are undesirable
things in all respects. The best of them gives a
sickening show at the prices—3d. and 6d. There’s
no money in it for the performers or their parents.
An audience of IGO people, exclusive of deadheads,
means about S(K. which has to pay rent of hall,
advertising and advance agent, and a toffy
pianist, before the 10 “bloomin’ artists” get a
w
ELTON, as the Guv' nor, at Sydney Criterion.
look in. Last, but not least, there’s the manager
who takes the money, and who seems to live
pretty comfortably over the business. The chil-
dren can’t get much out of it, beyond a bad train-
ing. Two of the girls in one of these troupes
have voices that could be made valuable if they
were captured by some competent teacher, and
taken out of harm’s way immediately. At pre-
sent they seem to be educating themselves in the
worst form of music-hall twang, and will soon get
beyond redemption.
*# * *
“The Importance of Being Earnest,”
which was staged at Melbourne Princess’s on
Saturday, suggests that Oscar Wilde’s characters
grew brighter and lost some of their high-toned
boredom as the shadow of the imminent gaol
began to fall upon their author. Some of them
are reasonably young, whereas in “ Lady Winder-
mere ”■ they were all very old and haggard in their
minds, and several of them have a certain fresh-
))ess which was conspicuously absent in the other
crowd. None of Wilde’s new crowd of people
look real, but they probably were not intended to;
they all live in an atmosphere of high-pressure
epigram, and . the trail of an unearthly smart-
ness is over them. But none of them have an
objectionable past, and they don’t commit any
notable sins, and their brilliance is amusing and
harmless. The title seems to have been thrown
j n promiscuously, as it were, for no one is earnest,
or even pretends to be, and no one professes to
the importance of anything. Ward and
Boucicault, and the pretty Miss Beryl Faber, sup-
port the most of this pleasant butterfly drama,
which is to hold the stage until Friday,
Then there will be a spell of the “ Village Priest,”
and the season closes with another attack of
“ Mrs. Tanqueray.”

Miss Elizabeth Watson, the sister of Aus-
tralia’s lamented Henrietta, made her first appear-
ance at Melbourne Royal on Saturday, and was
accepted with enthusiasm, partly for her own
sweet sake and partly for that of Henrietta afore-
mentioned. The new drama, “Saved from the
Sea,” is not, however, a specially striking effort.
It belongs to the ordinary, bulky variety of
horror, in which the raw, red villain is pre-
dominant, and the hero is always being sent to
R a( d f° r enormous periods on no sufficient
evidence, and the simple heroine is heiress to a
stupendous fortune, and a very large and crude
conspiracy is blown up at last on the unsupported
evidence of somebody whose testimony is obviously
wort hless. Also the two villains bungle as dread-
fully as usual. Which is saying a good deal, for
average melodramaHe viHam emit walk
round a log without toi-iug hiumeil. ihe play
relies mostly on Walter Baker and the attractive
Mias Watson, though several dozen other charac-
ters get in each other’s way a good deal and
block the view of some really line scenery.

Marshall Hall would not commence the programme at
Johnnie Kruse’s (first) concert until everybody had
slopped breathing; and alternated tapping his baton
with gazing severely at the audience. The infidels stood
it for five minutes, and t hen laughed loudly and rudely.
After that no further delay attended the production of
Siegfried.
Last mail brings news of the death of Chas. Harding,
the Australian tenor, in England. A rough, strident
vocalist, with the gift of a voice, his lack of cultivation
shut him from the first rank. He sang in Australia with
Miss Leaf and Miss Plaisted. Aged 49. He was a manly
man and had many friends.
Louis Lohr has taKen a lease of Melb. Bijou, and will
run it in connection with Sydney Criterion, after the
manner of B. and B. in the brave days of old.
One of the rules and regulations attached to contracts
with Harry Rickards gets broken occasionally—“ All
members of the co. must conduct themselves in a be-
coming manner, both off and on the stage, as much
depends upon their ladylike and gentlemanly deportment
in private and public.”
The London newspaper men, if they had read “ Dorian
Gray,” were magnanimous in their notices of Oscar
Wilde’s plays. Dorian speaks of a little Hebrew manager
who complained "that all the dramatic critics were in a
conspiracy against him, and that they were all to he
bought.” To which Dorian’s evil genius replies—“ I
believe he was quite right theie. But, on the other hand,
most of them are not at all expensive.”
Melb. audiences have a keen scent for local allusions.
An enthusiastic “push” from Collingwood way strayed
into the Royal, the other night. When Baker, in the
third act of "A Sailor’s Knot,” delivered Ids gallery
speech about the glory “of Nelson and Collingwood,” the
“ push ” applauded vigorously. If Bland can work in a
reference to Geelong in his next piece its success is
assured.
At the Polygorama entertainment given in Melb. Town
Hall, views of the South Seas were being thrown on the
screen. Seven pictures were displayed at a time, and a
rev. lecturer orated on each in turn. Suddenly a slide
was shown which would have been a good illustration for
the suggested part, of Louis Becke’s “A Ponapea.n Con-
venanoe." The audience laughed—they could because it
was dark and the lecturer said he would not dwell too
long on the scenes as time was pressing, and the lantern
men changed the pictures.
The rivalry between Melb. suburban dramatic com-
panies led to an amusing duel I lie other day between tw o
bill-stickers. It was caused by the usual barnstormer
practice of pasting- over the hills of the rival show. The
men slashed at each other with their brushes; one
emptied his bucket over the other, and then they closed
and fell. No one came near enough to separate them,
because paste was flying in all directions, like mud off a
wheel, but the men left, off when they were tired, and got
up and made for the nearest horse-trough to clean them-
selves. Had the suit been hot tlie pair would have stuck
together;. ,

in Auckland with a big show about the end of September
next.

Sir Edward Malet’s “book” for Fred. Cowen’s new
English opera is a rather feeble.production, William the
Conqueror, for instance, sings :
The courtiers stood at the king’s right hand,
And they told of a beauteous far-off land,
And the king forgot to quaff his wine,
Sighing, “ How, ah ! how can this land be mine ? ”
Cowen’s music, also, is said to lack strength of character.

Dr. Morgan O’Connor, of Wagga, is dead
—a real old Irish gentleman and politician, who
would he alive now had he not, in his 70th year,
sustained a fracture of the hip-bone through a
fall while dancing.
An Anglican church lately ojyened at Cue,
Westralia, is built of rich quartz contributed by
the owners of various local mines. It will he a
good asset in case of winding-up, and will then
present the “Golden Shanty ” in a new phase.
Lawyer Purves fixes a golden rule for the
devil’s younger sons: “Do the least work you
can for the most money.” There are lawyers who
provide another reading—“Do the most men yon
can for all their money,” but they get admonished
by the judges—sometimes.
Near Condobolin, N.S. W., a selector
named Gimblett, aged 23,. returned home and
found that his 19-year-old wife had suicided by
hanging herself with a greenhidestrap. Then the
afflicted husband hanged himself with the same
greenhide strap, and in death they were Hot
divided. They leave a three-month sold baby.
Minnie Dean, baby-farmer and murderess,
was, on Monday last, at Invercargill, M.L.,
strangled by tlie Government man-butcher, after
breakfasting on half-an-ouuce of brandy. She
kept her marvellous nerve to the last, protested
her innocence, and exclaimed, after being tied up,
“ Oh, God ! let me not suffer.”
Southern Cross (W.A.), the place where
Bailey Deeming was captured, has some trouble
on with its Mayor over a cablegram of grovel to
the Queen. The Mayor, who basks under a short
name and four large initials, requested the Council
to settle a bill of <£3 Os. 6d. for “ a cable message
to Her Majesty congratulating her on the 58th
anniversary of her accession.” The councillors
objected, and, when last heard from, the Mayor
was plunging violently about the municipal pre-
mises and using the sort of language which the
average Mayor does use when requested to pay for
his own grovel. Somehow the genuine groveller
always wants someone else to pay for his congra-
tulatory cablegram. If Southern Cross is like its
Mayor the hairy-eared Deeming did well tc out
of that town.
Victoria got to the end of its financial year
with a deficiency of .£114,000, which was about
the amount exp -cted, and by far the best result
achieved since Gillies’ time. The accounts would
have squared if the Fat Men of the Council had
passed the Land Tax Bill, but they didn’t, and,
as there is no means of putting effectual pressure
upon them, Premier Turner accepts the inevitable
for the present. Next year £’250,000 worth of
Shiels’ Treasury bills fall due, and as Turner lias
to liquidate them and also to prevent a renewal of
the present shortage, and has no extra revenue in
view at present, he has manfully hacked another
.£400,000, or so, off the expenditure, and, by this
means, lie hopes to make ends meet. If his esti-
mates come out right, the accumulated deficit will
be reduced by a quarter of a million. Meanwhile,
the Council has gained very little by its rejection
of the proposed new Land-tax ; the only result
has been a heavy reduction in the municipal
subsidy, which means increased taxation for local
government purposes. The more advanced party
in Victoria is beginning to complain that Turner
is a man without democratic instincts, but, at all
events, for an uphill fight with an empty Treasury
he is the most promising person Australia has
produced for a good many years.
Last week, The Bulletin, in alluding to
the election ol Speaker Abbott to the presidency
of N.S.W. Art Society, remarked that “an
organisation which could not keep within its own
ranks any honor which might attach to its presi-
dentship only invited the contempt of the public.”
With reference to this, a correspondent contends
that, “ dealing with the peculiar circumstances of
the society, it cannot be held that any contempt
attaches to the majority of members through the
election of Speaker Abbott. Last year, a majority
professedly in favor of electing artists, and artists
only, to manage and judge, had an excellent
opportunity of carrying its precepts into practice
by electing as president an artist nominated for
the position; but, instead of electing him, or
nominating another artist to oppose him, they put
up an outsider, Mr. Reginald Bluxsome, not an
artist, but a Philistine, and secretary of the Water
and Sewerage Board, who beat Mr. Collingridge
by two votes.” Proceeds this writer: “The
annual smoke-nights of the Art Society were
usually Bohemian affairs ; yet the first under Mr.
Bloxsome had on the card that it was a ‘ dress ’
affair. Now, I confess I haven’t got a diess-snib
—and many other members are, no doubt, similarly
circumstanced. The Art Society, from being a
decent Bohemian organisation, was thenceforth
conducted on “dress-suit” principles, and even
some of the good, old, clever Bohemians, who
theretofore smoked tobacco and wore sac coats,
took (after they sold a picture or two) to cigars
and long coats. Seeing that tlte members pre-
ferred lone —which I myself do not despise—l am
glad to find that, this year, those who last year
supported Collingridge, endeavored to get it, by
putting up a man—an outsider, a Philistine, if
yon will—but one who occupies one of the highest
social positions in the province. I am not per-
sonally influenced by position or title, but, if they
would have an outsider, thought I, let’s give them
a first-class outsider; if they would have tone,
let’s give it them, with plenty of intellect as well.
And so I voted for the Speaker of the Legislative
Assembly in preference to Mr. Reginald Blox-
some.”

THE GOOD SLEEP OF A BAD MAN.
In a certain prison that we all have heard of lay
a convict upon his narrow iron cot. He was to be
hanged the next morning. Yet he lay there,
covered by a rough blanket, sleeping as quietly
and soundly as a tired schoolboy. Occasionally
the guard in the passage outside peered between
the bars of the cell, only to find his charge breath-
ing deeply and regularly. This man had violated
the law prohibiting murder ; yet he had not vio-
lated the physical laws governing his own body,
and nature rewarded him as if he had been the
noblest of his race.
That same night, less than a mile away, a rich
man tossed and tumbled upon his luxurious bed.
He was a good and useful member of society, yet
he could not sleep. And, worse still, this hap-
pened to him every night. Sleep—that blessing
which the Psalmist says, “God giveth his
beloved,” was practically a stranger to this man.
What ailed him? The tortures of conscience?
Want of money ? The fear of enemies ? Nothing
of the sort. Then why didn’t he sleep as well as
the murderer ? You would like to know ? Right,
let us look into the matter.
“I got no sleep at night; I would lie for hours
tossing about. In the morning I was worse tired
than when I went to bed.”
Thus writes Mrs. Eliza Mathews, of 1, North
Road, Burnt Oak, Edgware, near London, under
date of September 22nd, 1892. Just two years
before this time she lost her health. A foul taste
in the mouth, loss of appetite, and great distress
after eating were among the first things she com-
plained of. She craved food at times, and fancied
she could eat heartily, yet when the very dishes
she had asked for were set before her she turned
from them as though they were filth from the
gutter. Her skin grew sallow, her eyes yellow,
and she had a constant pain at her chest, sides,
and between her shoulders. Her bowels were
constipated, and the least exertion set her heart
thumping as if it must jump up into her mouth.
At such times it was as much as ever that she
could get her breath. She got so thin and weak
she was no good for work. She couldn’t walk out
doors without stopping to rest every few rods
almost.
The doctor did what he could for her, all any
doctor could do. At first he said lie thought her
illness was owing to the smell of the farm-yard.
This looked possible. Even the smell of violets
has made strong men turn pale and faint dead
away. Yet the doctor was wrong. If he had
been right, she would have got better when the
family left the farm at Bently Priory and went to
live at Burnt Oak. But she was not improved by
the change of air ; she grew worse and worse.
“In May, 1887,” says Mrs. Mathews, “I went
over to Chlemsford to visit my aunt, Mrs.
Troughton. She told me of the good Mother
Seigel’s Curative Syrup had done her when she
had indigestion and dyspepsia. She bought me a
bottle, and I began taking it. After a few doses I
felt relief, I kept on taking it, and in two months
I was strong and well as ever. My husband and
friends were astonished, yet I assured them that
Seigel’s Syrup had done it. Yours truly, (Signed)
Eliza Mathews.”
The point is plain enough. The convict slept
soundly because he was a healthy man, although
he was a wicked one. Our rich friend rolled about
all night because his nerves were unstrung by the
state of his stomach. Our correspondent was
prostrated by the same thing—indigestion and
dyspepsia. The remedy named (aired her because
it has that power. The reason remains a secret
with the roots and herbs from which it is made.
Yet so long as it drives away disease and gives us
back our health and strength, who cares for its
mystery ? Results, not arguments, are what we
all want.
“Burnt Oak House, Edgware, September, 22nd,
1892. I have known Mrs. Mathews for some
seven years, and remember her long and lingering
illness. She informs me that Mother Seigel’s
Curative Syrup cured her, after medical and other
means failed. Mrs. Mathews is a lady of respecta-
bility, and her word can be implicitly relied upon.
You can use this statement in any way you may
think proper. Yours truly, (Signed) T. H. House,
Grocer and General Provision Dealer, Burnt Oak
Stores, Edgware.”

Dear Bulletin, —I went to a Sydney
■doctor the other day. He gave me a prescription.
The body of it was just this : “ Mist. Rubra ” —
no more. “ You take this,” says he, “ to Quercus,
the chemist, at such-and-such a number in So-so
street.” “But I deal with Picrotoxin and Co.,”
said I, “ and as I happen to know that this is
only a ‘ tally ” prescription—* Red Mixture ’ —I
want a proper one; a menu of contents, please.”
“You go to Quercus, and that prescription will
fix you up all right. Picrotoxin and Co. put any-
thing in their mixtures, and you can’t rely on
their drugs.” “Defamation, that! doctor, if I like
to make trouble. I’ve been in the trade myself,
and know.” ... Of course I got my pre-
scription, and the doetor lost one commission on
Red Mixture out of Quercus. It’s a pretty little
game, but it’s time the public knew about it.
Every man has the right to choose his own
chemist. I could tell a few rousing yarns of
medical-commission connections and of “Mix-
tures ” that were real patents for absorbing the
surpluses from any and every cause, until the
“Winchester quarts ” had to be broken to get at
the curious insolubles gradually sedimented, layer
on layer, making thebottle useless. —Yours, Dipso.
Dear Bulletin, —Let me supplement your
last correspondent’s par. re the fortunes made by
original Broken Hill holders. The Horns were
“ somebody ” prior to buying in to the Hill—they
had a lot of money out all over the Barrier. The
Wilsons were also somebody, inasmuch as W. R.
Wilson was general-manager for the Barrier
Ranges Association, and a hard worker and
genuine speculator. To Horn and Wilson,
ably assisted by W. P. M‘Gregor, of Glen-
lyon, belongs the credit of forming the
property into a large consolidated co. But
the lion’s share of kudos in connection with
this property unquestionably belongs to George
M‘Culloch, then of Mt. Gipps. He was the
ruling genius long ere either Walter Sully or J. S.
Reid had any interest in the mine. As a matter
of fact, Reid did not come in till after the mine
was floated, and shares were .£l4 or £l5 each.
Sully certainly did own a small original interest,
but how does your correspondent arrive at the
idea that Sully and Reid were the only know-
ledgeable workers of the crowd, when neither ever
worked an hour on the mine, and the “ work-
ing ” of the float, &c., was due, as I have said, to
Wilson, Horn, M‘Gregor, Bowes Kelly, and
George M'Culloch? Yes, to George M'Culloch,
I, as an outsider who watched the rise of this
marvellous mine, assert belongs the credit of
keeping together the Proprietary in the sometimes
black and cheerless days before any co. was formed.
Your correspondent also errs when he says Charley
Rasp pegged out blocks 1 to 14. The blocks pegged
out were 10 to 10—seven in all, and they were
held by a syndicate of the employes of Mt. Gipps
station (on which the mine is situated) with G.
M‘Culloch, manager of the station, as secretary for
said syndicate. The names of the original holders
were Rasp, M‘Culloch, Urqubart, Lind, Poole,
James and Phillip Charley, and they held one-
seventh each. Owing to sales of half inter-
ests here and there, the shares were made into
fourteenths, which in turn became again divided
by sales, and so on till the co. was formed. I
don’t quite agree with your correspondent as to
the amount of kudos he gives Reid and Sully.
Certainly they did their best, but there were others
who did far more, and who assuredly did not
make their success by “stubbing their toes in the
dark.” There was a fairly long and trying
struggle before Broken Hill was an assured suc-
cess, when her poor returns were completely over-
shadowed by the brilliant, but patchy, Day Dream,
Lubra, Apollyon, Bobbie Burns, &c., &c. The
majority of the originals stuck well and deserve
what they got.—W.

“If every mother knew of
its value no other would be
used. ”
Cable news that Brazil felt injured by a
British annexation of Trinidad must have set the
little world of foreign-stamp collectors thinking.
The only Trinidad known to philatelists has been
quite English for many years. The ancient figure
of Britannia on the postage - stamps was sup-
planted by Queen Vic.’s head some years ago, and
the money is, in the language of stamp albums,
“like Great Britain.’’ Trinidad proper lies not
far from the Venezuelan coast, and is, in fact, a
good deal nearer to Brazil than the paltry little
island, way out in the South Atlantic, about which
they are making such a to-do. The other Trinidad
is a lone, forgotten lump of rocky ground, only
six miles in circumference, so England may as
well have it, and stamp it with V. R’s. beautiful
young head according to the custom of the country
“ Gallia,” by Menie Dowie, is a fine sample
of the fin-de-siecle novel. Gallia is a lovely New
Woman, brainy, phiianthropical, sweet-tempered,
and disposed to hug the man she loves without
asking his consent. He holds aloof from the
maiden’s strong intellectual caress, but lacks the
manliness to admit that his coolness is mere pru-
dence, forced upon him by heart-disease. This
leaks out in the last chapter, after Gallia has
entered upon the task of trying to love another.
Gallia has liberal views on marriage and
maternity. A woman, she expvins to her
girl friends, may be an ideal wi\ c e, although
her constitution doesn’t justify experiments
in the way of offspring. “ What, then, would
be the reasonable tiling to yo ? Sacrifice
the poor woman for the sako of a weakly
baby ? ” Gallia answers her own conundrum
on the spot. “No, of course not, but gee in a
mother ! She would he a splendid, healthy
accredited woman, and probably physically very
attractive. The man’s sentiment, if lie had any,
would be greatly in her favor. He would have
been able to indulge his very highest feeling in
the choice of his wife. It would make enormously
in favor of morality.” This is a novel for hus-
bands to read and dream over, bet ore the winter
fire, when their lawful spouses have retired to
rest. Married ladies, as a rule,.will not support
the notion for “getting in a mother.” Menie
Dowie writes for men. Her story, readable from
cover to cover, is dedicated to her own husband.
She sympathises with the male person’s sense of
duty to amorous impulse, and rounds off her
reflections with a mock Osoaric epigram. Thus—
“ Nature has no regard for the fitness of
things, and Life arranges the most tasteless
contrasts. For instance, it was far from
agreeable of them both to make Gallia attract
Mark with the light lit by Essex’s kisses,
and it was equally outr6 in them to send Mark to
the arms of his mistress with his head filled with
the thoughts of another woman. But none of the
persons concerned were to blame for these con-
tingencies. Life and Nature were alone respon-
sible, and can only be excused on the ground that
neither is well-bred.” The mistress whose arms
Mark was sent to is a confiding little French girl.
She falls ill (under circumstances which The Bul-
letin would blush to understand), and Mark finds
poor Oara one day groaning upon the couch, in
charge of a charwoman. The charwoman thinks
he ought to know something about the case, but
he doesn’t. He is astonished to hear that the
poor girl was taken ill whilst dancing. “ A
sharp look from the faded eye of Mrs. Miles
was lost upon him.” He bent over Cara to
enquire why she had danced so much. Cara said “I
was frightened, and so I thought I would dance
and dance.” He shook his head vaguely. “ Bein’
no stairs to come up and down,” said Mrs. Miles,
in a furtive undertone” . . . Somehow there
is an air of reality about “Gallia.” It is con-
sistently written. One hopes that the heroine will
be happy with her husband, despite her passion
for the icicle with the diseased heart. She pro-
mises him that “my hands and arms will know
perfectly how to clasp you,” although she has no
sentiment worth mentioning. “Love-making,”
says Gallia, “is a trick of heredity. Hands, arms,
and lips are born with the cunning of it.” Where-
upon “Mark shivered with sudden passion.” An
entertaining work, with no unpleasant char-
acters in the plot, and very little plot that doesn’t
shiver with passion.
All around the always beautiful Albany
Pass, and the islands of that extreme portion of
North Queensland, the cocoanut trees (planted by
Govt.) now grow in profusion, and make an ideal
tropical picture of what was even formerly one of
the loveliest show places of the eastern coast of
the continent.
Scene: Public-house bar. First utter,
but mellow, stranger to second utter, but per-
fectly-sober, stranger—[N.B.—Each stranger is
engaged in discussing a “ solitary ”] —Excuse
me; but I know y’r face —will you j’n me? Met
you th’ other day with man called Lycett—know
’m ?” Second utter, but perfeetly-sober, stranger,
to whom the name Lycett has a foreign sound, but
to whom also a cheap drink is only an occasional
luxury ; “Know him ! Well, I should think so;
I know him as well nearly as I know you.” They
drink together at the first utter, but mellow,
stranger’s expense.


The N.S.W. Opposition—Who Shall
Lead It?
Editor Bulletin, — The king is dead! Yester-
day George Dibbs was leader of the N.S.W.
Protectionist party —to-day, none so poor lo do
him reverence. Even the meanest mid lowest
specimen of roaddom and bridgedom declines to
sink his insignificance to make room for a noble
ruin—a discredited leader, or inisleader, of men.
George Dibbs is a man who casually wandered
into politics. His training, his temperament,
his mental bias, his vices, his very virtues, all
unfitted him for the political sphere. Yet, in a
mistaken moment, lie became a politician. In
a country like N.S. Wales any man is a possible
leader. In tlie country which endured—nay,
worshipped—the able trickster Parkes, sup-
ported the pauderer Martin, tolerated that
mixture of humbug and self-seeking Stuart,
and loved that strange combination of slang,
snuffle and blasphemy, Jack Robertson, it was
not a surprise when, one morning, George Dibbs
walked accidentally into the room of a newly-
fledged Protectionist parly, and, catching the
words “ We want a leader ! ” offered his alleged
services, and was accepted.
» * •
Who can forget the night when he delivered
his first speeeli as a convert from Freetrade in
Sydney Masonic Hall ? A crowded audience,
a crowded platform, enthusiasm fore and aft.
Ou the platform uany a good man who had
fought the battle of Protection against crusted
tradition, inborn stupidity, religious cant, and
racial prejudice, sat there that night knowing
not that a buccaneer was being given command
of the ship, and that many a man would go
overboard aud many a shipwreck threaten
before the new captain could be dislodged. The
history of Protection since then lias been but
a record of dwindling disaster. The common-
est political tyro could never have committed
the mistakes which are placed lo the record
of this big-hearted bungler.. Every step lias
been marked by the most malicious stupidity,
the most malign contempt for the interests of
a great parly —for even the common safety of
its members. The instinct of self-preservation
is common to the lowest, as well as the highest
of the animal kingdom. So in politics. The
soaring eagle of politics, even in his loftiest
flight, knows the right time to flutter down-
wards. But this big, manly man, this fine
fibre of a gentleman, knew no more about the
instinct of political self-preservation than a
sheep does. Seeking lo represent a great
majority of the people, he always insulted the
masses. Scorning to be the tool of a section of
tlie community, he always placated tlie classes.
Always ou the look-out for political chances,
be always missed them. Unconvinced, uncon-
cerned, unable to distinguish Freetrade from
Protection, indifferent when shown the diver-
gence, he floundered along through the shifting
quicksands of politics, sinking lower and lower
until escape was impossible,, and with oppor-
tunities neglected, with chances thrown away,
a party decimated by his callous, wilful or
stupid acts, he is shut out of political life at a
moment most inopportune for himself, but, we
may hope, most opportune for his party.
* m *
What was the secret of his decline and fall ?
For one thing, as lias been said, bis tempera-
ment. Shallow, unread, ignorant of literature,
especially iguoranl of political economy, he in-
troduced into politics a Micawber-like fatalism,
an unready groping for something tangible in
the fluctuations of an unconvinced mind.
Brave, undoubtedly ; manly and generous, un-
questionably ; kindly aud attractive in bis per-
sonality, he would have won all hearts had he
only had a very small percentage of sincere
political conviction to base his personal quali-
ties upon. Again, never in tlie whole history
of politics has a man been cursed with such a
run of political ill-luck as George Dihbs.
All the financial disasters, all tlie deficits,
all the brutal and unjustifiable hangings, all the
unemployed crises, have managed to bring
themselves under notice in connection with his
name. Dickens knew the value of a name to
create a mental bias in a reader. It was a
lucky thought when he invented the name of
Squeers for Yorkshire schoolmasters as they
were then. Aud so tlie monosyllable Dibbs has
always had a fatal significance attached to it in
New South Wales, sufficient to make the owner
and any party he led stagger under it. He
stopped the sale of public estate. Yet he is
cursed as the creator of the “Dibbs deficit.”
He was opposed to the introduction of Chinese,
yet he has been called Chinaman Dibbs. He
feeds tlie hungry, clothes tlie naked and never
turns a deaf ear to a tale of distress. Yet he is
accused of having said that the working men
should “ eat grass.” What a fatality of rapidly
rolling circumstances have followed in his un-
happy footsteps—until the fortunes of his party
may be said to depend upon his utter extinction
as its leader aud his entire disassociatiou from
its policy. #
Long live the king ! But who shall he be ?
Lyne? Well, he has long hankered for the
sceptre of authority. A blatant man, with a
pugnacious demeanour, a crusted Conservatism,
a selfishness which one could cut with a knife, a
bullying habit; per contra, a fair administra-
tive capacity, and a reputation for courage and
unswerving consistency aud for sticking to those
who stick to him. Added to this, a small know-
ledge of procedure, no fertility of resource, poor
debating power, no knowledge of constitutional
law, and you exhaust the man. Next—Joint
See! No administrative capacity, a long-
winded flatulence in debate, a habit of repeti-
tion, a costermonger’s knowledge of figures, a
constant habit of declaring that he is “ animated
bv the very best of intentions ” —a substance we
.bear Hell is,paved with. Shall he assume the
-.man tie of Dibbs? O’Sullivan, stand forth ! Shifty,
mustable as water, adroit; no debating power, but
;a certain force and finesse which occasionally
tells. Well, this man is preferable to tlie weak
and apologetic. See, or tlie headstrong and
bulky, belligerent Lyne. Next —Crick. Well,
here's a leader, at any rate. A born leader is a
man who knows ids own road, aud never needs,
to follow. Crick is all that, and something
more. He lias the highest debating power and
readiness-rough, Lis true, but great for all
that. Courage is his. As a constitutional
authority he ranks above the Speaker. When
he debates a constitutional point or a point of
order, he settles the question clearly and de-
finitely. The wordy Barton could not out-
argue him. Neither could the silly, gabbling
Reid, or the utterly insignificant Gould, who
rushes in always where angels fear to tread.
Against Crick in the full height of his intellec-
tual power, with his wild oats nearly sown,
stands the Crick that was, with a load of pre-
judice to carry, with a cross of boyish foolish-
ness to hear. The past rises up against him.
Then, again, the unfortunate combination of
letters constituting liis name—Crick—nearly as
had as Dihbs or Squeers. And yet, in the
kingdom of the politically blind, he stands alone
a one-eyed king.
* » *
These are the present visible alternatives
from Dihbs in the leadership of the Pro-
tectionist party. Away into the night of
oblivion goes the titled Republican, the
soft-hearted, Executive hanger, the attrac-
tive personality of political repulsiveness.
Who shall succeed him ? May his rest he long
and peaceful, for his party shall build its
success upon the negative service he does by
his absence ! The longer his absence, the better
for his parly, though the kindly, headstrong,
blundering man of reality is nothing like tlie
creature which has been set up in the daily
press and labelled Dibbs —saddled with all the
political delinquencies of the last 15 years,
loaded with the crimes which others have com-
mitted, held responsible for disasters which
others have brought about, attributed with
sentiments utterly foreign to the man and Ids
nature. And who shall succeed him ? The
king is dead, long live the king—but which !
Jacques.

Dear Bulletin, —You said last week that “the
omission of Giffen from Hie next Australian Eleven would,
no doubt, mean Hie withdrawal of Hie w hole 8. A. brigade,
which would never do.” The whole S.A. brigade except
Giffen would secretly chuckle were Giffen left out. The
whole S.A. brigade remembers how Giffen forced brother
Walter into l he last team in preference to several better
S.A. men. When a cricketer has slaved 12 years or so for
the purpose of realising bis darling ambition, (bat grand
English trip, lie won’t gladly forego bis present chance
because Giffen ousted him before. There are two or three
of Hie S.A. brigade who think that an English trip would
make them more serviceable to Australia than even Giffen
is. There are several S.A. men and many Vies, and
Sydneyites who say that Giffen lost Hie past season’s first,
and last test-matches. Giffen’s conceit and temper have
made him the most unpopular cricketer in Australia, and
the whole S.A. brigade would delight in seeing the great
man brought to reason. —Key.

E l. Bulletin, —The reason why George Giffen
should not captain Hie next Australian eleven is that
Giffen, when captain, acts as if Giffen were Hie whole
eleven. For instance, Capt. Giffen starts with Hie best
bowler (t.e., Giffen); and if the best bowler doesn’t come
off lie toils <iii the next best, w ho is Giffen ; ami if this
second-best, bowler doesn’t succeed, he puts on the best
change bowler, viz., Giffen; and if this best change
bowler gets p st‘ il, he tries l he whole eleven one after the
other, w hich is Giffen ; and he, too, failing, not lung is left
but for the captain lo put himself on, who is Giffen. This
being ineffectual, Giffen goes on himself lo keep the runs
down, and by this time Hie opposing team has scored
neaily 1000 and won Hie match. I have in my mind’s-
eye two men who could most adequately fill the post, if
only Hie one could be relied upon lo do the right thing at
Hie proper time, and if Hie other would keep off the cursed
drink. Notwithstanding these drawbacks, either would
lie belter than Hie always sober and too energetic Giffen,
for Hie first could be bullied into taking advice from Hie
rest of tlie team, and the second could never get so drunk
as to believe himself eleven men.—Sam.

The W. G. Grace testimonial committee plead

pion, as it is an open secret that his life-long devotion to
the interests of our national game has necessarily inter-
fered with Ins practice as a medical man.” Grace lias
us.'d his medical diploma for all it was worth, extracting
larger professional fees through it as an amateur cricketer
than any professional cricketer everobtained, and making
in one y ear’s Australian tour ±3OOO, which as a doctor lie
would probably have taken ten favorable years to earn.
Hectors of the W. G. Grace order of intellect write pre-
scriptions for 2s. 6d. in Engiaud, and are as thick there as
colonels are in Yankeelaud.

Megson, the N.S.W. crack cyclist, shortly goes

The amateur athletic associations of Sydney
define an amateur as “ one w ho lias never competed for a
money prize, slaked bet, or declared wager, or who lias
not knowingly competed with or against a professional
for a prize of any description, or for public -exhibition, or
who has never taught, pursued, or assisted in Hie prac-
tice of any athletic exercise as a means of livelihood or
for pecuniary gain.” Few Australian amateurs reach
this lily-white standard. H fenders of the cash or bogus
amateur argue that, under the ideal am.at nr system, the
amateurs arranged wins a id backed their men accord-
ingly, and were altogether so Impel, ssly corrupt that the
introduction of tin* filthy -lucre sy stem i < ally endm ed to
comparative straightness. One result, of the above defini-
tion is to relegate the “amateur ” ciickelers of the Aus-
tralian elevens to the professional ranks.

T 1 m N.S.W. League of Wheelmen’s road-race
brought 32 cyclists to the post to graft 19 miles and back
over the hard and Lilly' road between Parramatta and
Penrith. Those sleepy villages turned out in force for
the event; close upon 200 cyclists were upon the road
pacing and encouraging their fancies. Willis, tire
Head Bird, was paced by many admirers, including a
racehorse. But lie fell, like many others, with a sad fall.
Of those wdio capsized, II rris, it is said, is still in tied.
The cracks, Megson end L wis, didn’t race; at least,
Megson did for three miles and then gave up because bis
tire had punctured ; Lew is couldn't race because of an
affliction which has immortalised Job. Buck, who is 45
years old, rode three machines altogether, the first punc-
turing and the second breaking. Five miles from home
Buck led M’Coombe, the second man, by five minutes,
but when only a mile remained M’Coombe and a big bill
came together and the exhausted Buck was beaten.
Thenceforth M'Coombe ran merrily on and won by lialf-a-
mile. Buck, encouraged l>y six friends, struggled on,
getting second place, lint the severe ordeal prostrated him
for two hours after. M’Coombe, who won on a Humber
machine, is an ex-Maorilai der and Victorian, He won in
’92 the one-mile championship of Maoriland and the five-
mile championship of Australasia. The first prize was a
new cycle.

The 8000 people, including Lieutenant-Governor
Harley, who watched Victoria play N.S.W. Rugby foot-
ball, on Sydney Cricket Ground lastSaiurday, came away
with the conviction that no biilliant Victorian footballer
lias yet gone over lo Rugby. The majority of the crack
N.S.W. players were away in Brisbane losing the match
against Queensland, but (base left behind beat ibe Vic-
torians by 25 to C. The Vies, numbered, it is said, 10 ex-
Maoiiiand Rugby men, but surely no Maoiilander ever
played Rugby in the courteous and obliging spirit the
Victorians showed. Madigan, for instance, w lien nearing
full-back M'Malion, never dropped the ball and started
dribbling it; lie held it under his arm with the zeal of a
crusader nursing a sacred relic, and he charged the full-
back with tlie fell resolve of an enraged bull. Rugby, to
be played properly, must be played either with all pluck
or no sense, and as the Vies, last Saturday played with
pluck temp red by sense their exhibition new ssarily failed
to reach the crowd’s accepted standard of Rugby foot-
ball. The return match is play ed on Saturday, 17i.fi hist.

South Adelaide, by its defeat of Norwood club
on Aug. 3, secured S.A. football premiership. Little in-
terest centres in S.A. football this season —in some degree
owing lo the superiority of the Souths, but mainly
because the public have tired of the game. All the
college boys and “amateurs” play lacrosse, which is
making immense strides in Adelaide. Where, years ago,
10,000 witnessed a football-match bet ween Norwood and
South, scarcely 3UOO can be mustered this season.

Last Monday, at Sydney Amateur Athletic
Club, there was a big gathering and some good boxing.
Twee-die began by knocking B.ivis lo a standstill with
large gloves in three rounds; then Mason fought three
bitter and determined rounds with Foster. After a
“young-iiianfrom-llie-c am try ” spar, the fam ms amateur
Seale showed in splendid form in three rounds with Jim
Barron. But the Burden-Funnell ten-rounds fixture was
the event of the evening. Burden is a short, round man
oflOst.; Fumiell, a taller, wiry man of 9sl. 811). Both
acre in good condition, and, up to the end of the second
round, showed many tactics and much slipping and
baulking, tile only blow struck being a left-hand hook,
meant for the chin but reaching Funnell’a nose. In the
third round. Burden’s right, which had been threatening
Funnell's body, reached bis jaw in a flash and sent him
down, and when he got up he was again knocked down.
He arose once more at the sound of “nine,” only to he
rushed and punched right “out.” Funnel) bad won
some ten fights, and this is his first defeat. Burden has
won a dozen or so.

It was not the regular racing sweeps that urged
onQ. Parliament to pass tire law suppressing lotteries alter
the end of '95 - it was the advantage taken of the State s
leniency by some Charters Towers people. At the Towers
whole shop-fronts were knocked out. Long cotnileis ran
across the back, and at each clerks were busy day and
night selling shilling tickets in cash lotteries. No wonder
the storekeepers and publicans combined to squelch the
little games.

The starting-price dodge is very prevalent
round Melb. Trainer Blank lias bis boise, Bloodsucker,
entered for the Suburban Handicap, and as he is lit and
carries only 7st. the moke is almost a certainty. But the
public know almost as much about Bloodsucker as Blank
dots, and sn, if he tried to back on the course, the price
would shorten and he would he left in the cold. So he
sends it out u nbacked, and the over-cunning public, wink-
ing at iis If, says “Not having any,” and lias the satis-
faction of seeing Bloodsucker win from end to end. On
returning to town they find that just before Hie race the
horse bad been backed to the fullest extent with all the
starting-price bookmakers. Result, starting-price 10 to 1,
and S.P. books very sick.

Most of the old-established British bookmakers
were suffering from gout-” poor man's gout’ probably
—on Derby Day, and laid the odds under difficulties.
Dick Dunn is mentioned as one of the most pathetically
gouty subjects at Epsom, which ought to shake’
makers’ faith in Turkish baths. Dunn, of old, used to
take “a blanky good sweatin’ ’ about four tunes a
week, yet he goes about nowadays with a corpulent
arm in a sling, or a foot that can be seen coming hall-a-
mile away. Poor Lord Chesterfield 1 Dick Dunn is
entitled Lord Chesterfield on account of his awfully im-
proper language, , • •
Talking of Derby Day and its vanities, G. R.
Sims remarks that the three-card trick is pla>ed out.
Here and there a party of aged spielers may b ® P‘
forming the ancient fakes and betting bad halftr ™ .
among themselves, but the crowd passes 'hem by with
disdain, or stops awhile to “ chaff. Even Ilingland has
Outgrown this primitive form of swindle.

Why has no Vio. football captain sense enough
to take on another man when one of his team is compelled
to leave the ground through injury? The rules stale
that the game shall be played between 20 men aside, yet
the idiotic spectacle of a team playing with 19, or even
18 men for three-quarters nf the game is not uncommon,
while often and often a disabled and utterly useless player
remains stuck in goal 011 the off-chance of touching the
ball on its way through.

F. A. Iredalo, the N.S.W. batsman, scored 80
runs per innings for eight, innings in last season’s Sydney
premiership matches, and put up a record by scoring two
centuries in one season’s Australia v. England contests.

J.D. : The Fizzers were a famous mob of wild
horses, the progeny of a thoroughbred called the Fizz* r,
which only became extinct in or about ’74, and could be
found anywhere on the plains between Togamain—owned
by Jacky Dow—and the Murray, in earlier times. Jacky
Dow was one of the earliest and biggest squatters between
the Murrumhidgee and Murray. All Riverina men know
of the Fizzers, and many can tell of famous rides after
the mob. A man who captured a great many Fizzers still
lives in Peechelha—where Morgan was shot-and is known
as "Jack the Fizzer.” He is the sire of the famous
horsewoman, Mrs. Ambrose, who owns and rides at Aus-
tralian shows Gratis, Nimrod, Innisfail, and other horses.

The yarn comes from Mb. Victoria, N.S.W.,
that, in the recent Federal rifle-shootinir contest. F.
Winser, when shooting at 700yds. distance, had to allow
7ft. for windage. Blizzards are becoming an Australian
industry. , * *
The two Marshes (aboriginals) and the three
Martineers are most extraordinary contradictions of the
theory that “ brothers of cracks, boss nr man, ain’t never
no good.” Jack and L. Marsh are N.S. Wales’ two very
best sprint runners, and Fred, Ernie, anil C. A. Maitineer
are within two yards of each other in six score, and the
two first were, in their day, within three yards of world’s
championship form. Their father, in his day, was one of
the first three jockeys in Australasia, and he isn’t a bad
’tin yet, while his sons take to the pigskin like young
ducks take to water.

The manner in which the victorious Daimio
was trained for his engagements puzzled the Fb mington
touts considerably. All he was doing, so far as they conid
see. was an ordinary working gallop every morning, and
he never went near the schooling fences. Consequently,
the talent wouldn’t have him at any price. They were
not aware that, besides his morning gallop on the tracks,
he was taken over four or five miles of hilly, hunting
country around Maribyrnong, jumping everything he
came across, and carrying a 12st. man. Consequently,
when the race came on, he outjumped, outgalloped, and
outstayed everything.

The Turco-Figyptian Tobacco Co., which
has its Sydney branch at 108 Pitt-street, grows its
renowned tobacco on its own plantations in Mace-
donia, where the unspeakable Turk possesses a
■oil and climate specially adapted to tobacco
culture. Nowhere save in his dominions docs it
present Quite the same delicate aroma, and all
attempts to grow an exactly similar quality else-
where have failed. Possibly the result is owing
to the fact that the blood and brains of almost
every race known to history have been spilt on
the Macedonian plains, and the soil has been
enriched bv old dead crusader and many other
fertilisers—anyhow, the result is incontestable,
whether the dead crusader is the cause or not.*

veloped a serum for inoculation against snakebite,

possible, after the splendid results of diphtheria

The Queensland bores already in operation
would supply 100 towns the size of Brisbane with
water. And yet this is only an insignificant frac-
tion of the real rainfall. According to geologist
Jack, the Darling only receives 1J per cent, of the
rainfall in its catchment basin, the remainder
soaking down into the cretaceous rocks. _ r lhe
question is, would a prolonged drought seriously
affect the output of the artesian wells in the West ?
Many authorities say it would.
Moses Coldwells, one of the Liberator
Building Society directors, happened to expire the
other day, and his decease is attributed (says the
cable) to the great mental worry occasioned by
the prosecution. Worry, one may observe, didn t
run Mo. Coldwells to earth until his old pal,
Jabez Balfour, had been fetched back to London.
He lived through the wails of busted shareholders
and the screams of the press as long as Jabez was
keeping retribution at bay. When the actual
prosecution arrived, the ex-member for Lambeth
North thought it time to die a natural death. He
couldn't do less under the circumstances, and he
might have had the good taste to suicide.
A perennially hard-up Adelaide citizen
recently had an order of five days’ imprisonment
issued against him on an unsatisfied debt-sum-
mons. Whereupon the prosecuting lawyer re-
ceived the following ;
My Dear Sir,—Yours yesterday’s date just received.
While sincerely thanking you, I am sorry to have so far
trespassed on your friendly forbearance, which I will do
no longer. If you will be good enough to take out a
warrant, and send Connor (my own particular sergeant)
down to escort me to gaol, I will meet him at the Glan-
ville Station (where the Largs train joins the Semaphore
line) at 9 o'clock sharp. As you are perfectly aware,
Wednesday evening is, from the debtor’s point of view,
the most eligible lime to commence a five days’ order, and
I am sure you will spare me the unnecessary annoyance
of being arrested by the 1< cal bailiff. Moreover, by baring
me arrested as I desire, you w ill ire doing me a great good
service, as on Friday I shall be able to again see tire only
doctor who lias ever thoroughly examined me, and in
whom I have every confidence as completely understand-
ing my constitution, and who is only available under cer-
tain circumstances. Youi s, &c.
Percy Spence has arrived in London, and
at once had a half-page accepted by the Graphic.
Blackie and Son have given him another book to
illustrate (lie did one for them in Australia,
Faveno’s “Secret of the Australian Desert”),
and lie has also another private commission to
illustrate a boy’s book. He writes: “The
Academy exhibition is stunning. Tadema,
Leighton, Stanhope Forbes, Luke Fildes and
many others are first-rate ; but there is an all-
pervading air of cleanliness about the pictures
that is positively sickening. When you see
Bastien Lepage or Pelouse, you feel quite re-
freshed after the academical work. The English
artist disdains dirt. His landscape has evidently
to be washed before he paints it. The idea of
having any dirt about the earth seems absurd to
these sweetly pretty painters. That a farmer
returning from work should have a speck of dirt
on him seems to the English artist an impossi-
bility.”
Mr. J. I, Marshall, the well-known Sydney
dentist, announces his removal to 28 Wynyard-
square, (near Pfahlert’s), where he has much
greater facilities to accommodate his numerous
patrons. The operating and waiting rooms are
handsomely furnished, the former being fitted
with the latest dental appliances from America.
The workshop contains all the paraphernalia
requisite for vulcanising and gold rolling, and also
for the manufacture and preparation of artificial
teeth.*
When old James Wilson owned St. Albans,
a big supporter of the stable, whom we will call
Joe, once matched a man for a sum of money in a
footrace. Not being altogether sure of the ped.’s
morals he sent an artful friend to interview him
and see if “ he would listen to reason ” for a con-
sideration. The “ped.” professed to be most in-
dignant, and wound up a vigorous protest by ask-
ing what the tempter thought his mother would
say if he were to lose a race he might have won !
Joe, on receiving the answer, was satisfied, and
piled on the “sugar,” thinking his man was all
right. But, alas and alack! at half the distance
the honest “ ped.” palpably fell, and the only
satisfaction Joe had was to yell in his ear, “ What-
ever will your mother say ? ”*
Some years ago an Israelite, who made a
small book, and a local trainer were suspected of
touting the St. Alban’s horses at work, but they
were not found out until one morning just before
a trial gallop. The then proprietor armed himself
with an ancient pistol, charged only with powder,
and went for a quiet walk round the track as if to
inspect it. Under the ample boughs of the acacia
hedge, about a quarter of a mile from the winning-
post, he noticed a heap of something which he
made for at quick march. At his approach up
rose the suspected parties (they had not seen the
pistol) and made off. Their movements were
greatly accelerated by a tremendous report, alter
which ditches and fences were taken in Grand
National style, and they got off clear, minus a
hat and an overcoat.*
Gossage’a prize soap, for which Gill Bros.,
of 33 York-street, are sole Sydney agents, marks
another advance in the great art and mystery of
cleanliness. In remote days the primitive Aryan
man was content to go wholly unwashed ; lie
accumulated vegetable mould from his cradle —if
he had one—to his grave. Then he occasionally
scraped himself with a flint. Later he began to
wash in a perfunctory fashion, and so the process
went on until now, in the cleanest circles of
society, soap-making has become a fine art, and
every respectable inhabitant washes himself
occasionally, if not oftener. Soap has gone
through many gradations ; it began with a bar of
a coarse and horrible yellow material which took
the skin off the patient in places and left him of a
bright red color, and it ends, so far, with Gos-
sage’s, pure and fragrant."

Hardly any of the big Australasian life-assur-
ance societies ever dreamt, in the good days of high
interest and constantly-increasing revenues, of putting
aside a respectable reserve-fund to cover losses at some
possible future date, when the value of their properties
and their mortgages might go down heavily. Only a
few of them, even now, make any serious effort in this
direction. The Mutual of Victoria has just £6020 to
covet possible depreciation on £900,000 worth of property,
or about 13s. 4d. per £lOO. The Widows’ Fund has
£3768 to cover possible losses on £1,200,000 worth of pro-
perty, or 6s. 3d. per cent. The balance-sheet of the
Mutual Life Association shows no reserve whatever.
Neither does that of the Colonial Mutual. The Govt.
Life Depaitraent of Maoriland has a reserve of £43,000,
equal to *.2 per cent., and the National Mutual has
£35,000, equal to over t 2 4s. percent. The City Mutual
(Sydney) has £l5OO (£1 ss. per cent.); the Temperance
and General, £2834, or almost the same as the City
Mutual; and the A.M.P. has the (by comparison) stupen-
dous reserve of £416,000, or £3 4s. per cent. This last
reserve, which shows out magnificently in contrast to
the others, has, however, all been accumulated in the
last two or three years, so that the present policy-holders
are paying for the want of forethought shown in times
past, and for the high bonuses of previous years. Still the
fact that it has been made shows a readiness to grapple
with the situation, and if the A.M. P. maintains its reserve
for the future no such trouble should again arise. Amongall
these offices the Maoriland Govt. Department is the best
protected, inasmuch as a comparal ively small proportion
of its funds are invested in land and mortgages—the
items where tire heaviest depreciation generally comes.
The A.M.P. conies next, and the National Mutual third,
while the rest are various distances behind—some of
them quite out of sight. The possibilities of a big insur-
ance office, with a million or two of mortgages and a
merely nominal reserve, or no visible reserve at all, are
alarming in a time of severe depression.
♦ » * *
Some of the reasons why a uniform and entirely
new form of bank balance-sheet should be enforced when
the new Banking legist dion comes along will be found in
the following facts: Among the banks doing business in
the three eastern provinces, the Bank of N.S. W., the
City of Sydney, Bank of New Zealand, Commercial of
Sydney, and A.J.S. don’t show the amount of their work-
ing expenses, so that whether the management is extra-
vagant or otherwise is not known even to the share-
holders. The A.J.S. lumps together its new fixed
deposits and its long-dated reconstructed deposits. The
Commercial of Sydney, Royal of Queensland, Colonial
(Melb.), Bank of Victoria, Queensland National and
8.N.Q., lump together their reconstructed deposits, new
fixed deposits, current accounts and “other liabilities”
in one awful mass. The City of Melbourne lumps
together reconstructed deposits, new deposits, current-
accounts, and amounts due to other banks. Among the
reconstructed banks the Commercial of Australia, Lon-
don and National(MelbJ are the only ones whiCfr show
accurately the amount of their old fixed deposits
still unpaid or not released, so that a definite
idea can be formed of how they are getting on at
wiping out their old liabilities. Also the Commercial
of Australia is the only one which shows separately the
amount of unrealisable assets of the old bank and the
amount of interest they yield, as distinguished from the
revenue from ordinary banking business, and it is the
only one which shows the amount of property on hand
apart from its own banking premises. The Union-and the
Bank of New Zealand are the only ones which explain
fully how their reserve-fund is invested or where it can be
found if wanted.
The National (Melb.) announces that its
advances are £5,813,000 “after deducting office re-
serves,” but what the office reserves are, or how much
they are, and consequently how much provision is made
against depreciation, doesn’t appear. The City Bank of
Sydney, which is about the briefest of the lot, seems
to lump together, in one item, bills discounted, Govt,
or other debentures, notes of other banks, miscellaneous
landed property, overdrafts, and mortgages. A few
banks distinguish between their assets in England and
those in Australia, but the majority don’t. Not one gives
separately the amount of advances on mortgage and those
on miscellaneous securities. Three or four state what
amount has been paid in interest on deposits, but most of
them lie low' on this point. Some of them state that pro-
vision has been made for bad anddoubtful debts, but not one
explains how much has gone in this direction, and many
banks don’t even allege that they have made any provi-
sion at all. The nearest approach to accuracy in this
respect is the Commercial of Australia, which shows the
amount regularly set aside to cover losses on its old
business; and the London and A.J.S., which show each
half-year the state of their contingency-fund set aside for
the same purpose. Also, in three cases the auditors only
state that the balance-sheet is correct “ according to the
books,” which means absolutely nothing. Under all
these circumstances the public, in most cases, knows
absolutely nothing about the condition of any bank
except what can be gathered from the agitated state of
the share-market it the directors try to sell out too
hurriedly before the crash arrives. A balance-sheet, with-
outa system of independent Slate audit, only' rests, in any
case, on the statements of the manager and directors,
and the evil is further aggravated by the fact that these
gentlemen, in many cases, practically state nothing at all.
♦ * ♦ *
One sufficient reason why the public should
prefer small banks to large ones is that the former are, in
most cases, much the safer of the two. As an example,
in the Royal Bank of Australia the unpaid capital is
equal to times the whole liabilities to the public ; that
of the Bank of N.Q. is equal to its whole liabilities, and
that of the Ballarat Banking Co. to 60 percent, of its
liabilities. On the other side, the unpaid capital of the
Queensland National or the City of Melbourne wouldn’t
cover one-tenth of what they owe to the public, and
many other large banks are in a very similar situation.
* * * »
The Vic. Bills of Sale Act, in existence some
years, was framed by the principal of a trade protection
society, and is about the best Australian measure of its
kind. It provides that before a B.S. can be granted 14
days’ notice must be given. This is published in the
trade circulars, so that creditors may be able to stop the
B.S. until their claims are satisfied. But why should the
banks and big pastoral oo.’s be able to get their stock-
mortgages and wool and crop liens through without any
notice of intention to file, while other people can’t get a
fiver on their furniture without 14 days’ previous pub-
licity?
* ♦ * *
One smart person, at all events, has got badly
left in the Maoriland gold-boom. A local merchant had
a lot of shares which, despite the general inflation, kept
going st°adily down. He deposited them with a broker,
giving instructions to sell at a certain figure ; but they
got further and further away from it every day. So the
owner went up to inspect the mine, and from there he
wired in frantic terms to hold on—not to sell at any price
—to refuse all offers, and various other things to the same
hysterical effect. Whereupon the agent concluded that
a big discovery had been made, and he promptly wired
back: “Shares sold at your price just before telegram
arrived.” And now that merchant is safely out of a bad
spec., and a broker with a huge bundle of unsaleable
scrip on hand wants to bite the globe to express his
feelings.
• * * *
Nearly every fashionable Melb. hotel has its
tame money-lender (a rabid extortioner, as a rule), who
lives in the best rooms, fares sumptuously daily, and is
always prepared to judiciously stand a bottle in likely
“ pithneth ” quarters. The billiard-room marker, hall-
porter, the elevator man, and all the rest, are tipped to
give Shylock “ the office ”asto a lodger’s position. One
shark boasts that he has been making over £2OOO a year
profit on his hotel introductions.
It is said that empty Danish butter-kegs come
to Auckland (M.L.) to he filled with butter and shipped
hack to Denmark, where they are marked as Danish, and
shipped to London. Then the butter competes with the
Maoriland article in that great market under the brand of
some well-known Copenhagen exporter.
* * * *
Perth agents get as much as £3 from clients for
obtaining them houses, and then the tenant has often to
pay a half-year’s rent in advance.

The true history of repeated applications for the
throwing open of certain Vic. water-reserves for timber-
getting would he of much interest in banking and other
circles.
* * * *
Mr. Deering, late S.A. Assistant Agent-General,
invested his retiring allowance of £2500 in buying an
annuity. The office got the best of the deal, for he died
within a year. “A relation of mine,” writes a Bulletin
correspondent, “failed in a similar bargain. At 60 she
invested all her money in buying an annuity, and she
limited the term to 20 years, as she was sure she would
not live past 80, and so got a little more money by the
arrangement. She survived till 95, however, and had to
rely on her family for the last decade.”
* • » »
Edmund Mitchell, erst special correspondent
for the Age, &c., has returned to London from Westralia,
and, when the mail left, was engineering, with prospects
of success, a new mining co. called the Glasgow Westra-
lian Gold Mines. Principal features: (1) Vendors take no
cash and will rely upon the mine-returns. (2) Before
their shares rank for div. 100 per cent, must have been
paid to other shareholders. (3) No directors’ fees to be
paid; and, what is most unusual, there is no “waiver”
clause, all contracts and agreements being disclosed.
Capital, £15,000 only.
* * * ♦
The Bulletin hears of 5-6ths of a mine being
nominally sold for a big sum to a man who, though he
paid no deposit, had the show bonded to him for four
months, and took away on his pack-horse of stone
estimated to contain 50oz. gold. That was five months
ago, and now the owners decline £lOOO to Is. that they
never see an oz. of the atone. The owners of that show
were never on the “ Barrier ” in the days of “ travelling
on the slug.”

On July 17, the shares in Broken Hill Pro-
prietary Co. were held thus: Total for Australasia,
397,167; London, 562,833. In Adelaide on that date there
were 3203 shareholders, holding 226,054 shares ; in Melb.
661, holding 129,091; Hobart 91, holding 4774; Sydney
124, holding 23,446; Broken Hill 116, holding 4100;
and Port Pirie 30, holding 1252. One Adelaide man has
16,000 shares.
* ♦ ♦ ♦
At Fermoy opal field (Warrnambool Downs, 80
miles from Winton, 120 from Longreach), opened about a
year ago, £4OOO worth of excellent opals have been sold
to buyers, beside private deals. The opals, writes a Bul-
letin correspondent, are got at an average depth of 10ft.;
the deepest shaft in which good gems have been obtained
is 25ft. The opals are patchy, and the original mines
have been worked out. About 50 miners are on the
ground, and about the same number prospecting on
adjoining runs—upon one of which a couple of good finds
have been made. Last month Mr. Goldstein (S. Hoffnung
and Co., Sydney) bought about £l3OO worth of opal at
Fermoy ; Mr. Aplin, of Aplin and Co., Brisbane, about
£3OO, and Mr. Pender, of Melbourne, some £4OO worth—
amongst them one piece of sandstone opal of pipe forma
tion which realised £63.
* ♦ ♦ »
“Economy’’ writes: Payable stone is some-
times accompanied by expensive management. At a
mine near Glen Wills, about 30 miners are employed.
There are no less than six bosses, consisting of local
director (stipendiary), manager, underground manager,
and three shift bosses. The 00. having no battery, some
of the first three named must find their billets tolerably
easy. This big expense, added to the poorer returns of
free gold (as the stone becomes more mineralised with
depth), and the cost (something like 30s. a ton) of getting
stone to the public battery, minimises shareholders’
chances of profit.

The ship Greta has brought to Sydney, from
Italy, 1700 tons of sulphur. The consignees have a snug
monopoly of the local manufacture of sulphuric acid.

of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Oollins-street, Melb., a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic : 1, New Books; 2, Second-
hand Books; 3, Stationery ; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery ; 8, Pictures; 9,

Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half a million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half a mile
long. •
