A young woman walked into Melbourne
Supreme Court, the other morning, and placed a
sultry affidavit before his Honor. She was a co-
res pondent wjiq, aoipehow, didn’t appear in a
divorce case tried Two iiponths ago, and she now
dropped in promiscumlidy with a view to quashing
the decree • nisi, which inis not yet been made
absolute. The damsel’s affidavit set forth that in
November, 1890, she was requested by the fair
petitioner, to live with her husband as his
wife, down at Qneenscliff, while the lady her-
self carried on a laundry with another fellow at
MeU>, The damsel did as desired, her paramour
passing (at Qneenscliff) as his lawful wife’s brother-
in-law. Previous to this arrangement she had, in
the capacity of domestic servant to the congenial
pair, set up amorous relations with the husband
under bis wife’s patronage. The course of illicit
love rah smooth because it was carefully levelled
for them by “missus.” Finally, in May last, the
wife visited her husband, when the damsel hap-
pened to be within earshot, and promised him
if he would refrain from opposing her divorce-
suit. He refrained, and —if the affidavit be truth-
ful—probably drew bis <£G. There seems to be a
beautiful lesson for married folks in the co-
respondent’s statement. It shows how peace and
goodwill may be maintained between a wife and
husband, after mutual affection is dead to all
feeling of jealousy, by the introduction of a third
partner into the firm. The lodger, instead of
causing grief and ructions, is, under these im-
proved circumstances, a poorer of oil upon
troubled waters, and the acquiescent third party
sails gracefully away to a new haven of shelter :
A LOBERTINE LOVE SONG.
When Love is dead, poor mortal thing I
Quite dead, not merely,dozing,
A dismal lay most dullards sing
The while ’tis decomposing.
Corruption crawls from cold desires
Across their lives ill fated ;
O fling the corse, when Love expires,
Upon some bright new passion’s tires—
’Tis easily cremated.
Fools case it in a gloomy shell
And oaken coffin polished.
Or plant it in a flowery dell
By worms to be demolished,
Placing a stone to mark the spot
Whereon decease is dated.
When Love is dead, embalm it not"
See that your furnace Ares are hot.
And have the thing cremated.
Nothing remains, save ashes grey,
Quite clean and disinfected ;
And they are better blown away
Than carefully collected.
Enjoy new raptures, strong and sweet,
Until each sense he sated
And Love’s last heart h is ceased to beat—
Then wrap it in a winding sheet
And get yourself cremated.
A New Cream Separator.
The Sharples Cream Separator is guaranteed
to obtain from the milk of the farmer’s cow
and Ids neighbor’s more cream than can be
separated by any other invention. Testimonials
are numerous and highly commendatory : “ it
skims clean and does it cheaply ; ” “ no other
machine can be compared to it; ” “it saves
money and is thus a money-maker;” “beats
all competitions on butter-yield,” &c., &c. It
began its prosperous career six years ago in
America, and the first model, though going
daily ever since, has cost for repairs but one
dollar. Its economy is inferable from its requir-
ing but one-third the steam, and but one-tenth
the oil of its best rival ; it needs neither engine,
shafting, gearing, nor belting. It occupies but
two square feet of space, is of the simplest con-
struction ; it can be cleaned in four or five
minutes. Moreover, no Sharpies machine has
ever burst, and the manufacturers stand alone
amongst prominent makers of separators from
the fact of no person ever having been injured
in the slightest degree by one of their machines,
although thousands are in daily use. And thus,
while other cream separators occasionally spread
havoc and death and hurl the unprepared
butter-maker into that mystic eternity where
they skim not neither do they milk—the
Sharpies goes faithfully on as smoothly as a top
upon a china plate, cooling and aerating the
cream, resulting in the finest butter and biggest
dividends. It is being introduced the whole
world over, and is already saving fuel, oil, life,
time and money in America, Europe, Australia
and Maoriland. It keeps on taking first
prizes at dairy shows ; it has secured every
important prize for the last five years in
America, including the gold medal at the
World’s Fair, Chicago. On the - *"whole, it is a
machine calculated to butter on both sides the
bread of the farmer who uses it. TheN.S.W.
and Queensland agents are Denham Brothers,
of Sydney and Brisbane.*
Younger and Co.’s ale—the celebrated
Revolver brand—is a cool, light beverage, whose
rich brown hue, when it is held up against the
summer sun of Australia, is inspiration for a
painter. If Rubens or Raffaele had painted it the
work would have ranked as a masterpiece.
Younger’s ale is clear, absolutely pure, sparkling
and invigorating, with no sediment in it and no
adulterants of any kind. Very few liquors can
equal it for quenching the great Australian thirst
—probably, in the December days, the greatest
thirst on earth. Then there is coolness and
refreshing shade in the pale, amber hue of this
famous ale, and its gurgle sounds like a stream
plashing down a ferny dell, and the snowy head
upon it suggests a view of a distant Alp, and the
man who drinks it feels as if he could lick six of
the men he was a minute previously, or twelve of
the men he would shortly have been if the refresh-
ment hadn’t arrived.*

Mr. P. R. Larkin, the popular proprietor
of the Prince of Wales Hotel, 778-784 Qeorge-
street, Sydney, has returned per Oceana after
doing a prolonged tour of the world. His return
is not likely to improve the quality of the liquor
at his hotel, or the general management of the
establishment, for both have been as good as they
well could be, but all the same a great number of
people will be glad to see the veteran after his
long absence. Mr. Larkin is one of the oldest-
established hotel-keepers in Sydney, and during
all his long career his personal character and his
liquor have always been able to stand inspection,
and the eagle eye of the Cold Tea fanatic has
never been able to find any trace of fusel oil or
Other adulterant in either of them. Also, so long
»s the Prince of Wales is under its present
nanig-ment, tin- proprietor intends that the
f. ii L dispersed there will lie, as heretofore, the
Dost to be obtained in the city, and that they will
oe sold at the lowest price which will enable him
to keep out of the insolvency court.*
With the first touch of spring all the
drapery houses seem anxious to exhibit their new
spring and summer novelties. Show days are the
means adopted of opening the seasons. Mel-
bourne Indies always look forward to these days
! when the fashions for the coming season are re-
vealed to them in the most attractive and
picturesque manner. Messrs. Hicks, Atkinson
and Sons, of Collins-street, announce that they
are making their special spring show this week,
and invite the inspection of all ladies who desire
to see the choicest examples of Parisian and Lon-
don taste.*
ENO’S “FRUIT SALT.”—In pimples and
blotches on the face, sallowness of the skin, and
depression of spirits, it is most useful, for not the
least of its recommendations is its resemblance to
fruit in the natural way in which it relieves the
system of effete or poisonous matter, which, if
retained, poisons the blood ; and its ad vantages
over fruit are that it can be always at hand when
required. Its preparation has been truly styled
one of the triumphs of modern chemistry. In hot
or foreign climates it is invaluable. It allays
nervous excitement, and restores the nervous sys-
tem to its proper condition (by natural means).
In the nursery it is beyond praise. Sold by all
Chemists and stores.*
Warner’s Safe Cure seems to have becom 6
almost indispensable on earth, and it is even
hinted that the regularity of the heavenly bodies
is secured by periodical doses of the same well-
advertised remedy. So when H. H. Warner and
Co.’s representative, who has just finished with
Sydney and suburbs, is met touring through
N.S. W. this month, he will probably be found
well worth inspection.*
Francis Abigail, in his press articles on the
prison system of N.S.W., incidentally alludes to
a “man who had married nine wives, all now
alive, and killed the child of the last one,” and
who, “by some extraordinary proceeding, gets
liberated in seven years.”

The agitation that justice may be (Jone in
regard to tlie hideous and unparalleled Mount
Rennie trial, which agitation The Bulletin is
proud of having .journalistically initiated and
kept alive for years, grows steadily, and will
be greatly promoted by the admirable series of
articles printed by the Australian Star. These
articles, which The Bulletin hopes to see re-
printed as a pamphlet, should be carefully studied
by every member of Parliament, as the question
will be made a burning one during the session.
The Barrier people, who remember Dis-
trict-Court Judge Gibson’s sneer : “ And these are
the men to whom democracy has given a vote ! ”
are somewhat entertained at the way in which his
Honor treated certain absent jurors the other day.
“1 will not,” he gravely said, “fine them on this
occasion, but direct that they shall not receive
their jurors’ fees.” At the hands of a Judge
noted for his severity, what a punishment! They
should not be paid for duties which they had not
performed 1 The defaulting jurymen, by the way,
were in the very first flight of local wealth and in-
fluence, yet for them it was sufficient punishment
that they should not receive what they had not
earned !
Detective Loniaine, who suicided in Vic-
toria, is said to have been another “ missing heir.”
According to the “story now circulating, he ran
away at an early age from his home and his
wealthy relatives, and became a humble policeman
under an assumed name. His chief purpose in
life seemed to be to keep out of sight of a certain
enormously rich uncle who de-dred to leave him
all his money, and when the old man died, and
Lomaine found that he was urgently wanted to
come along and inherit .£120,000, he blew his
brains out. Some mm have a hard time of it in
their struggles to avoid inheriting large sums of
money.

Dear Bulletin, —The true story of th e
disinterment and republication of Gordon’s poem>
“The Feud,” is as follows: In 1864, Mr. J. B-
Mather, now S.A. Advertiser artist, was
engaged in the printing-office of the Border
Watch, at Mount Gambier, S.A. The pro-
prietors pointed out some rough proofs in a box
and strictly enjoined their employes not to touch
them, as the matter contained was meant for'
limited private circulation. Mather was a small
boy, and the prohibition gave peculiar value to
these scraps of paper. So he took one and kept it
for years, as he was attracted by the poetry
and knew the author, who used to drop into
the Watch office and yarn for hours with
editor, printer, or devil. Returning to Mount
Gambier for a holiday, in ’9l or ’92, Mather asked
Mr. A. F. Laurie, one of the owners of the paper,
whether he remembered the existence of the poem,
and, if so, whether he thought a copy could be
found. The reply was that Mr. Laurie recollected
that only 30 copies were printed, and that he
believed it impossible to find any. However, a
search was rewarded by the rescue of one time-
stained pamphlet, which now lies on the table
before me. It is printed on good thick paper,
in paper covers, and, on the front page, bears
these words; —
The Feud,
A ballad
Dedicated to Noel Paton, R.S.A.,
As a key to his illustrations of
‘•The Dowie Dens o’ Yarrow,”
By A. Lindsay.
Mount Gambier :
Laurie, Watson, and Laurie.
1864.
The explanation given for the omission of the sur-
name is that Gordon did not wish to admit the
authorship, except to his friends. Mather gave a
copy of the poem to Editor Bonython, who sent it
to Mr. A. Patchett Martin, in England, and also
published it in the S.A. Chronicle. —Yours, W.

Lady Colchester says to an interviewer :
“Revolutions pass and leave behind them great
benefits. We are passing through a critical time
and some of the exaggerations of emancipated
womanhood are sad; but one day the exaggera-
tion will pass, the senseless chatter of the new
womanhood will disappear like foam, and the
strong, pure current of the strengthened and
widened sphere of woman’s usefulness will be seen
flowing peacefully below.” After which it is sad
to find that Lady C. is on the Grand Council of
the Primrose League, an organisation intended to
check political advancement by encouraging new
womanhood in acute snobbism.

J TO
T
HER MAJESTY
AND
HIS ROYAL HIGHNESS THE
Prince »r Wales
Retribution.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He generally cruised in the black depths tmdet
the lee of the breakwater. Sometimes a well-put
stone from the pier threw up the water a yard or
so from his visible fin—somewhat shattered by an
ill-timed frolic with a working dredge. The
gloomy wharfinger, pondering certain facts and
possibilities, had dubbed him “Retribution,” and
the name had stuck. And in this disagreeable
manner did he connect his name and himself
with the family of Joe Simpson.
On Sunday afternoons it was Joe’s custom to
unbend his mind from the boiler-plate and rivet,
and woo the lighter fancies of the sea. This day
Joe had permitted his little girl to accompany
him. He regretted the impulse; for she was
restless, and disturbed the Sabbath fisherman’s
calm.
“Let be—let it be.” A fillip on the ear sends
the child whimpering to the rails. She was
always fingering the bait. A woman strolls down,
lurching occasionally as her ragged boots strike
the uneven woodwork. She is on speaking terms
■with several firemen belonging to the vessels on
the berth, and from the fumes of yesterday’s orgy
springs tlie conviction that it is Saturday after-
noon still. A twist of red hair is blown into the
corner of her large, smiling mouth. Three men
are fishing, shoulder to shoulder, rod to rod; a
half-turned cheek catches her eye.
“ Why, splice me if it ain’t Joe 1 ”
She comes forward with many greetings learned
from the lips of sailor-men, and permits her hands
to wander affectionately about his head.
“ Gerrout! ”
She recoils with lofty assumption.
“Don’t threaten me, Joe Simpson; not me,
d’ye hear?” . . . ...
Hereupon she mentions, in conjunction with
Simpson, the name of a well-known fighting
stoker; also the other fishermen appear to have
assumed a taunting aspect, and the aggressive
stoker is apparently willing to fight any number
of persons at her bidding until he dies from
exhaustion. She cracks nuts on an empty seat,
and flings the shells at an elderly gentleman who
walks the pier regularly after Sunday-school.
The black fin drifts over with the tide. A surf
springs along the line of storm-tossed boulders.
“ Keep your damned fingers from the bait ! ”
Joe is angry and raises a jack-knife to rap Little
Yellow-Hair’s knuckles. She creeps away with
tear-filled eyes. Some one lands a kicking
flounder.
From afar the bedraggled creature lifts up her
voice in querulous song; the elderly gentleman,
up for- the third time, remonstrates. She sinks
into a monotonous crooning, edged with blas-
phemy, Her besotted memory wanders back to
the filmy past before the fall—when she was a
waitress at the “Welcome” boarding-house, where
Joe promised to marry her—“ the swine ! ”
“ Who are you, eh? ” From the inside of her
bleared eyes she glances sullenly at the fretting
child. „
“ I’m Bessie Simpson, please. The yellow-
haired mite backed unconsciously from the frown-
ing, begrimed face.
“ Come here, you little hussy, d’ye hear? ’
But Bessie only backed faster. The woman
darted forward, throwing out her hands—perhaps
it was a warning gesture. There was a little cry*
a splash. The black fin disappeared.
“Gawd! there’s old Retribution sneaking
under the piles, Joe. Ain’t he a daisy? Beta
caaer he’s bigger’n the Customs launch. What’S
that? O Christ ! ”
There was an imbecile shout from a half-drunken
woman, a terrific thrashing of water into white
and red where Little Yellow-Hair had slipped in.
“ Let go, you cursed hounds ! Let me go ! ”
Two fishermen struggle fiercely with lunatic
Joe at the end of the pier.
ALBA DORION.
A Coolgardie Hush .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Some weeks ago a prospector arrived in Coolgardie
from “out,” bursting with the news that rich
alluvial had been struck near Lake Cowan. Al-
ready 10,000 oz, had been brought in, he said, and
lodged with one of the banks. The papers located
the find 75 miles from the township, and about
two miles from Lake Cowan. That was good
enough for Coolgardie. . M „
My mate and myself got the “office” very early.
Our luck was in. We hired a camel (cow) and
loaded her with a fortnight’s provisions, and dry-
blowing utensils such as picks, dishes, sieves, etc.
Loading a camel is an art which wants practice.
Our tins of concentrated indigestion rolled about
all over the saddle, and the ropes we tied would
have shocked a sailor. However, with the aid of
two men who knew nothing about it, and another
who had packed a horse when he Was a boy, we
fixed our stuff on the ship of the desert so that it
wouldn’t fall off to any great extent. Then we
St The'road was like all Westralian roads—a dreary
strip of dust and sand, running through country
covered with the eternal gum, stunted scrub and
bastard saltbush, broken now and again by huge
granite rocks, with soaks at their bases. At one
of these we halted for our first camp, 27 miles
from Coolgardie. About 9 a.m. next day we were
overtaken by the first sign of rush We had seen, in
the shape of a weary and draggled cyclist, Who
had left town with a handful of biscuits and a tin
of meat. We gave him some “ tinned dorg” and
a drink, which put so much heart into him that
he instantly began his return journey.
That day we negotiated 30 miles. About 4
p.m. next day we calculated that we had come 84
miles from (joolgardie, and decided to strike in
due east next morning and hit the lake. We soon
struck the new 13 1 in das Hoad and the vanguard of
the rush —twenty or thirty men with camels or
paokhorses. Each man had a friend who had seen
the gold brought in from the find, and every eagle
was confident of reaching the carcase to-
m Next day a party of us, eight in all, continued
our course eastward. Our leader (whom we
named Stanley, because he always went on_ in
front to show us the way, and invariably lost him-
self) was a cheery, imaginative youth who had
been everywhere, and who knew everyone you
could mention— including Rudyard Kipling, who,
he said, was “ a long red-’aired bloke, wot used to
be a barman in Coolgardie.” The anxious man of
the party bestrode a rugged Westralian horse,
whose condition spoke of a li. ) spent in rushing
on a little glass and less Water. His saddle was a
blanket secured by a rCpe surcingle: ahd another
bit of rope, thrown across and looped at each end,
made grand stirrups. The bridle was a handsome
combination of rope and fencing - wire. ihe
anxious man wore dungaree trousers far too short in
the leg, and he had lengthened them by sewing
bag on the ends with string and a pack-needle.
We discovered that he cooked well, so we always
made him sleep in the dampest places, so that he
would be up and have breakfast ready early. Ihe
rest of the party were of the nondescript type
that always haunts rushes —except one fat man,
who never spoke, but blew his nose _ most artisti*
cally, and spent all his spare time in baking and
eating sodden damper.
Next day we reached the north side of Lake
Cowan at a place called Binyarinya, and, getting
up on a high rock in the neighborhood, saw that
the eastern side was barren and sandy, and a
very unlikely place for alluvial. Stanley, of course,
had been right through it, and confirmed our ideas.
So we steered north-east, in the hope of striking
tracks, but found none. In the afternoon we
altered our course and struck the main road; and
then we met the rush in earnest. Parties of old
dry blowers, numbering from ten to thirty, sur-
rounded our group every half-mile, and demanded
the road to the indecent rush. “Ain’t you bin
there ? No kid now ! ’’ On one occasion we saw
a large party which had hired an Afghan and a
few camels to take them out; and now that the
locality of the rush was found hard to determine,
they turned on their Afghan with “You take us
rush! We paid money! You take us rush!
“Which way rush?” asked the bewildered Asiatic.
“You tell which way rush and I take you rush .
The dry blowers threatened to grease the Mahom-
medan with bacon if he didn’t 44 take them rush
instantly, and he came over to us to borrow some
cartridges. We lent them and left speedily.
Where the road crosses Lake 4 Lefroy we met
the first teams from town. The horse-power was
in every case totally inadequate to the weight of
the loads, so all hands had to yoke up abreast of a
sapling, and assist in dragging the drays through
the water. Most of the men resented being turned
into beasts of burden, and expressed a desire to
handle “the bloke wot started this (indecent)
rush.” After crossing Lake Lefroy, we heard a
rumor that there had been nu hud at Lake Cowan,
but a rich patch had been struck in the Wood-
giemoola district, 12 mi.es S.E. of the Cardiff
Castle mine. We reached camp at Wbodgiemoola
early next afternoon, and loafed about till dark.
It was raining, and everything was gloomy and
miserable. Then the fat man spoke for the first
time i he looked up from his sodden damper and
proposed athletic sports. No one took any notice,
but we were all glad that he had not spoken
before. .... ...
The anxious man began feeding his horse with
our oatmeal and rice, and, when we objected, went
away to find some grass. He returned with the
news that there was a duck on the lake, opposite
our camp, and proposed that' someone should
shoot it for tea. Stanley had a repeating Win-
chester and 14 cartridges. He could also shoot—
“ not too stinkin’.” He fired 12 shots without hit-
ting anything, even the lake ; with the thirteenth
he blew one ear off the anxious man’s horse;
and with the last bullet he shot the duck dead.
The anxious man moved about and framed blas-
phemies, but the horse didn’t seem to mind. One
bf the party owned a dog, a champion retriever
which he was never tired of praising. It had
taken goodness knows how many prizes ; and the
owner’s yarns about its wonderful sagacity had
brightened many a weary mile. The dog was
sent to retrieve the bird, while his owner stood
proudly on the bank and said many times “ Good
dog! Go fetch.” The dog understood. He
reached the bird and swam with it to the other
side of the lake, where he proceeded to eat it at
All the "next three days we Wandered about
seeking “rush.” Our provisions Were nearly
done, and we realised that it was time to go
back. When we reached town we found that a
crowd of hoaxed miners had already returned, and
wanted to tear down the newspaper offices. The
promoter of the rush was seized, kept under lock
and key for one night, and then taken out in a
cart next day, guarded by four desperate dry-
blowers determined to make him show where the
lOjOOOoz. were taken from. However, he escaped
from his guards one night, and has not been seen
since. How that man must have laughed !
Ooolgardie, July 5.
PORTHOS.
Statements are made, and denied, that
the Salvation Army in Melb. has led to respect-
able women marrying criminals. In one case,
the result was a divorce, but in another there has
been 10 years’ allegedly-happy union.
The Melbourne weekly which lately pub-
lished the portrait of a bar-lady as a type of
beauty would greatly oblige_ The Bulletin by
taking credit for the aforesaid work of art in its
next half-dozen issues. The average person who
happens to note a newspaper illustration in a
shop window, or elsewhere, is sooner or later
bound to believe that he, or she, “ saw it in The
Bulletin.” Not wishing to rob a contem-
porary of any distinction it is justly entitled to,
The Bulletin now mentions that the type of
beauty aforesaid was not, as alleged by popular
error, published in these columns. There are two
or three reasons why the announcement seems
called for, and one of them is that the young
lady’s portrait turns out to be not much like the
original.
Somebody writing in Sydney D.T. remarks
that N.S.W. National Gallery ‘‘looks all the
better for the weeding-out to which the pictures
have been subjected in consequence of the loans
to country districts,” and proceeds to say that
“ Early Morning on the Doggerbank ” should cer-
tainly not have been marked for removal. ‘‘lf
large canvasses are necessary,” says the writer,
“ as well as small ones, to complete the loans to
Bathurst, Goulhurn and Newcastle, then there are
a pair of bush scenes—huge, upright pictures, high
up on the southern wall of the Australian court—
and there is Mr. Johnstone’s painting, “Off the
Track,” that suggest themselves as being in need
of country air. And, though this may seem a
poor compliment to the people of the towns
referred to, it is manifestly the correct thing
to do, seeing that Sydney is a centre,” &c.
It is indeed a ghastly thing that there should be
found in Australia any man capable of expressing
such sentiments in regard to “Off the Track” —
which was, until lately, skied over De Neuville’s
“ Rorke’s Drift.” “Rorke’s Drift” is a great
work, but “ Off the Track ” is also a great picture
—one which contains more of the soul of the grim
Australian bush than all the other Australian
pictures in the Gallery bundled together. John-
stone is a genius who has left Australia for ever,
and “ Off the Track ”is his worthy memorial. It
is a canvas to which every true Australian takes
off his hat.

The Old Camp Oven.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We don’t keep a gran’ pianner in our hut
beside the creek,
An’ I’m pretty certain Hanner couldn’t
bang it, anyhow,
But we’ve got one box of music, an’ I’d
rather hear its squeak
Than the daisiest cantata that’s bin
fashioned up to now.
It’s an ole camp-oven merely, with a handle
made of wire,
But no organ built could nearly compen-
sate to me fer it
When I come off graft an’ find it playin’
tunes afore the fire,
An’ I’m feelin’ sorter vacant, but jes’ won-
derfully fit.
In its sizzle, sizzle, sizzle,
There’s a thousan’ little airs,
An’ no man can sit an’ grizzle
’Bout his troubles an’ his cares
Whiles the flames are gaily windin’.
An’ the tea is down to brew,
An’ thq ole camp-oven s grindin’
All the reels he ever knew.
When the %et winds meet an’ whip me in
the early winter nights.
An’ the hissin’ hailstones clip me all the
way across the flat,
As I battle for’ards, water-logged, to’ard the
beck’nin’ lights,
There is alwus there a welcome to console
a chap for that.
For my little wife is glowin’ brisk an’ bright
beside the lamp,
An’ the ole camp-oven’s goin’. Gosh ! I
feel jes’ like a kid
As I peel an’ sluice so slippy, an’ I hear the
storm winds vamp
To the singin’ of the oven when the missus
lifts the lid.
There’s a sizzle an’ a splutter
An’ a whirr of many harps,
Where’s the instrument can utter
Such a maze of flats an’ sharps ?
Not fer me the great creations
When the ole camp-oven plays
“ Home sweet Home ” with variations
At the end of workin’ days.
In' tlie evenin’s dim an’ hazy, stretched out-
side along a butc,
Feelin’ reasonably lazy, blowin’ clouds
that curl an’ climb,
l can hear the old camp-oven on the logs
before the hut,
Rippin’ out a mellow chorus that jes’ suits
the place an’ time.
If we strike it in the ranges, or The Win'-
mill turns out well,
I suppose there’ll be some changes, an’ I’ll
wanter make things gee,
But the time will never happen when I’ll be
so steep a swell
That the ole camp-oven’s measure won’t
be melody to me.
’Neath its bubble, bubble, bubble,
There’s the lilt of jigs an’ reels ;
All the common kind of trouble
That the “ ’orney-’anded ” feels
Is wiped out in half a minute
By the restfulness it brings,
An’ the peaceful rapture in it
When the old camp-oven sings.
Melbourne.
EDEARD DYSON.
Followed Under! A Queer Quest.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ In the interests of science,” said Fetherstone
—and fired.
One of the creatures fell shrieking from the tree;
the other two sprang nimbly down, skipped to
and fro for a moment, then squatted chattering
beside the fallen.
The shooter made a wry face. “Painfully
human sort of thing,” he said. “Never did like
firing at apes—but this looks uncommonly like a
new species.”
He put a cartridge in the empty barrel, crept
through a patch of lallang, looked again, and
thrilled with self-congratulation.
“ Entirely new species, by all that’s lucky:
wonderfully smooth coat! Nearly the color of a
Dyak, too ! Pity ! —but I must have another !”
A dozen yards further, upon hands and knees ;
then the rifle rose slowly—steadied—went down
again with a jerk.
“Why, bless my sou' ! they are Dyaks—they
can stand erect? It’s a man I’ve potted—or a
boy, rather! Hang the imps ! —such climbing
would have taken anybody in ! ”
Fetherstone mustered up his little stock of
Dyak and walked out of the sheltering lallang.
The wounded boy was being helped on to his feet
by the others; the white man got unseen within
20 yards, and then a fresh surprise stopped him.
Yard-high and stark naked—these three creatures
were not boys, after all! They were men! —and
one of them grizzled. Fetherstone closed his eyes
—then looked again ; but there was no doubt
about it—these pigmies were adults ! Before
him stood three of the “monkey-men” of the
Malays—the orang karra whose existence he had
so often laughed to scorn.
“Human beings, sure enough ! —and well-built
little—hullo ! they’re off ! ” —and Fetherstone
started, top-speed, after the dwarfs as they fled
down towards the lake—the wounded one holding
his own, and all three traversing the ground so
fast that the white man only got to the bank in
time to see them vanish heads-first into the dark-
green water.
“Dive like dagger-birds, of course !” he said, as
he sat down on the rocky verge. “Well, I’ve
seen ’em for myself, at all events, though it’ll be
hard to get any white man to believe me ! ”
Then he lit his pipe, and waited, and watched,
and wondered, for nearly five minutes before the
astonishing truth burst upon him. They weren’t
coming up at all. He jumped to his feet as he
remembered the Dyak assertion that these people
lived under water !
“ Which, of course, is rubbish. And yet, if
they’d showed even their noses, I must have seen
’em; the lake’s like glass.”
He tucked his Martini under his arm, and
patrolled the bank until he had determined that,
of two things, one must be true. Either the
pigmies were still in the water —drowned, or they
had left it through some passage with a sub-
aqueous entrance. The rock was volcanic basalt,
rising gradually backwards to the thunder-smitten
hill that stood out black and naked against the
pale-green slopes of jungle behind. Fetherstone
looked back to the ridge from which the dwarfs
had started, followed with his eye the track of
their flight, and marked that the spot they had
plunged from was not the nearest to their starting-
place. “ If there’s an under-water hole, it’s there-
abouts,” he decided. “They’d make a line for
it, of course.”
He got his clothes off, strapped his sheath-knife
round his loins, dived, and bottomed at about
20ft. Along the rock he groped as he rose—it
was solid to the surface. He scrambled out, moved
eight or nine feet along the edge, took breath, and
plunged again ; and this time, as the water forced
him upwards, he came upon a greater darkness in
the shimmering shadows—an opening into which
he swam. But still the water lifted him, until his
back was lightly pressed against a roof of rock.
This, with half-a-dozen inward strokes, he cleared,
and found his head, next moment, out of water,
but in profoundest darkness. Further in he swam,
until his feet touched sloping bottom and he
scrambled out upon smooth rock.
So far, his keen exploring interest had left no
room for fear; but now, as he strove to pierce a
darkness inconceivable, he shuddered. Could he
find the way out again ? Yes !—in that direction
was a faint something—a trifle less intensity of
gloom, and with a nervous dash he went down,
swiftly along the tunnel, and up again into the
reassuring sunlight. But, as he dressed, ho took
shame to himself. He, a pioneer of science ? The
first white man to behold the legendary pigmies of
Borneo ! —was he going to turn tail upon further
discovery when the way was straight before him?
He put down the coat he was about to don, took
from it a little vulcanite case, and from that again
a small thermometer, the place of which he sup-
plied with vestas. Off then went his clothes
again, and, with the little case of matches fas-
tened to a bootlace round his neck, he made the
sub-aqueous trip once more.
It was a cleft-like cave, with upward-sloping
floor, and walls that narrowed inwards till they
seemed to meet. But, with a second match, the
shivering adventurer found the angle not com-
plete. Where should have been the apex was a
lofty slit, just wide enough to let him through
into the warmer air of a natural passage that led
gently upwards till, when his seventh match went
out, there came, reflected on the rock above him,
an intermittent reddish glow that strengthened as
he went. Then before him he saw a transverse
ridge thrown into outline by the leaping light be-
hind it. Fetherstone looked over and down, for
far below him was the floor of the mighty cave he
dimly saw.
There they were !—“ monkey-men,” “ water-
dwarfs,” or what not!—squatting, jabbering and
eating round the fire that snatched here and
there out of the gloom a faint indication of the
lofty roof. In sable silhouette upon the hither
side of the blaze, and touched into ruddy bronze
upon the further, these half-hundred pigmies filled
the watcher with a horror he had not felt for more
repulsive savages—an odd perversion of the solid
sense that served him well enough in upper air.
The gnomes of German story—the “ wee folk ” of
his native land—a hundred legends jostled in his
brain, till at the need for instant action the cob-
webs cleared away.
Beyond the fire the cave-floor rose again to
where, almost level with the watcher’s place, there
showed upon the gloom a little patch of greyish
light. 111-defined and faint—it could have but
one source. “Daylight,” said Petherstone to
himself. “There’s an opening over there from
the hill. Water’s a short cut, I suppose.”
To catch a better view he stood upon the ridge
of rock—sent a loose stone scattering down —and
was seen.
The little women squealed and snatched up
their doll-like children—then, with a shrill yell,
the little men started up the slope towards the
glimmering white figure of the enemy. For half-
a-minute Fetherstone scorned to bolt. He even
laughed ; but then came “flick, flick” upon the
rocks around him, and he knew that it was no
laughing matter. These were sumpitan-darts, and
back he rushed into the rocky corridor, bruising
himself cruelly as he stumbled through the pitchy
darkness, and falling plump into the unseen water
before he thought he had got near it.
Two Dyaks, wondering over a rifle and a heap
of clothes, saw, rising from the deep, a battered
human countenance, and put a hundred yards
between them and the bank before they ventured
to look back. The Antu—if Antu it was---was
putting on the Whiteman’s leg-sheaths, and calling
to them, in good Malay, not to be a pair of idiots,
“ And the voice,” said one of them, as he told
the tale that night to his gaping fellows, “ was
the voice of Fersun Tuan. Then went we back,
trembling, and behold, it was the Tuan himself!
And when we asked of him the meaning, he said
a strange thing. ‘ I have been,’ he said, ‘ among
the water-dwarfs! ’ ”
An awe-struck silence followed, and when, at
dawn next morning, Fetherstone was stepping
from his door, he stumbled over a basket of
mangoes, with two pencil-fishes crossed upon the
fruit. It was the offering to a god !
“ Your career’s finished, Phil. Fetherstone ! ” he
said to himself, as he kicked the whole concern off
the platform. “ You can’t get higher than
apotheosis ! ”
ALEX. MONTGOMERY.
A triumph of diplomacy ! At the N. W.
corner of N.S. Wales and the S.W. 'corner of
Queensland there is a skilfully-contrived gap left
in the rabbit-proof fence, by which those pro-
lific pests are neatly shunted off into S. Aus-
tralian territory. As the bunnies have destroyed
all the horse-feed on S. Australian side, the
squatters are compelled to employ Queensland
carriers instead of S. Australian, and send their
wool to Longreach.
